Lottery Ticket No. 514\r\n CHAPTER II. The Blue Diamond\r\n CHAPTER III. Herlock Sholmes Opens Hostilities\r\n CHAPTER IV. Light in the Darkness\r\n CHAPTER V. An Abduction\r\n CHAPTER VI. Second Arrest of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\n CHAPTER VII. The Jewish Lamp\r\n CHAPTER VIII. The Shipwreck\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I.\r\nLOTTERY TICKET NO. 514.\r\n\r\n\r\nOn the eighth day of last December, Mon. Gerbois, professor of\r\nmathematics at the College of Versailles, while rummaging in an old\r\ncuriosity-shop, unearthed a small mahogany writing-desk which pleased\r\nhim very much on account of the multiplicity of its drawers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust the thing for Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s birthday present,\xe2\x80\x9d thought he. And as he\r\nalways tried to furnish some simple pleasures for his daughter,\r\nconsistent with his modest income, he enquired the price, and, after\r\nsome keen bargaining, purchased it for sixty-five francs. As he was\r\ngiving his address to the shopkeeper, a young man, dressed with\r\nelegance and taste, who had been exploring the stock of antiques,\r\ncaught sight of the writing-desk, and immediately enquired its price.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is sold,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the shopkeeper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! to this gentleman, I presume?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonsieur Gerbois bowed, and left the store, quite proud to be the\r\npossessor of an article which had attracted the attention of a\r\ngentleman of quality. But he had not taken a dozen steps in the street,\r\nwhen he was overtaken by the young man who, hat in hand and in a tone\r\nof perfect courtesy, thus addressed him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, monsieur; I am going to ask you a question that you\r\nmay deem impertinent. It is this: Did you have any special object in\r\nview when you bought that writing-desk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I came across it by chance and it struck my fancy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you do not care for it particularly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I shall keep it\xe2\x80\x94that is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause it is an antique, perhaps?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; because it is convenient,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Mon. Gerbois.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case, you would consent to exchange it for another desk that\r\nwould be quite as convenient and in better condition?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! this one is in good condition, and I see no object in making an\r\nexchange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Gerbois is a man of irritable disposition and hasty temper. So he\r\nreplied, testily:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg of you, monsieur, do not insist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the young man firmly held his ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how much you paid for it, monsieur, but I offer you\r\ndouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree times the amount.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! that will do,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the professor, impatiently; \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wish\r\nto sell it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man stared at him for a moment in a manner that Mon. Gerbois\r\nwould not readily forget, then turned and walked rapidly away.\r\n\r\nAn hour later, the desk was delivered at the professor\xe2\x80\x99s house on the\r\nViroflay road. He called his daughter, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is something for you, Suzanne, provided you like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuzanne was a pretty girl, with a gay and affectionate nature. She\r\nthrew her arms around her father\xe2\x80\x99s neck and kissed him rapturously. To\r\nher, the desk had all the semblance of a royal gift. That evening,\r\nassisted by Hortense, the servant, she placed the desk in her room;\r\nthen she dusted it, cleaned the drawers and pigeon-holes, and carefully\r\narranged within it her papers, writing material, correspondence, a\r\ncollection of post-cards, and some souvenirs of her cousin Philippe\r\nthat she kept in secret.\r\n\r\nNext morning, at half past seven, Mon. Gerbois went to the college. At\r\nten o\xe2\x80\x99clock, in pursuance of her usual custom, Suzanne went to meet\r\nhim, and it was a great pleasure for him to see her slender figure and\r\nchildish smile waiting for him at the college gate. They returned home\r\ntogether.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd your writing desk\xe2\x80\x94how is it this morning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarvellous! Hortense and I have polished the brass mountings until\r\nthey look like gold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you are pleased with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPleased with it! Why, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how I managed to get on without it\r\nfor such a long time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs they were walking up the pathway to the house, Mon. Gerbois said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we go and take a look at it before breakfast?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! yes, that\xe2\x80\x99s a splendid idea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe ascended the stairs ahead of her father, but, on arriving at the\r\ndoor of her room, she uttered a cry of surprise and dismay.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Mon. Gerbois.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe writing-desk is gone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen the police were called in, they were astonished at the admirable\r\nsimplicity of the means employed by the thief. During Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nabsence, the servant had gone to market, and while the house was thus\r\nleft unguarded, a drayman, wearing a badge\xe2\x80\x94some of the neighbors saw\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94stopped his cart in front of the house and rang twice. Not knowing\r\nthat Hortense was absent, the neighbors were not suspicious;\r\nconsequently, the man carried on his work in peace and tranquility.\r\n\r\nApart from the desk, not a thing in the house had been disturbed. Even\r\nSuzanne\xe2\x80\x99s purse, which she had left upon the writing-desk, was found\r\nupon an adjacent table with its contents untouched. It was obvious that\r\nthe thief had come with a set purpose, which rendered the crime even\r\nmore mysterious; because, why did he assume so great a risk for such a\r\ntrifling object?\r\n\r\nThe only clue the professor could furnish was the strange incident of\r\nthe preceding evening. He declared:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe young man was greatly provoked at my refusal, and I had an idea\r\nthat he threatened me as he went away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the clue was a vague one. The shopkeeper could not throw any light\r\non the affair. He did not know either of the gentlemen. As to the desk\r\nitself, he had purchased it for forty francs at an executor\xe2\x80\x99s sale at\r\nChevreuse, and believed he had resold it at its fair value. The police\r\ninvestigation disclosed nothing more.\r\n\r\nBut Mon. Gerbois entertained the idea that he had suffered an enormous\r\nloss. There must have been a fortune concealed in a secret drawer, and\r\nthat was the reason the young man had resorted to crime.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor father, what would we have done with that fortune?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nSuzanne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy child! with such a fortune, you could make a most advantageous\r\nmarriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuzanne sighed bitterly. Her aspirations soared no higher than her\r\ncousin Philippe, who was indeed a most deplorable object. And life, in\r\nthe little house at Versailles, was not so happy and contented as of\r\nyore.\r\n\r\nTwo months passed away. Then came a succession of startling events, a\r\nstrange blending of good luck and dire misfortune!\r\n\r\nOn the first day of February, at half-past five, Mon. Gerbois entered\r\nthe house, carrying an evening paper, took a seat, put on his\r\nspectacles, and commenced to read. As politics did not interest him, he\r\nturned to the inside of the paper. Immediately his attention was\r\nattracted by an article entitled:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThird Drawing of the Press Association Lottery.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. 514, series 23, draws a million.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe newspaper slipped from his fingers. The walls swam before his eyes,\r\nand his heart ceased to beat. He held No. 514, series 23. He had\r\npurchased it from a friend, to oblige him, without any thought of\r\nsuccess, and behold, it was the lucky number!\r\n\r\nQuickly, he took out his memorandum-book. Yes, he was quite right. The\r\nNo. 514, series 23, was written there, on the inside of the cover. But\r\nthe ticket?\r\n\r\nHe rushed to his desk to find the envelope-box in which he had placed\r\nthe precious ticket; but the box was not there, and it suddenly\r\noccurred to him that it had not been there for several weeks. He heard\r\nfootsteps on the gravel walk leading from the street.\r\n\r\nHe called:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuzanne! Suzanne!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was returning from a walk. She entered hastily. He stammered, in a\r\nchoking voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuzanne ... the box ... the box of envelopes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat box?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one I bought at the Louvre ... one Saturday ... it was at the end\r\nof that table.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you remember, father, we put all those things away together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe evening ... you know ... the same evening....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut where?... Tell me, quick!... Where?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere? Why, in the writing-desk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the writing-desk that was stolen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, mon Dieu!... In the stolen desk!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe uttered the last sentence in a low voice, in a sort of stupor. Then\r\nhe seized her hand, and in a still lower voice, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt contained a million, my child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! father, why didn\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, naively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA million!\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cIt contained the ticket that drew the grand\r\nprize in the Press Lottery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe colossal proportions of the disaster overwhelmed them, and for a\r\nlong time they maintained a silence that they feared to break. At last,\r\nSuzanne said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, father, they will pay you just the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow? On what proof?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMust you have proof?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you haven\xe2\x80\x99t any?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was in the box.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the box that has disappeared.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and now the thief will get the money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! that would be terrible, father. You must prevent it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment he was silent; then, in an outburst of energy, he leaped\r\nup, stamped on the floor, and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, he shall not have that million; he shall not have it! Why\r\nshould he have it? Ah! clever as he is, he can do nothing. If he goes\r\nto claim the money, they will arrest him. Ah! now, we will see, my fine\r\nfellow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will you do, father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDefend our just rights, whatever happens! And we will succeed. The\r\nmillion francs belong to me, and I intend to have them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA few minutes later, he sent this telegram:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGovernor Cr\xc3\xa9dit Foncier\r\n\xe2\x80\x9crue Capucines, Paris.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cAm holder of No. 514, series 23. Oppose by all legal means any\r\n    other claimant.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGERBOIS.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nAlmost at the same moment, the Cr\xc3\xa9dit Foncier received the following\r\ntelegram:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. 514, series 23, is in my possession.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEvery time I undertake to relate one of the many extraordinary\r\nadventures that mark the life of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, I experience a feeling\r\nof embarrassment, as it seems to me that the most commonplace of those\r\nadventures is already well known to my readers. In fact, there is not a\r\nmovement of our \xe2\x80\x9cnational thief,\xe2\x80\x9d as he has been so aptly described,\r\nthat has not been given the widest publicity, not an exploit that has\r\nnot been studied in all its phases, not an action that has not been\r\ndiscussed with that particularity usually reserved for the recital of\r\nheroic deeds.\r\n\r\nFor instance, who does not know the strange history of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Blonde\r\nLady,\xe2\x80\x9d with those curious episodes which were proclaimed by the\r\nnewspapers with heavy black headlines, as follows: \xe2\x80\x9cLottery Ticket No.\r\n514!\xe2\x80\x9d ... \xe2\x80\x9cThe Crime on the Avenue Henri-Martin!\xe2\x80\x9d ... \xe2\x80\x9cThe Blue\r\nDiamond!\xe2\x80\x9d ... The interest created by the intervention of the\r\ncelebrated English detective, Herlock Sholmes! The excitement aroused\r\nby the various vicissitudes which marked the struggle between those\r\nfamous artists! And what a commotion on the boulevards, the day on\r\nwhich the newsboys announced: \xe2\x80\x9cArrest of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy excuse for repeating these stories at this time is the fact that I\r\nproduce the key to the enigma. Those adventures have always been\r\nenveloped in a certain degree of obscurity, which I now remove. I\r\nreproduce old newspaper articles, I relate old-time interviews, I\r\npresent ancient letters; but I have arranged and classified all that\r\nmaterial and reduced it to the exact truth. My collaborators in this\r\nwork have been Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin himself, and also the ineffable Wilson, the\r\nfriend and confidant of Herlock Sholmes.\r\n\r\nEvery one will recall the tremendous burst of laughter which greeted\r\nthe publication of those two telegrams. The name \xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\xe2\x80\x9d was in\r\nitself a stimulus to curiosity, a promise of amusement for the gallery.\r\nAnd, in this case, the gallery means the entire world.\r\n\r\nAn investigation was immediately commenced by the Cr\xc3\xa9dit Foncier, which\r\nestablished these facts: That ticket No. 514, series 23, had been sold\r\nby the Versailles branch office of the Lottery to an artillery officer\r\nnamed Bessy, who was afterward killed by a fall from his horse. Some\r\ntime before his death, he informed some of his comrades that he had\r\ntransferred his ticket to a friend.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am that friend,\xe2\x80\x9d affirmed Mon. Gerbois.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProve it,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the governor of the Cr\xc3\xa9dit Foncier.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I can prove it. Twenty people can tell you that I was an\r\nintimate friend of Monsieur Bessy, and that we frequently met at the\r\nCaf\xc3\xa9 de la Place-d\xe2\x80\x99Armes. It was there, one day, I purchased the ticket\r\nfrom him for twenty francs\xe2\x80\x94simply as an accommodation to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you any witnesses to that transaction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, how do you expect to prove it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy a letter he wrote to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA letter that was pinned to the ticket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProduce it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was stolen at the same time as the ticket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you must find it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was soon learned that Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had the letter. A short paragraph\r\nappeared in the _Echo de France_\xe2\x80\x94which has the honor to be his official\r\norgan, and of which, it is said, he is one of the principal\r\nshareholders\xe2\x80\x94the paragraph announced that Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had placed in\r\nthe hands of Monsieur Detinan, his advocate and legal adviser, the\r\nletter that Monsieur Bessy had written to him\xe2\x80\x94to him personally.\r\n\r\nThis announcement provoked an outburst of laughter. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had\r\nengaged a lawyer! Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, conforming to the rules and customs of\r\nmodern society, had appointed a legal representative in the person of a\r\nwell-known member of the Parisian bar!\r\n\r\nMon. Detinan had never enjoyed the pleasure of meeting Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nfact he deeply regretted\xe2\x80\x94but he had actually been retained by that\r\nmysterious gentleman and felt greatly honored by the choice. He was\r\nprepared to defend the interests of his client to the best of his\r\nability. He was pleased, even proud, to exhibit the letter of Mon.\r\nBessy, but, although it proved the transfer of the ticket, it did not\r\nmention the name of the purchaser. It was simply addressed to \xe2\x80\x9cMy Dear\r\nFriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy Dear Friend! that is I,\xe2\x80\x9d added Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, in a note attached to\r\nMon. Bessy\xe2\x80\x99s letter. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the best proof of that fact is that I hold\r\nthe letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe swarm of reporters immediately rushed to see Mon. Gerbois, who\r\ncould only repeat:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy Dear Friend! that is I.... Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin stole the letter with the\r\nlottery ticket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him prove it!\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Lupin to the reporters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must have done it, because he stole the writing-desk!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed\r\nMon. Gerbois before the same reporters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him prove it!\xe2\x80\x9d replied Lupin.\r\n\r\nSuch was the entertaining comedy enacted by the two claimants of ticket\r\nNo. 514; and the calm demeanor of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin contrasted strangely\r\nwith the nervous perturbation of poor Mon. Gerbois. The newspapers were\r\nfilled with the lamentations of that unhappy man. He announced his\r\nmisfortune with pathetic candor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnderstand, gentlemen, it was Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s dowry that the rascal stole!\r\nPersonally, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care a straw for it,... but for Suzanne! Just think\r\nof it, a whole million! Ten times one hundred thousand francs! Ah! I\r\nknew very well that the desk contained a treasure!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was in vain to tell him that his adversary, when stealing the desk,\r\nwas unaware that the lottery ticket was in it, and that, in any event,\r\nhe could not foresee that the ticket would draw the grand prize. He\r\nwould reply;\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! of course, he knew it ... else why would he take the trouble\r\nto steal a poor, miserable desk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor some unknown reason; but certainly not for a small scrap of paper\r\nwhich was then worth only twenty francs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA million francs! He knew it;... he knows everything! Ah! you do not\r\nknow him\xe2\x80\x94the scoundrel!... He hasn\xe2\x80\x99t robbed you of a million francs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe controversy would have lasted for a much longer time, but, on the\r\ntwelfth day, Mon. Gerbois received from Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin a letter, marked\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cconfidential,\xe2\x80\x9d which read as follows:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur, the gallery is being amused at our expense. Do you not\r\n    think it is time for us to be serious? The situation is this: I\r\n    possess a ticket to which I have no legal right, and you have the\r\n    legal right to a ticket you do not possess. Neither of us can do\r\n    anything. You will not relinquish your rights to me; I will not\r\n    deliver the ticket to you. Now, what is to be done?\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cI see only one way out of the difficulty: Let us divide the\r\n    spoils. A half-million for you; a half-million for me. Is not that\r\n    a fair division? In my opinion, it is an equitable solution, and an\r\n    immediate one. I will give you three days\xe2\x80\x99 time to consider the\r\n    proposition. On Thursday morning I shall expect to read in the\r\n    personal column of the _Echo de France_ a discreet message\r\n    addressed to _M. Ars. Lup_, expressing in veiled terms your consent\r\n    to my offer. By so doing you will recover immediate possession of\r\n    the ticket; then you can collect the money and send me half a\r\n    million in a manner that I will describe to you later.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cIn case of your refusal, I shall resort to other measures to\r\n    accomplish the same result. But, apart from the very serious\r\n    annoyances that such obstinacy on your part will cause you, it will\r\n    cost you twenty-five thousand francs for supplementary expenses.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cBelieve me, monsieur, I remain your devoted servant,\r\n\r\n\r\nARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nIn a fit of exasperation Mon. Gerbois committed the grave mistake of\r\nshowing that letter and allowing a copy of it to be taken. His\r\nindignation overcame his discretion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing! He shall have nothing!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, before a crowd of\r\nreporters. \xe2\x80\x9cTo divide my property with him? Never! Let him tear up the\r\nticket if he wishes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet five hundred thousand francs is better than nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not the question. It is a question of my just right, and that\r\nright I will establish before the courts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! attack Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin? That would be amusing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but the Cr\xc3\xa9dit Foncier. They must pay me the million francs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout producing the ticket, or, at least, without proving that you\r\nbought it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat proof exists, since Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin admits that he stole the\r\nwriting-desk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut would the word of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin carry any weight with the court?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter; I will fight it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe gallery shouted with glee; and wagers were freely made upon the\r\nresult with the odds in favor of Lupin. On the following Thursday the\r\npersonal column in the _Echo de France_ was eagerly perused by the\r\nexpectant public, but it contained nothing addressed to _M. Ars. Lup_.\r\nMon. Gerbois had not replied to Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s letter. That was the\r\ndeclaration of war.\r\n\r\nThat evening the newspapers announced the abduction of Mlle. Suzanne\r\nGerbois.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe most entertaining feature in what might be called the Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\ndramas is the comic attitude displayed by the Parisian police. Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin talks, plans, writes, commands, threatens and executes as if the\r\npolice did not exist. They never figure in his calculations.\r\n\r\nAnd yet the police do their utmost. But what can they do against such a\r\nfoe\xe2\x80\x94a foe that scorns and ignores them?\r\n\r\nSuzanne had left the house at twenty minutes to ten; such was the\r\ntestimony of the servant. On leaving the college, at five minutes past\r\nten, her father did not find her at the place she was accustomed to\r\nwait for him. Consequently, whatever had happened must have occurred\r\nduring the course of Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s walk from the house to the college. Two\r\nneighbors had met her about three hundred yards from the house. A lady\r\nhad seen, on the avenue, a young girl corresponding to Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndescription. No one else had seen her.\r\n\r\nInquiries were made in all directions; the employees of the railways\r\nand street-car lines were questioned, but none of them had seen\r\nanything of the missing girl. However, at Ville-d\xe2\x80\x99Avray, they found a\r\nshopkeeper who had furnished gasoline to an automobile that had come\r\nfrom Paris on the day of the abduction. It was occupied by a blonde\r\nwoman\xe2\x80\x94extremely blonde, said the witness. An hour later, the automobile\r\nagain passed through Ville-d\xe2\x80\x99Avray on its way from Versailles to Paris.\r\nThe shopkeeper declared that the automobile now contained a second\r\nwoman who was heavily veiled. No doubt, it was Suzanne Gerbois.\r\n\r\nThe abduction must have taken place in broad daylight, on a frequented\r\nstreet, in the very heart of the town. How? And at what spot? Not a cry\r\nwas heard; not a suspicious action had been seen. The shopkeeper\r\ndescribed the automobile as a royal-blue limousine of twenty-four\r\nhorse-power made by the firm of Peugeon & Co. Inquiries were then made\r\nat the Grand-Garage, managed by Madame Bob-Walthour, who made a\r\nspecialty of abductions by automobile. It was learned that she had\r\nrented a Peugeon limousine on that day to a blonde woman whom she had\r\nnever seen before nor since.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was the chauffeur?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA young man named Ernest, whom I had engaged only the day before. He\r\ncame well recommended.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he here now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. He brought back the machine, but I haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen him since,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMadame Bob-Walthour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know where we can find him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might see the people who recommended him to me. Here are the\r\nnames.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUpon inquiry, it was learned that none of these people knew the man\r\ncalled Ernest. The recommendations were forged.\r\n\r\nSuch was the fate of every clue followed by the police. It ended\r\nnowhere. The mystery remained unsolved.\r\n\r\nMon. Gerbois had not the strength or courage to wage such an unequal\r\nbattle. The disappearance of his daughter crushed him; he capitulated\r\nto the enemy. A short announcement in the _Echo de France_ proclaimed\r\nhis unconditional surrender.\r\n\r\nTwo days later, Mon. Gerbois visited the office of the Cr\xc3\xa9dit Foncier\r\nand handed lottery ticket number 514, series 23, to the governor, who\r\nexclaimed, with surprise:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you have it! He has returned it to you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was mislaid. That was all,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mon. Gerbois.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you pretended that it had been stolen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt first, I thought it had ... but here it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will require some evidence to establish your right to the ticket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill the letter of the purchaser, Monsieur Bessy, be sufficient!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that will do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mon. Gerbois, producing the letter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. Leave these papers with us. The rules of the lottery allow\r\nus fifteen days\xe2\x80\x99 time to investigate your claim. I will let you know\r\nwhen to call for your money. I presume you desire, as much as I do,\r\nthat this affair should be closed without further publicity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Gerbois and the governor henceforth maintained a discreet silence.\r\nBut the secret was revealed in some way, for it was soon commonly known\r\nthat Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had returned the lottery ticket to Mon. Gerbois. The\r\npublic received the news with astonishment and admiration. Certainly,\r\nhe was a bold gamester who thus threw upon the table a trump card of\r\nsuch importance as the precious ticket. But, it was true, he still\r\nretained a trump card of equal importance. However, if the young girl\r\nshould escape? If the hostage held by Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin should be rescued?\r\n\r\nThe police thought they had discovered the weak spot of the enemy, and\r\nnow redoubled their efforts. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin disarmed by his own act,\r\ncrushed by the wheels of his own machination, deprived of every sou of\r\nthe coveted million ... public interest now centered in the camp of his\r\nadversary.\r\n\r\nBut it was necessary to find Suzanne. And they did not find her, nor\r\ndid she escape. Consequently, it must be admitted, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had won\r\nthe first hand. But the game was not yet decided. The most difficult\r\npoint remained. Mlle. Gerbois is in his possession, and he will hold\r\nher until he receives five hundred thousand francs. But how and where\r\nwill such an exchange be made? For that purpose, a meeting must be\r\narranged, and then what will prevent Mon. Gerbois from warning the\r\npolice and, in that way, effecting the rescue of his daughter and, at\r\nthe same time, keeping his money? The professor was interviewed, but he\r\nwas extremely reticent. His answer was:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Mlle. Gerbois?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe search is being continued.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin has written to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you swear to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it is true. What are his instructions?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the interviewers attacked Mon. Detinan, and found him equally\r\ndiscreet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Lupin is my client, and I cannot discuss his affairs,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nreplied, with an affected air of gravity.\r\n\r\nThese mysteries served to irritate the gallery. Obviously, some secret\r\nnegotiations were in progress. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had arranged and tightened\r\nthe meshes of his net, while the police maintained a close watch, day\r\nand night, over Mon. Gerbois. And the three and only possible\r\nd\xc3\xa9nouements\xe2\x80\x94the arrest, the triumph, or the ridiculous and pitiful\r\nabortion\xe2\x80\x94were freely discussed; but the curiosity of the public was\r\nonly partially satisfied, and it was reserved for these pages to reveal\r\nthe exact truth of the affair.\r\n\r\n\r\nOn Monday, March 12th, Mon. Gerbois received a notice from the Cr\xc3\xa9dit\r\nFoncier. On Wednesday, he took the one o\xe2\x80\x99clock train for Paris. At two\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, a thousand bank-notes of one thousand francs each were\r\ndelivered to him. Whilst he was counting them, one by one, in a state\r\nof nervous agitation\xe2\x80\x94that money, which represented Suzanne\xe2\x80\x99s ransom\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ncarriage containing two men stopped at the curb a short distance from\r\nthe bank. One of the men had grey hair and an unusually shrewd\r\nexpression which formed a striking contrast to his shabby make-up. It\r\nwas Detective Ganimard, the relentless enemy of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. Ganimard\r\nsaid to his companion, Folenfant:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn five minutes, we will see our clever friend Lupin. Is everything\r\nready?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many men have we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEight\xe2\x80\x94two of them on bicycles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnough, but not too many. On no account, must Gerbois escape us; if he\r\ndoes, it is all up. He will meet Lupin at the appointed place, give\r\nhalf a million in exchange for the girl, and the game will be over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why doesn\xe2\x80\x99t Gerbois work with us? That would be the better way,\r\nand he could keep all the money himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but he is afraid that if he deceives the other, he will not get\r\nhis daughter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat other?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard pronounced the word in a solemn tone, somewhat timidly, as if\r\nhe were speaking of some supernatural creature whose claws he already\r\nfelt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very strange,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Folenfant, judiciously, \xe2\x80\x9cthat we are\r\nobliged to protect this gentleman contrary to his own wishes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but Lupin always turns the world upside down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard,\r\nmournfully.\r\n\r\nA moment later, Mon. Gerbois appeared, and started up the street. At\r\nthe end of the rue des Capucines, he turned into the boulevards,\r\nwalking slowly, and stopping frequently to gaze at the shop-windows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch too calm, too self-possessed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cA man with a\r\nmillion in his pocket would not have that air of tranquillity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is he doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! nothing, evidently.... But I have a suspicion that it is\r\nLupin\xe2\x80\x94yes, Lupin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment, Mon. Gerbois stopped at a news-stand, purchased a\r\npaper, unfolded it and commenced to read it as he walked slowly away. A\r\nmoment later, he gave a sudden bound into an automobile that was\r\nstanding at the curb. Apparently, the machine had been waiting for him,\r\nas it started away rapidly, turned at the Madeleine and disappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNom de nom!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s one of his old tricks!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard hastened after the automobile around the Madeleine. Then, he\r\nburst into laughter. At the entrance to the Boulevard Malesherbes, the\r\nautomobile had stopped and Mon. Gerbois had alighted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick, Folenfant, the chauffeur! It may be the man Ernest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFolenfant interviewed the chauffeur. His name was Gaston; he was an\r\nemployee of the automobile cab company; ten minutes ago, a gentleman\r\nhad engaged him and told him to wait near the news-stand for another\r\ngentleman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the second man\xe2\x80\x94what address did he give?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Folenfant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo address. \xe2\x80\x98Boulevard Malesherbes ... avenue de Messine ... double\r\npourboire.\xe2\x80\x99 That is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut, during this time, Mon. Gerbois had leaped into the first passing\r\ncarriage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the Concorde station, Metropolitan,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to the driver.\r\n\r\nHe left the underground at the Place du Palais-Royal, ran to another\r\ncarriage and ordered it to go to the Place de la Bourse. Then a second\r\njourney by the underground to the Avenue de Villiers, followed by a\r\nthird carriage drive to number 25 rue Clapeyron.\r\n\r\nNumber 25 rue Clapeyron is separated from the Boulevard des Batignolles\r\nby the house which occupies the angle formed by the two streets. He\r\nascended to the first floor and rang. A gentleman opened the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes Monsieur Detinan live here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is my name. Are you Monsieur Gerbois?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was expecting you. Step in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs Mon. Gerbois entered the lawyer\xe2\x80\x99s office, the clock struck three. He\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am prompt to the minute. Is he here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Gerbois took a seat, wiped his forehead, looked at his watch as if\r\nhe did not know the time, and inquired, anxiously:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill he come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the lawyer, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I do not know, but I am\r\nquite as anxious and impatient as you are to find out. If he comes, he\r\nwill run a great risk, as this house has been closely watched for the\r\nlast two weeks. They distrust me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey suspect me, too. I am not sure whether the detectives lost sight\r\nof me or not on my way here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you were\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be my fault,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the professor, quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot\r\nreproach me. I promised to obey his orders, and I followed them to the\r\nvery letter. I drew the money at the time fixed by him, and I came here\r\nin the manner directed by him. I have faithfully performed my part of\r\nthe agreement\xe2\x80\x94let him do his!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a short silence, he asked, anxiously:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will bring my daughter, won\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut ... you have seen him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? No, not yet. He made the appointment by letter, saying both of you\r\nwould be here, and asking me to dismiss my servants before three\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock and admit no one while you were here. If I would not consent to\r\nthat arrangement, I was to notify him by a few words in the _Echo de\r\nFrance_. But I am only too happy to oblige Mon. Lupin, and so I\r\nconsented.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! how will this end?\xe2\x80\x9d moaned Mon. Gerbois.\r\n\r\nHe took the bank-notes from his pocket, placed them on the table and\r\ndivided them into two equal parts. Then the two men sat there in\r\nsilence. From time to time, Mon. Gerbois would listen. Did someone\r\nring?... His nervousness increased every minute, and Monsieur Detinan\r\nalso displayed considerable anxiety. At last, the lawyer lost his\r\npatience. He rose abruptly, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will not come.... We shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t expect it. It would be folly on his\r\npart. He would run too great a risk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Mon. Gerbois, despondent, his hands resting on the bank-notes,\r\nstammered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Mon Dieu! I hope he will come. I would give the whole of that\r\nmoney to see my daughter again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door opened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf of it will be sufficient, Monsieur Gerbois.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese words were spoken by a well-dressed young man who now entered the\r\nroom and was immediately recognized by Mon. Gerbois as the person who\r\nhad wished to buy the desk from him at Versailles. He rushed toward\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is my daughter\xe2\x80\x94my Suzanne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin carefully closed the door, and, while slowly removing his\r\ngloves, said to the lawyer:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear ma\xc3\xaetre, I am indebted to you very much for your kindness in\r\nconsenting to defend my interests. I shall not forget it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Detinan murmured:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you did not ring. I did not hear the door\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoors and bells are things that should work without being heard. I am\r\nhere, and that is the important point.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy daughter! Suzanne! Where is she!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated the professor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMon Dieu, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\xe2\x80\x99s your hurry? Your daughter\r\nwill be here in a moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin walked to and fro for a minute, then, with the pompous air of an\r\norator, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Gerbois, I congratulate you on the clever way in which you\r\nmade the journey to this place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, perceiving the two piles of bank-notes, he exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! I see! the million is here. We will not lose any time. Permit me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne moment,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer, placing himself before the table. \xe2\x80\x9cMlle.\r\nGerbois has not yet arrived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs not her presence indispensable?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand! I understand! Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin inspires only a limited\r\nconfidence. He might pocket the half-million and not restore the\r\nhostage. Ah! monsieur, people do not understand me. Because I have been\r\nobliged, by force of circumstances, to commit certain actions a little\r\n... out of the ordinary, my good faith is impugned ... I, who have\r\nalways observed the utmost scrupulosity and delicacy in business\r\naffairs. Besides, my dear monsieur if you have any fear, open the\r\nwindow and call. There are at least a dozen detectives in the street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin raised the curtain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that Monsieur Gerbois could not throw Ganimard off the\r\nscent.... What did I tell you? There he is now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the professor. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I swear to you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat you have not betrayed me?... I do not doubt you, but those\r\nfellows are clever\xe2\x80\x94sometimes. Ah! I can see Folenfant, and Greaume, and\r\nDieuzy\xe2\x80\x94all good friends of mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Detinan looked at Lupin in amazement. What assurance! He laughed\r\nas merrily as if engaged in some childish sport, as if no danger\r\nthreatened him. This unconcern reassured the lawyer more than the\r\npresence of the detectives. He left the table on which the bank-notes\r\nwere lying. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin picked up one pile of bills after the other,\r\ntook from each of them twenty-five bank-notes which he offered to Mon.\r\nDetinan, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe reward of your services to Monsieur Gerbois and Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. You\r\nwell deserve it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou owe me nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the lawyer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! After all the trouble we have caused you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd all the pleasure you have given me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat means, my dear monsieur, that you do not wish to accept anything\r\nfrom Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. See what it is to have a bad reputation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe then offered the fifty thousand francs to Mon. Gerbois, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur, in memory of our pleasant interview, permit me to return you\r\nthis as a wedding-gift to Mlle. Gerbois.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Gerbois took the money, but said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy daughter will not marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will not marry if you refuse your consent; but she wishes to\r\nmarry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you know about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that young girls often dream of such things unknown to their\r\nparents. Fortunately, there are sometimes good genii like Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\nwho discover their little secrets in the drawers of their writing\r\ndesks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you find anything else?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the lawyer. \xe2\x80\x9cI confess I am curious\r\nto know why you took so much trouble to get possession of that desk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn account of its historic interest, my friend. Although despite the\r\nopinion of Monsieur Gerbois, the desk contained no treasure except the\r\nlottery ticket\xe2\x80\x94and that was unknown to me\xe2\x80\x94I had been seeking it for a\r\nlong time. That writing-desk of yew and mahogany was discovered in the\r\nlittle house in which Marie Wal\xc3\xaawska once lived in Boulogne, and, on\r\none of the drawers there is this inscription: \xe2\x80\x98_Dedicated to Napoleon\r\nI, Emperor of the French, by his very faithful servant, Mancion_.\xe2\x80\x99 And\r\nabove it, these words, engraved with the point of a knife: \xe2\x80\x98To you,\r\nMarie.\xe2\x80\x99 Afterwards, Napoleon had a similar desk made for the Empress\r\nJosephine; so that the secretary that was so much admired at the\r\nMalmaison was only an imperfect copy of the one that will henceforth\r\nform part of my collection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! if I had known, when in the shop, I would gladly have given it up\r\nto you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the professor.\r\n\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin smiled, as he replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you would have had the advantage of keeping for your own use\r\nlottery ticket number 514.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you would not have found it necessary to abduct my daughter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbduct your daughter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear monsieur, you are mistaken. Mlle. Gerbois was not abducted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not. Abduction means force or violence. And I assure you\r\nthat she served as hostage of her own free will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf her own free will!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Mon. Gerbois, in amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn fact, she almost asked to be taken. Why, do you suppose that an\r\nintelligent young girl like Mlle. Gerbois, and who, moreover, nourishes\r\nan unacknowledged passion, would hesitate to do what was necessary to\r\nsecure her dowry. Ah! I swear to you it was not difficult to make her\r\nunderstand that it was the only way to overcome your obstinacy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Detinan was greatly amused. He replied to Lupin:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I should think it was more difficult to get her to listen to you.\r\nHow did you approach her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t approach her myself. I have not the honor of her\r\nacquaintance. A friend of mine, a lady, carried on the negotiations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blonde woman in the automobile, no doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely. All arrangements were made at the first interview near the\r\ncollege. Since then, Mlle. Gerbois and her new friend have been\r\ntravelling in Belgium and Holland in a manner that should prove most\r\npleasing and instructive to a young girl. She will tell you all about\r\nit herself\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bell of the vestibule door rang, three rings in quick succession,\r\nfollowed by two isolated rings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is she,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Detinan, if you will be so kind\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lawyer hastened to the door.\r\n\r\nTwo young women entered. One of them threw herself into the arms of\r\nMon. Gerbois. The other approached Lupin. The latter was a tall woman\r\nof a good figure, very pale complexion, and with blond hair, parted\r\nover her forehead in undulating waves, that glistened and shone like\r\nthe setting sun. She was dressed in black, with no display of jewelled\r\nornaments; but, on the contrary, her appearance indicated good taste\r\nand refined elegance. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin spoke a few words to her; then,\r\nbowing to Mlle. Gerbois, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI owe you an apology, mademoiselle, for all your troubles, but I hope\r\nyou have not been too unhappy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnhappy! Why, I should have been very happy, indeed, if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been\r\nfor leaving my poor father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen all is for the best. Kiss him again, and take advantage of the\r\nopportunity\xe2\x80\x94it is an excellent one\xe2\x80\x94to speak to him about your cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy cousin! What do you mean? I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, you understand. Your cousin Philippe. The young man whose\r\nletters you kept so carefully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuzanne blushed; but, following Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s advice, she again threw herself\r\ninto her father\xe2\x80\x99s arms. Lupin gazed upon them with a tender look.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Such is my reward for a virtuous act! What a touching picture! A\r\nhappy father and a happy daughter! And to know that their joy is your\r\nwork, Lupin! Hereafter these people will bless you, and reverently\r\ntransmit your name unto their descendants, even unto the fourth\r\ngeneration. What a glorious reward, Lupin, for one act of kindness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe walked to the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs dear old Ganimard still waiting?... He would like very much to be\r\npresent at this charming domestic scene!... Ah! he is not there.... Nor\r\nany of the others.... I don\xe2\x80\x99t see anyone. The deuce! The situation is\r\nbecoming serious. I dare say they are already under the porte-cochere\r\n... talking to the concierge, perhaps ... or, even, ascending the\r\nstairs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Gerbois made a sudden movement. Now, that his daughter had been\r\nrestored to him, he saw the situation in a different light. To him, the\r\narrest of his adversary meant half-a-million francs. Instinctively, he\r\nmade a step forward. As if by chance, Lupin stood in his way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going, Monsieur Gerbois? To defend me against them! That\r\nis very kind of you, but I assure you it is not necessary. They are\r\nmore worried than I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he continued to speak, with calm deliberation:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, really, what do they know? That you are here, and, perhaps, that\r\nMlle. Gerbois is here, for they may have seen her arrive with an\r\nunknown lady. But they do not imagine that I am here. How is it\r\npossible that I could be in a house that they ransacked from cellar to\r\ngarret this morning? They suppose that the unknown lady was sent by me\r\nto make the exchange, and they will be ready to arrest her when she\r\ngoes out\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment, the bell rang. With a brusque movement, Lupin seized\r\nMon. Gerbois, and said to him, in an imperious tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not move! Remember your daughter, and be prudent\xe2\x80\x94otherwise\xe2\x80\x94 As to\r\nyou, Monsieur Detinan, I have your promise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Gerbois was rooted to the spot. The lawyer did not stir. Without\r\nthe least sign of haste, Lupin picked up his hat and brushed the dust\r\nfrom off it with his sleeve.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Monsieur Detinan, if I can ever be of service to you.... My\r\nbest wishes, Mademoiselle Suzanne, and my kind regards to Monsieur\r\nPhilippe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drew a heavy gold watch from his pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Gerbois, it is now forty-two minutes past three. At forty-six\r\nminutes past three, I give you permission to leave this room. Not one\r\nminute sooner than forty-six minutes past three.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut they will force an entrance,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Mon. Detinan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou forget the law, my dear monsieur! Ganimard would never venture to\r\nviolate the privacy of a French citizen. But, pardon me, time flies,\r\nand you are all slightly nervous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe placed his watch on the table, opened the door of the room and\r\naddressing the blonde lady he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you ready my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drew back to let her pass, bowed respectfully to Mlle. Gerbois, and\r\nwent out, closing the door behind him. Then they heard him in the\r\nvestibule, speaking, in a loud voice: \xe2\x80\x9cGood-day, Ganimard, how goes it?\r\nRemember me to Madame Ganimard. One of these days, I shall invite her\r\nto breakfast. Au revoir, Ganimard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bell rang violently, followed by repeated rings, and voices on the\r\nlanding.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForty-five minutes,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Mon. Gerbois.\r\n\r\nAfter a few seconds, he left the room and stepped into the vestibule.\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin and the blonde lady had gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapa!... you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t! Wait!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Suzanne.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait! you are foolish!... No quarter for that rascal!... And the\r\nhalf-million?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe opened the outer door. Ganimard rushed in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat woman\xe2\x80\x94where is she? And Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was here ... he is here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard uttered a cry of triumph.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have him. The house is surrounded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the servant\xe2\x80\x99s stairway?\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Mon. Detinan.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt leads to the court,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is only one exit\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nstreet-door. Ten men are guarding it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he didn\xe2\x80\x99t come in by the street-door, and he will not go out that\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat way, then?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cThrough the air?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe drew aside a curtain and exposed a long corridor leading to the\r\nkitchen. Ganimard ran along it and tried the door of the servants\xe2\x80\x99\r\nstairway. It was locked. From the window he called to one of his\r\nassistants:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeen anyone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen they are still in the house!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are hiding in\r\none of the rooms! They cannot have escaped. Ah! Lupin, you fooled me\r\nbefore, but, this time, I get my revenge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nAt seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the evening, Mon. Dudonis, chief of the detective\r\nservice, astonished at not receiving any news, visited the rue\r\nClapeyron. He questioned the detectives who were guarding the house,\r\nthen ascended to Mon. Detinan\xe2\x80\x99s apartment. The lawyer led him into his\r\nroom. There, Mon. Dudonis beheld a man, or rather two legs kicking in\r\nthe air, while the body to which they belonged was hidden in the depths\r\nof the chimney.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xc3\xa9!... Oh\xc3\xa9!\xe2\x80\x9d gasped a stifled voice. And a more distant voice, from\r\non high, replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xc3\xa9!... Oh\xc3\xa9!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Dudonis laughed, and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere! Ganimard, have you turned chimney-sweep?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe detective crawled out of the chimney. With his blackened face, his\r\nsooty clothes, and his feverish eyes, he was quite unrecognizable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am looking for _him_,\xe2\x80\x9d he growled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin ... and his friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, do you suppose they are hiding in the chimney?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard arose, laid his sooty hand on the sleeve of his superior\r\nofficer\xe2\x80\x99s coat, and exclaimed, angrily:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you think they are, chief? They must be somewhere! They are\r\nflesh and blood like you and me, and can\xe2\x80\x99t fade away like smoke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but they have faded away just the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how? How? The house is surrounded by our men\xe2\x80\x94even on the roof.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about the adjoining house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no communication with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the apartments on the other floors?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know all the tenants. They have not seen anyone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure you know all of them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. The concierge answers for them. Besides, as an extra precaution,\r\nI have placed a man in each apartment. They can\xe2\x80\x99t escape. If I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nget them to-night, I will get them to-morrow. I shall sleep here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe slept there that night and the two following nights. Three days and\r\nnights passed away without the discovery of the irrepressible Lupin or\r\nhis female companion; more than that, Ganimard did not unearth the\r\nslightest clue on which to base a theory to explain their escape. For\r\nthat reason, he adhered to his first opinion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no trace of their escape; therefore, they are here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt may be that, at the bottom of his heart, his conviction was less\r\nfirmly established, but he would not confess it. No, a thousand times,\r\nno! A man and a woman could not vanish like the evil spirits in a fairy\r\ntale. And, without losing his courage, he continued his searches, as if\r\nhe expected to find the fugitives concealed in some impenetrable\r\nretreat, or embodied in the stone walls of the house.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II.\r\nTHE BLUE DIAMOND.\r\n\r\n\r\nOn the evening of March 27, at number 134 avenue Henri-Martin, in the\r\nhouse that he had inherited from his brother six months before, the old\r\ngeneral Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, ambassador at Berlin under the second Empire,\r\nwas asleep in a comfortable armchair, while his secretary was reading\r\nto him, and the Sister Auguste was warming his bed and preparing the\r\nnight-lamp. At eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock, the Sister, who was obliged to return to\r\nthe convent of her order at that hour, said to the secretary:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle Antoinette, my work is finished; I am going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, Sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not forget that the cook is away, and that you are alone in the\r\nhouse with the servant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave no fear for the Baron. I sleep in the adjoining room and always\r\nleave the door open.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Sister left the house. A few moments later, Charles, the servant,\r\ncame to receive his orders. The Baron was now awake, and spoke for\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe usual orders, Charles: see that the electric bell rings in your\r\nroom, and, at the first alarm, run for the doctor. Now, Mademoiselle\r\nAntoinette, how far did we get in our reading?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Monsieur not going to bed now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, I will go later. Besides, I don\xe2\x80\x99t need anyone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwenty minutes later, he was sleeping again, and Antoinette crept away\r\non tiptoe. At that moment, Charles was closing the shutters on the\r\nlower floor. In the kitchen, he bolted the door leading to the garden,\r\nand, in the vestibule, he not only locked the door but hooked the chain\r\nas well. Then he ascended to his room on the third floor, went to bed,\r\nand was soon asleep.\r\n\r\nProbably an hour had passed, when he leaped from his bed in alarm. The\r\nbell was ringing. It rang for some time, seven or eight seconds\r\nperhaps, without intermission.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Charles, recovering his wits, \xe2\x80\x9canother of the Baron\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhims.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe dressed himself quickly, descended the stairs, stopped in front of\r\nthe door, and rapped, according to his custom. He received no reply. He\r\nopened the door and entered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! no light,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, in a low voice, he called:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you there, mademoiselle? What\xe2\x80\x99s the matter? Is Monsieur le Baron\r\nill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo reply. Nothing but a profound silence that soon became depressing.\r\nHe took two steps forward; his foot struck a chair, and, having touched\r\nit, he noticed that it was overturned. Then, with his hand, he\r\ndiscovered other objects on the floor\xe2\x80\x94a small table and a screen.\r\nAnxiously, he approached the wall, felt for the electric button, and\r\nturned on the light.\r\n\r\nIn the centre of the room, between the table and dressing-case, lay the\r\nbody of his master, the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!... It can\xe2\x80\x99t be possible!\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered.\r\n\r\nHe could not move. He stood there, with bulging eyes, gazing stupidly\r\nat the terrible disorder, the overturned chairs, a large crystal\r\ncandelabra shattered in a thousand pieces, the clock lying on the\r\nmarble hearthstone, all evidence of a fearful and desperate struggle.\r\nThe handle of a stiletto glittered, not far from the corpse; the blade\r\nwas stained with blood. A handkerchief, marked with red spots, was\r\nlying on the edge of the bed.\r\n\r\nCharles recoiled with horror: the body lying at his feet extended\r\nitself for a moment, then shrunk up again; two or three tremors, and\r\nthat was the end.\r\n\r\nHe stooped over the body. There was a clean-cut wound on the neck from\r\nwhich the blood was flowing and then congealing in a black pool on the\r\ncarpet. The face retained an expression of extreme terror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome one has killed him!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, \xe2\x80\x9csome one has killed him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he shuddered at the thought that there might be another dreadful\r\ncrime. Did not the baron\xe2\x80\x99s secretary sleep in the adjoining room? Had\r\nnot the assassin killed her also? He opened the door; the room was\r\nempty. He concluded that Antoinette had been abducted, or else she had\r\ngone away before the crime. He returned to the baron\xe2\x80\x99s chamber, his\r\nglance falling on the secretary, he noticed that that article of\r\nfurniture remained intact. Then, he saw upon a table, beside a bunch of\r\nkeys and a pocketbook that the baron placed there every night, a\r\nhandful of golden louis. Charles seized the pocketbook, opened it, and\r\nfound some bank-notes. He counted them; there were thirteen notes of\r\none hundred francs each.\r\n\r\nInstinctively, mechanically, he put the bank-notes in his pocket,\r\nrushed down the stairs, drew the bolt, unhooked the chain, closed the\r\ndoor behind him, and fled to the street.\r\n\r\n\r\nCharles was an honest man. He had scarcely left the gate, when, cooled\r\nby the night air and the rain, he came to a sudden halt. Now, he saw\r\nhis action in its true light, and it filled him with horror. He hailed\r\na passing cab, and said to the driver:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo to the police-office, and bring the commissary. Hurry! There has\r\nbeen a murder in that house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cab-driver whipped his horse. Charles wished to return to the\r\nhouse, but found the gate locked. He had closed it himself when he came\r\nout, and it could not be opened from the outside. On the other hand, it\r\nwas useless to ring, as there was no one in the house.\r\n\r\nIt was almost an hour before the arrival of the police. When they came,\r\nCharles told his story and handed the bank-notes to the commissary. A\r\nlocksmith was summoned, and, after considerable difficulty, he\r\nsucceeded in forcing open the garden gate and the vestibule door. The\r\ncommissary of police entered the room first, but, immediately, turned\r\nto Charles and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou told me that the room was in the greatest disorder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharles stood at the door, amazed, bewildered; all the furniture had\r\nbeen restored to its accustomed place. The small table was standing\r\nbetween the two windows, the chairs were upright, and the clock was on\r\nthe centre of the mantel. The debris of the candelabra had been\r\nremoved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is.... Monsieur le Baron?\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Charles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s so!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the officer, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere is the victim?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe approached the bed, and drew aside a large sheet, under which\r\nreposed the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, formerly French Ambassador at Berlin. Over\r\nhim, lay his military coat, adorned with the Cross of Honor. His\r\nfeatures were calm. His eyes were closed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome one has been here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did they get in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, but some one has been here during my absence. There was\r\na stiletto on the floor\xe2\x80\x94there! And a handkerchief, stained with blood,\r\non the bed. They are not here now. They have been carried away. And\r\nsome one has put the room in order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho would do that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe assassin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we found all the doors locked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must have remained in the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he must be here yet, as you were in front of the house all the\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharles reflected a moment, then said, slowly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes ... of course.... I didn\xe2\x80\x99t go away from the gate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was the last person you saw with the baron?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle Antoinette, his secretary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has become of her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. Her bed wasn\xe2\x80\x99t occupied, so she must have gone out. I am\r\nnot surprised at that, as she is young and pretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how could she leave the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the door,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you had bolted and chained it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but she must have left before that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the crime was committed after her departure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d said the servant.\r\n\r\nThe house was searched from cellar to garret, but the assassin had\r\nfled. How? And when? Was it he or an accomplice who had returned to the\r\nscene of the crime and removed everything that might furnish a clue to\r\nhis identity? Such were the questions the police were called upon to\r\nsolve.\r\n\r\nThe coroner came at seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock; and, at eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Mon. Dudouis,\r\nthe head of the detective service, arrived on the scene. They were\r\nfollowed by the Procureur of the Republic and the investigating\r\nmagistrate. In addition to these officials, the house was overrun with\r\npolicemen, detectives, newspaper reporters, photographers, and\r\nrelatives and acquaintances of the murdered man.\r\n\r\nA thorough search was made; they studied out the position of the corpse\r\naccording to the information furnished by Charles; they questioned\r\nSister Auguste when she arrived; but they discovered nothing new.\r\nSister Auguste was astonished to learn of the disappearance of\r\nAntoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat. She had engaged the young girl twelve days before,\r\non excellent recommendations, and refused to believe that she would\r\nneglect her duty by leaving the house during the night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, you see, she hasn\xe2\x80\x99t returned yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said the magistrate, \xe2\x80\x9cand we\r\nare still confronted with the question: What has become of her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think she was abducted by the assassin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charles.\r\n\r\nThe theory was plausible, and was borne out by certain facts. Mon.\r\nDudouis agreed with it. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbducted? ma foi! that is not improbable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot only improbable,\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice, \xe2\x80\x9cbut absolutely opposed to the\r\nfacts. There is not a particle of evidence to support such a theory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe voice was harsh, the accent sharp, and no one was surprised to\r\nlearn that the speaker was Ganimard. In no one else, would they\r\ntolerate such a domineering tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! it is you, Ganimard!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mon. Dudouis. \xe2\x80\x9cI had not seen you\r\nbefore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have been here since two o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you are interested in some things outside of lottery ticket number\r\n514, the affair of the rue Clapeyron, the blonde lady and Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa-ha!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed the veteran detective. \xe2\x80\x9cI would not say that Lupin is\r\na stranger to the present case. But let us forget the affair of the\r\nlottery ticket for a few moments, and try to unravel this new mystery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nGanimard is not one of those celebrated detectives whose methods will\r\ncreate a school, or whose name will be immortalized in the criminal\r\nannals of his country. He is devoid of those flashes of genius which\r\ncharacterize the work of Dupin, Lecoq and Sherlock Holmes. Yet, it must\r\nbe admitted, he possesses superior qualities of observation, sagacity,\r\nperseverance and even intuition. His merit lies in his absolute\r\nindependence. Nothing troubles or influences him, except, perhaps, a\r\nsort of fascination that Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin holds over him. However that may\r\nbe, there is no doubt that his position on that morning, in the house\r\nof the late Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, was one of undoubted superiority, and his\r\ncollaboration in the case was appreciated and desired by the\r\ninvestigating magistrate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the first place,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9cI will ask Monsieur Charles to be\r\nvery particular on one point: He says that, on the occasion of his\r\nfirst visit to the room, various articles of furniture were overturned\r\nand strewn about the place; now, I ask him whether, on his second visit\r\nto the room, he found all those articles restored to their accustomed\r\nplaces\xe2\x80\x94I mean, of course, correctly placed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, all in their proper places,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Charles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is obvious, then, that the person who replaced them must have been\r\nfamiliar with the location of those articles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe logic of this remark was apparent to his hearers. Ganimard\r\ncontinued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne more question, Monsieur Charles. You were awakened by the ringing\r\nof your bell. Now, who, do you think, rang it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur le baron, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen could he ring it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter the struggle ... when he was dying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible; because you found him lying, unconscious, at a point more\r\nthan four metres from the bell-button.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he must have rung during the struggle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9csince the ringing, as you have said,\r\nwas continuous and uninterrupted, and lasted seven or eight seconds. Do\r\nyou think his antagonist would have permitted him to ring the bell in\r\nthat leisurely manner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, it was before the attack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlso, quite impossible, since you have told us that the lapse of time\r\nbetween the ringing of the bell and your entrance to the room was not\r\nmore than three minutes. Therefore, if the baron rang before the\r\nattack, we are forced to the conclusion that the struggle, the murder\r\nand the flight of the assassin, all occurred within the short space of\r\nthree minutes. I repeat: that is impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said the magistrate, \xe2\x80\x9csome one rang. If it were not the\r\nbaron, who was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe murderer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor what purpose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know. But the fact that he did ring proves that he knew that\r\nthe bell communicated with the servant\xe2\x80\x99s room. Now, who would know\r\nthat, except an inmate of the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard was drawing the meshes of his net closer and tighter. In a few\r\nclear and logical sentences, he had unfolded and defined his theory of\r\nthe crime, so that it seemed quite natural when the magistrate said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I understand it, Ganimard, you suspect the girl Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not suspect her; I accuse her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou accuse her of being an accomplice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI accuse her of having killed Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! What proof have you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe handful of hair I found in the right hand of the victim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe produced the hair; it was of a beautiful blond color, and glittered\r\nlike threads of gold. Charles looked at it, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Mademoiselle Antoinette\xe2\x80\x99s hair. There can be no doubt of it.\r\nAnd, then, there is another thing. I believe that the knife, which I\r\nsaw on my first visit to the room, belonged to her. She used it to cut\r\nthe leaves of books.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA long, dreadful silence followed, as if the crime had acquired an\r\nadditional horror by reason of having been committed by a woman. At\r\nlast, the magistrate said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us assume, until we are better informed, that the baron was killed\r\nby Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat. We have yet to learn where she concealed herself\r\nafter the crime, how she managed to return after Charles left the\r\nhouse, and how she made her escape after the arrival of the police.\r\nHave you formed any opinion on those points Ganimard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, where do we stand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard was embarrassed. Finally, with a visible effort, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll I can say is that I find in this case the same method of procedure\r\nas we found in the affair of the lottery ticket number 514; the same\r\nphenomena, which might be termed the faculty of disappearing.\r\nAntoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat has appeared and disappeared in this house as\r\nmysteriously as Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin entered the house of Monsieur Detinan and\r\nescaped therefrom in the company of the blonde lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes that signify anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does to me. I can see a probable connection between those two\r\nstrange incidents. Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat was hired by Sister Auguste twelve\r\ndays ago, that is to say, on the day after the blonde Lady so cleverly\r\nslipped through my fingers. In the second place, the hair of the blonde\r\nLady was exactly of the same brilliant golden hue as the hair found in\r\nthis case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that, in your opinion, Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the blonde Lady\xe2\x80\x94precisely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that Lupin had a hand in both cases?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is my opinion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis statement was greeted with an outburst of laughter. It came from\r\nMon. Dudouis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLupin! always Lupin! Lupin is into everything; Lupin is everywhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Lupin is into everything of any consequence,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ganimard,\r\nvexed at the ridicule of his superior.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, so far as I see,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mon. Dudouis, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have not\r\ndiscovered any motive for this crime. The secretary was not broken\r\ninto, nor the pocketbook carried away. Even, a pile of gold was left\r\nupon the table.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is so,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9cbut the famous diamond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat diamond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blue diamond! The celebrated diamond which formed part of the\r\nroyal crown of France, and which was given by the Duke d\xe2\x80\x99Aumale to\r\nLeonide Lebrun, and, at the death of Leonide Lebrun, was purchased by\r\nthe Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec as a souvenir of the charming comedienne that he\r\nhad loved so well. That is one of those things that an old Parisian,\r\nlike I, does not forget.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is obvious that if the blue diamond is not found, the motive for\r\nthe crime is disclosed,\xe2\x80\x9d said the magistrate. \xe2\x80\x9cBut where should we\r\nsearch for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the baron\xe2\x80\x99s finger,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Charles. \xe2\x80\x9cHe always wore the blue\r\ndiamond on his left hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw that hand, and there was only a plain gold ring on it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nGanimard, as he approached the corpse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook in the palm of the hand,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the servant.\r\n\r\nGanimard opened the stiffened hand. The bezel was turned inward, and,\r\nin the centre of that bezel, the blue diamond shone with all its\r\nglorious splendor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Ganimard, absolutely amazed, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will now apologize to Lupin for having suspected him, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMon. Dudouis, laughing.\r\n\r\nGanimard paused for a moment\xe2\x80\x99s reflection, and then replied,\r\nsententiously:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only when I do not understand things that I suspect Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuch were the facts established by the police on the day after the\r\ncommission of that mysterious crime. Facts that were vague and\r\nincoherent in themselves, and which were not explained by any\r\nsubsequent discoveries. The movements of Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat remained as\r\ninexplicable as those of the blonde Lady, and the police discovered no\r\ntrace of that mysterious creature with the golden hair who had killed\r\nBaron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec and had failed to take from his finger the famous\r\ndiamond that had once shone in the royal crown of France.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe heirs of the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec could not fail to benefit by such\r\nnotoriety. They established in the house an exhibition of the furniture\r\nand other objects which were to be sold at the auction rooms of Drouot\r\n& Co. Modern furniture of indifferent taste, various objects of no\r\nartistic value ... but, in the centre of the room, in a case of purple\r\nvelvet, protected by a glass globe, and guarded by two officers, was\r\nthe famous blue diamond ring.\r\n\r\nA large magnificent diamond of incomparable purity, and of that\r\nindefinite blue which the clear water receives from an unclouded sky,\r\nof that blue which can be detected in the whiteness of linen. Some\r\nadmired, some enthused ... and some looked with horror on the chamber\r\nof the victim, on the spot where the corpse had lain, on the floor\r\ndivested of its blood-stained carpet, and especially the walls, the\r\nunsurmountable walls over which the criminal must have passed. Some\r\nassured themselves that the marble mantel did not move, others imagined\r\ngaping holes, mouths of tunnels, secret connections with the sewers,\r\nand the catacombs\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nThe sale of the blue diamond took place at the salesroom of Drouot &\r\nCo. The place was crowded to suffocation, and the bidding was carried\r\nto the verge of folly. The sale was attended by all those who usually\r\nappear at similar events in Paris; those who buy, and those who make a\r\npretense of being able to buy; bankers, brokers, artists, women of all\r\nclasses, two cabinet ministers, an Italian tenor, an exiled king who,\r\nin order to maintain his credit, bid, with much ostentation, and in a\r\nloud voice, as high as one hundred thousand francs. One hundred\r\nthousand francs! He could offer that sum without any danger of his bid\r\nbeing accepted. The Italian tenor risked one hundred and fifty\r\nthousand, and a member of the Com\xc3\xa9die-Fran\xc3\xa7aise bid one hundred and\r\nseventy-five thousand francs.\r\n\r\nWhen the bidding reached two hundred thousand francs, the smaller\r\ncompetitors fell out of the race. At two hundred and fifty thousand,\r\nonly two bidders remained in the field: Herschmann, the well-known\r\ncapitalist, the king of gold mines; and the Countess de Crozon, the\r\nwealthy American, whose collection of diamonds and precious stones is\r\nfamed throughout the world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo hundred and sixty thousand ... two hundred and seventy thousand\r\n... seventy-five ... eighty....\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the auctioneer, as he\r\nglanced at the two competitors in succession. \xe2\x80\x9cTwo hundred and eighty\r\nthousand for madame.... Do I hear any more?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree hundred thousand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Herschmann.\r\n\r\nThere was a short silence.
