The countess was standing, smiling, but pale\r\nfrom excitement. She was leaning against the back of the chair in front\r\nof her. She knew, and so did everyone present, that the issue of the\r\nduel was certain; logically, inevitably, it must terminate to the\r\nadvantage of the capitalist, who had untold millions with which to\r\nindulge his caprices. However, the countess made another bid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree hundred and five thousand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother silence. All eyes were now directed to the capitalist in the\r\nexpectation that he would raise the bidding. But Herschmann was not\r\npaying any attention to the sale; his eyes were fixed on a sheet of\r\npaper which he held in his right hand, while the other hand held a torn\r\nenvelope.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree hundred and five thousand,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated the auctioneer. \xe2\x80\x9cOnce!...\r\nTwice!... For the last time.... Do I hear any more?... Once!...\r\nTwice!... Am I offered any more? Last chance!...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHerschmann did not move.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThird and last time!... Sold!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the auctioneer, as his hammer\r\nfell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFour hundred thousand,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Herschman, starting up, as if the sound\r\nof the hammer had roused him from his stupor.\r\n\r\nToo late; the auctioneer\xe2\x80\x99s decision was irrevocable. Some of\r\nHerschmann\xe2\x80\x99s acquaintances pressed around him. What was the matter? Why\r\ndid he not speak sooner? He laughed, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMa foi! I simply forgot\xe2\x80\x94in a moment of abstraction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is strange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, I just received a letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that letter was sufficient\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo distract my attention? Yes, for a moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard was there. He had come to witness the sale of the ring. He\r\nstopped one of the attendants of the auction room, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it you who carried the letter to Monsieur Herschmann?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho gave it to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is she?... She was sitting down there ... the lady who wore a\r\nthick veil.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has gone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, just this moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard hastened to the door, and saw the lady descending the stairs.\r\nHe ran after her. A crush of people delayed him at the entrance. When\r\nhe reached the sidewalk, she had disappeared. He returned to the\r\nauction room, accosted Herschmann, introduced himself, and enquired\r\nabout the letter. Herschmann handed it to him. It was carelessly\r\nscribbled in pencil, in a handwriting unknown to the capitalist, and\r\ncontained these few words:\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cThe blue diamond brings misfortune. Remember the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec.\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\r\n\r\nThe vicissitudes of the blue diamond were not yet at an end. Although\r\nit had become well-known through the murder of the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec and\r\nthe incidents at the auction-rooms, it was six months later that it\r\nattained even greater celebrity. During the following summer, the\r\nCountess de Crozon was robbed of the famous jewel she had taken so much\r\ntrouble to acquire.\r\n\r\nLet me recall that strange affair, of which the exciting and dramatic\r\nincidents sent a thrill through all of us, and over which I am now\r\npermitted to throw some light.\r\n\r\nOn the evening of August 10, the guests of the Count and Countess de\r\nCrozon were assembled in the drawing-room of the magnificent ch\xc3\xa2teau\r\nwhich overlooks the Bay de Somme. To entertain her friends, the\r\ncountess seated herself at the piano to play for them, after first\r\nplacing her jewels on a small table near the piano, and, amongst them,\r\nwas the ring of the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec.\r\n\r\nAn hour later, the count and the majority of the guests retired,\r\nincluding his two cousins and Madame de R\xc3\xa9al, an intimate friend of the\r\ncountess. The latter remained in the drawing-room with Herr Bleichen,\r\nthe Austrian consul, and his wife.\r\n\r\nThey conversed for a time, and then the countess extinguished the large\r\nlamp that stood on a table in the centre of the room. At the same\r\nmoment, Herr Bleichen extinguished the two piano lamps. There was a\r\nmomentary darkness; then the consul lighted a candle, and the three of\r\nthem retired to their rooms. But, as soon as she reached her apartment,\r\nthe countess remembered her jewels and sent her maid to get them. When\r\nthe maid returned with the jewels, she placed them on the mantel\r\nwithout the countess looking at them. Next day, Madame de Crozon found\r\nthat one of her rings was missing; it was the blue diamond ring.\r\n\r\nShe informed her husband, and, after talking it over, they reached the\r\nconclusion that the maid was above suspicion, and that the guilty party\r\nmust be Herr Bleichen.\r\n\r\nThe count notified the commissary of police at Amiens, who commenced an\r\ninvestigation and, discreetly, exercised a strict surveillance over the\r\nAustrian consul to prevent his disposing of the ring.\r\n\r\nThe ch\xc3\xa2teau was surrounded by detectives day and night. Two weeks\r\npassed without incident. Then Herr Bleichen announced his intended\r\ndeparture. That day, a formal complaint was entered against him. The\r\npolice made an official examination of his luggage. In a small satchel,\r\nthe key to which was always carried by the consul himself, they found a\r\nbottle of dentifrice, and in that bottle they found the ring.\r\n\r\nMadame Bleichen fainted. Her husband was placed under arrest.\r\n\r\nEveryone will remember the line of defense adopted by the accused man.\r\nHe declared that the ring must have been placed there by the Count de\r\nCrozen as an act of revenge. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe count is brutal and makes his wife very unhappy. She consulted me,\r\nand I advised her to get a divorce. The count heard of it in some way,\r\nand, to be revenged on me, he took the ring and placed it in my\r\nsatchel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe count and countess persisted in pressing the charge. Between the\r\nexplanation which they gave and that of the consul, both equally\r\npossible and equally probable, the public had to choose. No new fact\r\nwas discovered to turn the scale in either direction. A month of\r\ngossip, conjectures and investigations failed to produce a single ray\r\nof light.\r\n\r\nWearied of the excitement and notoriety, and incapable of securing the\r\nevidence necessary to sustain their charge against the consul, the\r\ncount and countess at last sent to Paris for a detective competent to\r\nunravel the tangled threads of this mysterious skein. This brought\r\nGanimard into the case.\r\n\r\nFor four days, the veteran detective searched the house from top to\r\nbottom, examined every foot of the ground, had long conferences with\r\nthe maid, the chauffeur, the gardeners, the employees in the\r\nneighboring post-offices, visited the rooms that had been occupied by\r\nthe various guests. Then, one morning, he disappeared without taking\r\nleave of his host or hostess. But a week later, they received this\r\ntelegram:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease come to the Japanese Tea-room, rue Boissy d\xe2\x80\x99Anglas, to-morrow,\r\nFriday, evening at five o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Ganimard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nAt five o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Friday evening, their automobile stopped in front of\r\nnumber nine rue Boissy-d\xe2\x80\x99Anglas. The old detective was standing on the\r\nsidewalk, waiting for them. Without a word, he conducted them to the\r\nfirst floor of the Japanese Tea-room. In one of the rooms, they met two\r\nmen, whom Ganimard introduced in these words:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Gerbois, professor in the College of Versailles, from whom,\r\nyou will remember, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin stole half a million; Monsieur L\xc3\xa9once\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, nephew and sole legatee of the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA few minutes later, another man arrived. It was Mon. Dudouis, head of\r\nthe detective service, and he appeared to be in a particularly bad\r\ntemper. He bowed, and then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble now, Ganimard? I received your telephone message\r\nasking me to come here. Is it anything of consequence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, chief, it is a very important matter. Within an hour, the last\r\ntwo cases to which I was assigned will have their d\xc3\xa9nouement here. It\r\nseemed to me that your presence was indispensable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd also the presence of Dieuzy and Folenfant, whom I noticed standing\r\nnear the door as I came in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, chief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor what? Are you going to make an arrest, and you wish to do it with\r\na flourish? Come, Ganimard, I am anxious to hear about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard hesitated a moment, then spoke with the obvious intention of\r\nmaking an impression on his hearers:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the first place, I wish to state that Herr Bleichen had nothing to\r\ndo with the theft of the ring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! oh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mon. Dudouis, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is a bold statement and a very\r\nserious one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is that all you have discovered?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Count de Crozon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. On the second day after the theft, three of your guests\r\nwent on an automobile trip as far as Cr\xc3\xa9cy. Two of them visited the\r\nfamous battlefield; and, while they were there, the third party paid a\r\nhasty visit to the post-office, and mailed a small box, tied and sealed\r\naccording to the regulations, and declared its value to be one hundred\r\nfrancs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see nothing strange in that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the count.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you will see something strange in it when I tell you that this\r\nperson, in place of giving her true name, sent the box under the name\r\nof Rousseau, and the person to whom it was addressed, a certain\r\nMonsieur Beloux of Paris, moved his place of residence immediately\r\nafter receiving the box, in other words, the ring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI presume you refer to one of my cousins d\xe2\x80\x99Andelle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ganimard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame de R\xc3\xa9al, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou accuse my friend, Madam de R\xc3\xa9al?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the countess, shocked and\r\namazed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to ask you one question, madame,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cWas Madam de\r\nR\xc3\xa9al present when you purchased the ring?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but we did not go there together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid she advise you to buy the ring?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe countess considered for a moment, then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think she mentioned it first\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, madame. Your answer establishes the fact that it was Madame\r\nde R\xc3\xa9al who was the first to mention the ring, and it was she who\r\nadvised you to buy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, I consider my friend is quite incapable\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me, countess, when I remind you that Madame de R\xc3\xa9al is only a\r\ncasual acquaintance and not your intimate friend, as the newspapers\r\nhave announced. It was only last winter that you met her for the first\r\ntime. Now, I can prove that everything she has told you about herself,\r\nher past life, and her relatives, is absolutely false; that Madame\r\nBlanche de R\xc3\xa9al had no actual existence before she met you, and she has\r\nnow ceased to exist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ganimard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour story is a very strange one,\xe2\x80\x9d said the countess, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it has no\r\napplication to our case. If Madame de R\xc3\xa9al had taken the ring, how do\r\nyou explain the fact that it was found in Herr Bleichen\xe2\x80\x99s tooth-powder?\r\nAnyone who would take the risk and trouble of stealing the blue diamond\r\nwould certainly keep it. What do you say to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94nothing\xe2\x80\x94but Madame de R\xc3\xa9al will answer it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! she does exist, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe does\xe2\x80\x94and does not. I will explain in a few words. Three days ago,\r\nwhile reading a newspaper, I glanced over the list of hotel arrivals at\r\nTrouville, and there I read: \xe2\x80\x98H\xc3\xb4tel Beaurivage\xe2\x80\x94Madame de R\xc3\xa9al, etc.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went to Trouville immediately, and interviewed the proprietor of the\r\nhotel. From the description and other information I received from him,\r\nI concluded that she was the very Madame de R\xc3\xa9al that I was seeking;\r\nbut she had left the hotel, giving her address in Paris as number three\r\nrue de Colis\xc3\xa9e. The day before yesterday I went to that address, and\r\nlearned that there was no person there called Madame de R\xc3\xa9al, but there\r\nwas a Madame R\xc3\xa9al, living on the second floor, who acted as a diamond\r\nbroker and was frequently away from home. She had returned from a\r\njourney on the preceding evening. Yesterday, I called on her and, under\r\nan assumed name, I offered to act as an intermedium in the sale of some\r\ndiamonds to certain wealthy friends of mine. She is to meet me here\r\nto-day to carry out that arrangement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! You expect her to come here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, at half-past five.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure it is she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame de R\xc3\xa9al of the Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon? Certainly. I have convincing\r\nevidence of that fact. But ... listen!... I hear Folenfant\xe2\x80\x99s signal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a whistle. Ganimard arose quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no time to lose. Monsieur and Madame de Crozon, will you be\r\nkind enough to go into the next room. You also, Monsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, and\r\nyou, Monsieur Gerbois. The door will remain open, and when I give the\r\nsignal, you will come out. Of course, Chief, you will remain here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe may be disturbed by other people,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mon. Dudouis.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. This is a new establishment, and the proprietor is one of my\r\nfriends. He will not let anyone disturb us\xe2\x80\x94except the blonde Lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blonde Lady! What do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, the blonde Lady herself, chief; the friend and accomplice of\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, the mysterious blonde Lady against whom I hold convincing\r\nevidence; but, in addition to that, I wish to confront her with all the\r\npeople she has robbed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked through the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see her. She is coming in the door now. She can\xe2\x80\x99t escape: Folenfant\r\nand Dieuzy are guarding the door.... The blonde Lady is captured at\r\nlast, Chief!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA moment later a woman appeared at the door; she was tall and slender,\r\nwith a very pale complexion and bright golden hair. Ganimard trembled\r\nwith excitement; he could not move, nor utter a word. She was there, in\r\nfront of him, at his mercy! What a victory over Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin! And what\r\na revenge! And, at the same time, the victory was such an easy one that\r\nhe asked himself if the blonde Lady would not yet slip through his\r\nfingers by one of those miracles that usually terminated the exploits\r\nof Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. She remained standing near the door, surprised at the\r\nsilence, and looked about her without any display of suspicion or fear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will get away! She will disappear!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ganimard.\r\n\r\nThen he managed to get between her and the door. She turned to go out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you going away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, monsieur, I do not understand what this means. Allow me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no reason why you should go, madame, and very good reasons\r\nwhy you should remain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is useless, madame. You cannot go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTrembling, she sat on a chair, and stammered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard had won the battle and captured the blonde Lady. He said to\r\nher:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAllow me to present the friend I mentioned, who desires to purchase\r\nsome diamonds. Have you procured the stones you promised to bring?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94no\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I don\xe2\x80\x99t remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome! Jog your memory! A person of your acquaintance intended to send\r\nyou a tinted stone.... \xe2\x80\x98Something like the blue diamond,\xe2\x80\x99 I said,\r\nlaughing; and you replied: \xe2\x80\x98Exactly, I expect to have just what you\r\nwant.\xe2\x80\x99 Do you remember?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe made no reply. A small satchel fell from her hand. She picked it up\r\nquickly, and held it securely. Her hands trembled slightly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9cI see you have no confidence in us, Madame de\r\nR\xc3\xa9al. I shall set you a good example by showing you what I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took from his pocketbook a paper which he unfolded, and disclosed a\r\nlock of hair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese are a few hairs torn from the head of Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat by the\r\nBaron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, which I found clasped in his dead hand. I have shown\r\nthem to Mlle. Gerbois, who declares they are of the exact color of the\r\nhair of the blonde Lady. Besides, they are exactly the color of your\r\nhair\xe2\x80\x94the identical color.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame R\xc3\xa9al looked at him in bewilderment, as if she did not understand\r\nhis meaning. He continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd here are two perfume bottles, without labels, it is true, and\r\nempty, but still sufficiently impregnated with their odor to enable\r\nMlle. Gerbois to recognize in them the perfume used by that blonde Lady\r\nwho was her traveling companion for two weeks. Now, one of these\r\nbottles was found in the room that Madame de R\xc3\xa9al occupied at the\r\nCh\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon, and the other in the room that you occupied at the\r\nH\xc3\xb4tel Beaurivage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say?... The blonde Lady ... the Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe detective did not reply. He took from his pocket and placed on the\r\ntable, side by side, four small sheets of paper. Then he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have, on these four pieces of paper, various specimens of\r\nhandwriting; the first is the writing of Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat; the second\r\nwas written by the woman who sent the note to Baron Herschmann at the\r\nauction sale of the blue diamond; the third is that of Madame de R\xc3\xa9al,\r\nwritten while she was stopping at the Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon; and the fourth\r\nis your handwriting, madame ... it is your name and address, which you\r\ngave to the porter of the H\xc3\xb4tel Beaurivage at Trouville. Now, compare\r\nthe four handwritings. They are identical.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat absurdity is this? really, monsieur, I do not understand. What\r\ndoes it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt means, madame,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the blonde Lady, the\r\nfriend and accomplice of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, is none other than you, Madame\r\nR\xc3\xa9al.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard went to the adjoining room and returned with Mon. Gerbois,\r\nwhom he placed in front of Madame R\xc3\xa9al, as he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Gerbois, is this the person who abducted your daughter, the\r\nwoman you saw at the house of Monsieur Detinan?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard was so surprised that he could not speak for a moment;\r\nfinally, he said: \xe2\x80\x9cNo?... You must be mistaken....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not mistaken. Madame is blonde, it is true, and in that respect\r\nresembles the blonde Lady; but, in all other respects, she is totally\r\ndifferent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t believe it. You must be mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard called in his other witnesses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you recognize Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, this is not the person I saw at my uncle\xe2\x80\x99s house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis woman is not Madame de R\xc3\xa9al,\xe2\x80\x9d declared the Count de Crozon.\r\n\r\nThat was the finishing touch. Ganimard was crushed. He was buried\r\nbeneath the ruins of the structure he had erected with so much care and\r\nassurance. His pride was humbled, his spirit was broken, by the force\r\nof this unexpected blow.\r\n\r\nMon. Dudouis arose, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe owe you an apology, madame, for this unfortunate mistake. But,\r\nsince your arrival here, I have noticed your nervous agitation.\r\nSomething troubles you; may I ask what it is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMon Dieu, monsieur, I was afraid. My satchel contains diamonds to the\r\nvalue of a hundred thousand francs, and the conduct of your friend was\r\nrather suspicious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you were frequently absent from Paris. How do you explain that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI make frequent journeys to other cities in the course of my business.\r\nThat is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMon. Dudouis had nothing more to ask. He turned to his subordinate, and\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour investigation has been very superficial, Ganimard, and your\r\nconduct toward this lady is really deplorable. You will come to my\r\noffice to-morrow and explain it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe interview was at an end, and Mon. Dudouis was about to leave the\r\nroom when a most annoying incident occurred. Madame R\xc3\xa9al turned to\r\nGanimard, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand that you are Monsieur Ganimard. Am I right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, this letter must be for you. I received it this morning. It was\r\naddressed to \xe2\x80\x98Mon. Justin Ganimard, care of Madame R\xc3\xa9al.\xe2\x80\x99 I thought it\r\nwas a joke, because I did not know you under that name, but it appears\r\nthat your unknown correspondent knew of our rendezvous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard was inclined to put the letter in his pocket unread, but he\r\ndared not do so in the presence of his superior, so he opened the\r\nenvelope and read the letter aloud, in an almost inaudible tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce upon a time, there were a blonde Lady, a Lupin, and a Ganimard.\r\nNow, the wicked Ganimard had evil designs on the pretty blonde Lady,\r\nand the good Lupin was her friend and protector. When the good Lupin\r\nwished the blonde Lady to become the friend of the Countess de Crozon,\r\nhe caused her to assume the name of Madame de R\xc3\xa9al, which is a close\r\nresemblance to the name of a certain diamond broker, a woman with a\r\npale complexion and golden hair. And the good Lupin said to himself: If\r\never the wicked Ganimard gets upon the track of the blonde Lady, how\r\nuseful it will be to me if he should be diverted to the track of the\r\nhonest diamond broker. A wise precaution that has borne good fruit. A\r\nlittle note sent to the newspaper read by the wicked Ganimard, a\r\nperfume bottle intentionally forgotten by the genuine blonde Lady at\r\nthe H\xc3\xb4tel Beaurivage, the name and address of Madame R\xc3\xa9al written on\r\nthe hotel register by the genuine blonde Lady, and the trick is played.\r\nWhat do you think of it, Ganimard? I wished to tell you the true story\r\nof this affair, knowing that you would be the first to laugh over it.\r\nReally, it is quite amusing, and I have enjoyed it very much.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cAccept my best wishes, dear friend, and give my kind regards to\r\n    the worthy Mon. Dudouis.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe knows everything,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Ganimard, but he did not see the humor\r\nof the situation as Lupin had predicted. \xe2\x80\x9cHe knows some things I have\r\nnever mentioned to any one. How could he find out that I was going to\r\ninvite you here, chief? How could he know that I had found the first\r\nperfume bottle? How could he find out those things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stamped his feet and tore his hair\xe2\x80\x94a prey to the most tragic\r\ndespair. Mon. Dudouis felt sorry for him, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Ganimard, never mind; try to do better next time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Mon. Dudouis left the room, accompanied by Madame R\xc3\xa9al.\r\n\r\n\r\nDuring the next ten minutes, Ganimard read and re-read the letter of\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. Monsieur and Madame de Crozon, Monsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec and\r\nMonsieur Gerbois were holding an animated discussion in a corner of the\r\nroom. At last, the count approached the detective, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear monsieur, after your investigation, we are no nearer the truth\r\nthan we were before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me, but my investigation has established these facts: that the\r\nblonde Lady is the mysterious heroine of these exploits, and that\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin directed them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose facts do not solve the mystery; in fact, they render it more\r\nobscure. The blonde Lady commits a murder in order to steal the blue\r\ndiamond, and yet she does not steal it. Afterward she steals it and\r\ngets rid of it by secretly giving it to another person. How do you\r\nexplain her strange conduct?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot explain it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course; but, perhaps, someone else can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Count hesitated, so the Countess replied, frankly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is only one man besides yourself who is competent to enter the\r\narena with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin and overcome him. Have you any objection to our\r\nengaging the services of Herlock Sholmes in this case?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard was vexed at the question, but stammered a reply:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo ... but ... I do not understand what\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me explain. All this mystery annoys me. I wish to have it cleared\r\nup. Monsieur Gerbois and Monsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec have the same desire, and\r\nwe have agreed to send for the celebrated English detective.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right, madame,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the detective, with a loyalty that did\r\nhim credit, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are right. Old Ganimard is not able to overcome Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin. But will Herlock Sholmes succeed? I hope so, as I have the\r\ngreatest admiration for him. But ... it is improbable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say that he will not succeed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my opinion. I can foresee the result of a duel between Herlock\r\nSholmes and Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. The Englishman will be defeated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, in any event, can we count on your assistance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so, madame. I shall be pleased to render Monsieur Sholmes all\r\npossible assistance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know his address?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; 219 Parker street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat evening Monsieur and Madame de Crozon withdrew the charge they had\r\nmade against Herr Bleichen, and a joint letter was addressed to Herlock\r\nSholmes.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III.\r\nHERLOCK SHOLMES OPENS HOSTILITIES.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does monsieur wish?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, like a man who never worries over the\r\ndetails of a meal; \xe2\x80\x9canything you like, but no meat or alcohol.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe waiter walked away, disdainfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! still a vegetarian?\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore so than ever,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThrough taste, faith, or habit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHygiene.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you never fall from grace?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! yes ... when I am dining out ... and wish to avoid being\r\nconsidered eccentric.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were dining near the Northern Railway station, in a little\r\nrestaurant to which Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had invited me. Frequently he would\r\nsend me a telegram asking me to meet him in some obscure restaurant,\r\nwhere we could enjoy a quiet dinner, well served, and which was always\r\nmade interesting to me by his recital of some startling adventure\r\ntheretofore unknown to me.\r\n\r\nOn that particular evening he appeared to be in a more lively mood than\r\nusual. He laughed and joked with careless animation, and with that\r\ndelicate sarcasm that was habitual with him\xe2\x80\x94a light and spontaneous\r\nsarcasm that was quite free from any tinge of malice. It was a pleasure\r\nto find him in that jovial mood, and I could not resist the desire to\r\ntell him so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! yes,\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cthere are days in which I find life as bright\r\nand gay as a spring morning; then life seems to be an infinite treasure\r\nwhich I can never exhaust. And yet God knows I lead a careless\r\nexistence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo much so, perhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! but I tell you, the treasure is infinite. I can spend it with a\r\nlavish hand. I can cast my youth and strength to the four winds of\r\nHeaven, and it is replaced by a still younger and greater force.\r\nBesides, my life is so pleasant!... If I wished to do so, I might\r\nbecome\xe2\x80\x94what shall I say?... An orator, a manufacturer, a politician....\r\nBut, I assure you, I shall never have such a desire. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, I\r\nam; Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, I shall remain. I have made a vain search in history\r\nto find a career comparable to mine; a life better filled or more\r\nintense.... Napoleon? Yes, perhaps.... But Napoleon, toward the close\r\nof his career, when all Europe was trying to crush him, asked himself\r\non the eve of each battle if it would not be his last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWas he serious? Or was he joking? He became more animated as he\r\nproceeded:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is everything, do you understand, the danger! The continuous\r\nfeeling of danger! To breathe it as you breathe the air, to scent it in\r\nevery breath of wind, to detect it in every unusual sound.... And, in\r\nthe midst of the tempest, to remain calm ... and not to stumble!\r\nOtherwise, you are lost. There is only one sensation equal to it: that\r\nof the chauffeur in an automobile race. But that race lasts only a few\r\nhours; my race continues until death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat fantasy!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you wish me to believe that you have\r\nno particular motive for your adoption of that exciting life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a smile, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are a clever psychologist. Work it\r\nout for yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe poured himself a glass of water, drank it, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you read _\xe2\x80\x98Le Temps\xe2\x80\x99_ to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHerlock Sholmes crossed the Channel this afternoon, and arrived in\r\nParis about six o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce! What is he coming for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little journey he has undertaken at the request of the Count and\r\nCountess of Crozon, Monsieur Gerbois, and the nephew of Baron\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec. They met him at the Northern Railway station, took him to\r\nmeet Ganimard, and, at this moment, the six of them are holding a\r\nconsultation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDespite a strong temptation to do so, I had never ventured to question\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin concerning any action of his private life, unless he had\r\nfirst mentioned the subject to me. Up to that moment his name had not\r\nbeen mentioned, at least officially, in connection with the blue\r\ndiamond. Consequently, I consumed my curiosity in patience. He\r\ncontinued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is also in _\xe2\x80\x98Le Temps\xe2\x80\x99_ an interview with my old friend\r\nGanimard, according to whom a certain blonde lady, who should be my\r\nfriend, must have murdered the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec and tried to rob Madame\r\nde Crozon of her famous ring. And\xe2\x80\x94what do you think?\xe2\x80\x94he accuses me of\r\nbeing the instigator of those crimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could not suppress a slight shudder. Was this true? Must I believe\r\nthat his career of theft, his mode of existence, the logical result of\r\nsuch a life, had drawn that man into more serious crimes, including\r\nmurder? I looked at him. He was so calm, and his eyes had such a frank\r\nexpression! I observed his hands: they had been formed from a model of\r\nexceeding delicacy, long and slender; inoffensive, truly; and the hands\r\nof an artist....\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGanimard has pipe-dreams,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d protested Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cGanimard has some cleverness; and, at\r\ntimes, almost inspiration.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInspiration!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. For instance, that interview is a master-stroke. In the first\r\nplace, he announces the coming of his English rival in order to put me\r\non my guard, and make his task more difficult. In the second place, he\r\nindicates the exact point to which he has conducted the affair in order\r\nthat Sholmes will not get credit for the work already done by Ganimard.\r\nThat is good warfare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever it may be, you have two adversaries to deal with, and such\r\nadversaries!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! one of them doesn\xe2\x80\x99t count.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the other?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSholmes? Oh! I confess he is a worthy foe; and that explains my\r\npresent good humor. In the first place, it is a question of\r\nself-esteem; I am pleased to know that they consider me a subject\r\nworthy the attention of the celebrated English detective. In the next\r\nplace, just imagine the pleasure a man, such as I, must experience in\r\nthe thought of a duel with Herlock Sholmes. But I shall be obliged to\r\nstrain every muscle; he is a clever fellow, and will contest every inch\r\nof the ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you consider him a strong opponent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do. As a detective, I believe, he has never had an equal. But I have\r\none advantage over him; he is making the attack and I am simply\r\ndefending myself. My r\xc3\xb4le is the easier one. Besides, I am familiar\r\nwith his method of warfare, and he does not know mine. I am prepared to\r\nshow him a few new tricks that will give him something to think about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe tapped the table with his fingers as he uttered the following\r\nsentences, with an air of keen delight:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin against Herlock Sholmes.... France against England....\r\nTrafalgar will be revenged at last.... Ah! the rascal ... he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsuspect that I am prepared ... and a Lupin warned\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped suddenly, seized with a fit of coughing, and hid his face in\r\nhis napkin, as if something had stuck in his throat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA bit of bread?\xe2\x80\x9d I inquired. \xe2\x80\x9cDrink some water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t that,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, in a stifled voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe want of air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you wish a window opened?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I shall go out. Give me my hat and overcoat, quick! I must go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe two gentlemen who came in just now.... Look at the taller one ...\r\nnow, when we go out, keep to my left, so he will not see me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one who is sitting behind you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I will explain it to you, outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHerlock Sholmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe made a desperate effort to control himself, as if he were ashamed of\r\nhis emotion, replaced his napkin, drank a glass of water, and, quite\r\nrecovered, said to me, smiling:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is strange, hein, that I should be affected so easily, but that\r\nunexpected sight\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you to fear, since no one can recognize you, on account of\r\nyour many transformations? Every time I see you it seems to me your\r\nface is changed; it\xe2\x80\x99s not at all familiar. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know why.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _he_ would recognize me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has seen me only once;\r\nbut, at that time, he made a mental photograph of me\xe2\x80\x94not of my external\r\nappearance but of my very soul\xe2\x80\x94not what I appear to be but just what I\r\nam. Do you understand? And then ... and then.... I did not expect to\r\nmeet him here.... Such a strange encounter!... in this little\r\nrestaurant....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, shall we go out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe better way is to act frankly ... to have confidence in him\xe2\x80\x94trust\r\nhim....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not speak to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not? It will be to my advantage to do so, and find out what he\r\nknows, and, perhaps, what he thinks. At present I have the feeling that\r\nhis gaze is on my neck and shoulders, and that he is trying to remember\r\nwhere he has seen them before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe reflected a moment. I observed a malicious smile at the corner of\r\nhis mouth; then, obedient, I think, to a whim of his impulsive nature,\r\nand not to the necessities of the situation, he arose, turned around,\r\nand, with a bow and a joyous air, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy what lucky chance? Ah! I am delighted to see you. Permit me to\r\nintroduce a friend of mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment the Englishman was disconcerted; then he made a movement\r\nas if he would seize Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. The latter shook his head, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would not be fair; besides, the movement would be an awkward one\r\nand ... quite useless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman looked about him, as if in search of assistance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo use,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cBesides, are you quite sure you can place your\r\nhand on me? Come, now, show me that you are a real Englishman and,\r\ntherefore, a good sport.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis advice seemed to commend itself to the detective, for he partially\r\nrose and said, very formally:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Wilson, my friend and assistant\xe2\x80\x94Monsieur Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson\xe2\x80\x99s amazement evoked a laugh. With bulging eyes and gaping mouth,\r\nhe looked from one to the other, as if unable to comprehend the\r\nsituation. Herlock Sholmes laughed and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, you should conceal your astonishment at an incident which is\r\none of the most natural in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you not arrest him?\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you not observed, Wilson, that the gentleman is between me and\r\nthe door, and only a few steps from the door. By the time I could move\r\nmy little finger he would be outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let that make any difference,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, who now walked around\r\nthe table and seated himself so that the Englishman was between him and\r\nthe door\xe2\x80\x94thus placing himself at the mercy of the foreigner.\r\n\r\nWilson looked at Sholmes to find out if he had the right to admire this\r\nact of wanton courage. The Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s face was impenetrable; but, a\r\nmoment later, he called:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWaiter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the waiter came he ordered soda, beer and whisky. The treaty of\r\npeace was signed\xe2\x80\x94until further orders. In a few moments the four men\r\nwere conversing in an apparently friendly manner.\r\n\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes is a man such as you might meet every day in the\r\nbusiness world. He is about fifty years of age, and looks as if he\r\nmight have passed his life in an office, adding up columns of dull\r\nfigures or writing out formal statements of business accounts. There\r\nwas nothing to distinguish him from the average citizen of London,\r\nexcept the appearance of his eyes, his terribly keen and penetrating\r\neyes.\r\n\r\nBut then he is Herlock Sholmes\xe2\x80\x94which means that he is a wonderful\r\ncombination of intuition, observation, clairvoyance and ingenuity. One\r\ncould readily believe that nature had been pleased to take the two most\r\nextraordinary detectives that the imagination of man has hitherto\r\nconceived, the Dupin of Edgar Allen Poe and the Lecoq of Emile\r\nGaboriau, and, out of that material, constructed a new detective, more\r\nextraordinary and supernatural than either of them. And when a person\r\nreads the history of his exploits, which have made him famous\r\nthroughout the entire world, he asks himself whether Herlock Sholmes is\r\nnot a mythical personage, a fictitious hero born in the brain of a\r\ngreat novelist\xe2\x80\x94Conan Doyle, for instance.\r\n\r\nWhen Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin questioned him in regard to the length of his sojourn\r\nin France he turned the conversation into its proper channel by saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat depends on you, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lupin, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cif it depends on me you can return to\r\nEngland to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a little too soon, but I expect to return in the course of\r\neight or nine days\xe2\x80\x94ten at the outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you in such a hurry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have many cases to attend to; such as the robbery of the\r\nAnglo-Chinese Bank, the abduction of Lady Eccleston.... But, don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nthink, Monsieur Lupin, that I can finish my business in Paris within a\r\nweek?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, if you confine your efforts to the case of the blue\r\ndiamond. It is, moreover, the length of time that I require to make\r\npreparations for my safety in case the solution of that affair should\r\ngive you certain dangerous advantages over me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Englishman, \xe2\x80\x9cI expect to close the business in\r\neight or ten days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd arrest me on the eleventh, perhaps?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, the tenth is my limit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin shook his head thoughtfully, as he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will be difficult\xe2\x80\x94very difficult.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDifficult, perhaps, but possible, therefore certain\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely certain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, as if he had clearly worked out the\r\nlong series of operations which would conduct his collaborator to the\r\ndesired result.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Herlock Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cI do not hold all the trump cards,\r\nas these cases are already several months old, and I lack certain\r\ninformation and clues upon which I am accustomed to base my\r\ninvestigations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch as spots of mud and cigarette ashes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, with an air of\r\nimportance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn addition to the remarkable conclusions formed by Monsieur Ganimard,\r\nI have obtained all the articles written on the subject, and have\r\nformed a few deductions of my own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome ideas which were suggested to us by analysis or hypothesis,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nadded Wilson, sententiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to enquire,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, in that deferential tone which\r\nhe employed in speaking to Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cwould I be indiscreet if I were to\r\nask you what opinion you have formed about the case?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nReally, it was a most exciting situation to see those two men facing\r\neach other across the table, engaged in an earnest discussion as if\r\nthey were obliged to solve some abstruse problem or come to an\r\nagreement upon some controverted fact. Wilson was in the seventh heaven\r\nof delight. Herlock Sholmes filled his pipe slowly, lighted it, and\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis affair is much simpler than it appeared to be at first sight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch simpler,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, as a faithful echo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say \xe2\x80\x98this affair,\xe2\x80\x99 for, in my opinion, there is only one,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nSholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cThe death of the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, the story of the ring, and,\r\nlet us not forget, the mystery of lottery ticket number 514, are only\r\ndifferent phases of what one might call the mystery of the blonde Lady.\r\nNow, according to my view, it is simply a question of discovering the\r\nbond that unites those three episodes in the same story\xe2\x80\x94the fact which\r\nproves the unity of the three events. Ganimard, whose judgment is\r\nrather superficial, finds that unity in the faculty of disappearance;\r\nthat is, in the power of coming and going unseen and unheard. That\r\ntheory does not satisfy me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what is your idea?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn my opinion,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cthe characteristic feature of the three\r\nepisodes is your design and purpose of leading the affair into a\r\ncertain channel previously chosen by you. It is, on your part, more\r\nthan a plan; it is a necessity, an indispensable condition of success.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you furnish any details of your theory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. For example, from the beginning of your conflict with\r\nMonsieur Gerbois, is it not evident that the apartment of Monsieur\r\nDetinan is the place selected by you, the inevitable spot where all the\r\nparties must meet? In your opinion, it was the only safe place, and you\r\narranged a rendezvous there, publicly, one might say, for the blonde\r\nLady and Mademoiselle Gerbois.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe professor\xe2\x80\x99s daughter,\xe2\x80\x9d added Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, let us consider the case of the blue diamond. Did you try to\r\nappropriate it while the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec possessed it? No. But the\r\nbaron takes his brother\xe2\x80\x99s house. Six months later we have the\r\nintervention of Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat and the first attempt. The diamond\r\nescapes you, and the sale is widely advertised to take place at the\r\nDrouot auction-rooms. Will it be a free and open sale? Is the richest\r\namateur sure to carry off the jewel? No. Just as the banker Herschmann\r\nis on the point of buying the ring, a lady sends him a letter of\r\nwarning, and it is the Countess de Crozon, prepared and influenced by\r\nthe same lady, who becomes the purchaser of the diamond. Will the ring\r\ndisappear at once? No; you lack the opportunity. Therefore, you must\r\nwait. At last the Countess goes to her ch\xc3\xa2teau. That is what you were\r\nwaiting for. The ring disappears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo reappear again in the tooth-powder of Herr Bleichen,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked\r\nLupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! such nonsense!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, striking the table with his\r\nfist, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t tell me such a fairy tale. I am too old a fox to be led\r\naway by a false scent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do I mean?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, then paused a moment as if he wished to\r\narrange his effect. At last he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blue diamond that was found in the tooth-powder was false. You\r\nkept the genuine stone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin remained silent for a moment; then, with his eyes fixed on\r\nthe Englishman, he replied, calmly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are impertinent, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpertinent, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Wilson, beaming with admiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, \xe2\x80\x9cand, yet, to do you credit, you have thrown a\r\nstrong light on a very mysterious subject. Not a magistrate, not a\r\nspecial reporter, who has been engaged on this case, has come so near\r\nthe truth. It is a marvellous display of intuition and logic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! a person has simply to use his brains,\xe2\x80\x9d said Herlock Sholmes,\r\nnattered at the homage of the expert criminal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so few have any brains to use,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, now, that the\r\nfield of conjectures has been narrowed down, and the rubbish cleared\r\naway\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now, I have simply to discover why the three episodes were\r\nenacted at 25 rue Clapeyron, 134 avenue Henri-Martin, and within the\r\nwalls of the Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon and my work will be finished. What\r\nremains will be child\xe2\x80\x99s play. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think you are right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case, Monsieur Lupin, am I wrong in saying that my business\r\nwill be finished in ten days?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn ten days you will know the whole truth,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will be arrested.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn order that I may be arrested there must occur such a series of\r\nimprobable and unexpected misfortunes that I cannot admit the\r\npossibility of such an event.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have a saying in England that \xe2\x80\x98the unexpected always happens.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey looked at each other for a moment calmly and fearlessly, without\r\nany display of bravado or malice. They met as equals in a contest of\r\nwit and skill. And this meeting was the formal crossing of swords,\r\npreliminary to the duel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lupin, \xe2\x80\x9cat last I shall have an adversary worthy of the\r\nname\xe2\x80\x94one whose defeat will be the proudest achievement in my career.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you not afraid!\xe2\x80\x9d asked Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlmost, Monsieur Wilson,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Lupin, rising from his chair, \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nthe proof is that I am about to make a hasty retreat. Then, we will say\r\nten days, Monsieur Sholmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, ten days. This is Sunday. A week from next Wednesday, at eight\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock in the evening, it will be all over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I shall be in prison?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa! not a pleasant outlook for a man who gets so much enjoyment out of\r\nlife as I do. No cares, a lively interest in the affairs of the world,\r\na justifiable contempt for the police, and the consoling sympathy of\r\nnumerous friends and admirers. And now, behold, all that is about to be\r\nchanged! It is the reverse side of the medal. After sunshine comes the\r\nrain. It is no longer a laughing matter. Adieu!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurry up!\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, full of solicitude for a person in whom\r\nHerlock Sholmes had inspired so much respect, \xe2\x80\x9cdo not lose a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a minute, Monsieur Wilson; but I wish to express my pleasure at\r\nhaving met you, and to tell you how much I envy the master in having\r\nsuch a valuable assistant as you seem to be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, after they had courteously saluted each other, like adversaries\r\nin a duel who entertain no feeling of malice but are obliged to fight\r\nby force of circumstances, Lupin seized me by the arm and drew me\r\noutside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of it, dear boy? The strange events of this evening\r\nwill form an interesting chapter in the memoirs you are now preparing\r\nfor me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe closed the door of the restaurant behind us, and, after taking a few\r\nsteps, he stopped and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you smoke?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Nor do you, it seems to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right, I don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe lighted a cigarette with a wax-match, which he shook several times\r\nin an effort to extinguish it. But he threw away the cigarette\r\nimmediately, ran across the street, and joined two men who emerged from\r\nthe shadows as if called by a signal. He conversed with them for a few\r\nminutes on the opposite sidewalk, and then returned to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, but I fear that cursed Sholmes is going to give me\r\ntrouble. But, I assure you, he is not yet through with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. He\r\nwill find out what kind of fuel I use to warm my blood. And now\xe2\x80\x94au\r\nrevoir! The genial Wilson is right; there is not a moment to lose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe walked away rapidly.\r\n\r\nThus ended the events of that exciting evening, or, at least, that part\r\nof them in which I was a participant. Subsequently, during the course\r\nof the evening, other stirring incidents occurred which have come to my\r\nknowledge through the courtesy of other members of that unique\r\ndinner-party.\r\n\r\n\r\nAt the very moment in which Lupin left me, Herlock Sholmes rose from\r\nthe table, and looked at his watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwenty minutes to nine. At nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock I am to meet the Count and\r\nCountess at the railway station.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, we must be off!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Wilson, between two drinks of whisky.\r\n\r\nThey left the restaurant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, don\xe2\x80\x99t look behind. We may be followed, and, in that case, let\r\nus act as if we did not care. Wilson, I want your opinion: why was\r\nLupin in that restaurant?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo get something to eat,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Wilson, quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, I must congratulate you on the accuracy of your deduction. I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have done better myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson blushed with pleasure, and Sholmes continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo get something to eat. Very well, and, after that, probably, to\r\nassure himself whether I am going to the Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon, as\r\nannounced by Ganimard in his interview. I must go in order not to\r\ndisappoint him. But, in order to gain time on him, I shall not go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, nonplused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, my friend, will walk down this street, take a carriage, two,\r\nthree carriages. Return later and get the valises that we left at the\r\nstation, and make for the Elys\xc3\xa9e-Palace at a galop.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when I reach the Elys\xc3\xa9e-Palace?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEngage a room, go to sleep, and await my orders.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nQuite proud of the important r\xc3\xb4le assigned to him, Wilson set out to\r\nperform his task. Herlock Sholmes proceeded to the railway station,\r\nbought a ticket, and repaired to the Amiens\xe2\x80\x99 express in which the Count\r\nand Countess de Crozon were already installed. He bowed to them,\r\nlighted his pipe, and had a quiet smoke in the corridor. The train\r\nstarted. Ten minutes later he took a seat beside the Countess, and said\r\nto her:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you the ring here, madame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you kindly let me see it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took it, and examined it closely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust as I suspected: it is a manufactured diamond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA manufactured diamond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; a new process which consists in submitting diamond dust to a\r\ntremendous heat until it melts and is then molded into a single stone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my diamond is genuine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, _your_ diamond is; but this is not yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is mine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is held by Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this stone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas substituted for yours, and slipped into Herr Bleichen\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntooth-powder, where it was afterwards found.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you think this is false?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely false.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Countess was overwhelmed with surprise and grief, while her husband\r\nscrutinized the diamond with an incredulous air. Finally she stammered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible? And why did they not merely steal it and be done with\r\nit? And how did they steal it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is exactly what I am going to find out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the Ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I shall leave the train at Creil and return to Paris. It is there\r\nthe game between me and Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin must be played. In fact, the game\r\nhas commenced already, and Lupin thinks I am on my way to the ch\xc3\xa2teau.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does it matter to you, madame? The essential thing is your\r\ndiamond, is it not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, don\xe2\x80\x99t worry. I have just undertaken a much more difficult task\r\nthan that. You have my promise that I will restore the true diamond to\r\nyou within ten days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe train slackened its speed. He put the false diamond in his pocket\r\nand opened the door. The Count cried out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is the wrong side of the train. You are getting out on the\r\ntracks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is my intention. If Lupin has anyone on my track, he will lose\r\nsight of me now. Adieu.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn employee protested in vain. After the departure of the train, the\r\nEnglishman sought the station-master\xe2\x80\x99s office. Forty minutes later he\r\nleaped into a train that landed him in Paris shortly before midnight.\r\nHe ran across the platform, entered the lunch-room, made his exit at\r\nanother door, and jumped into a cab.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDriver\xe2\x80\x94rue Clapeyron.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHaving reached the conclusion that he was not followed, he stopped the\r\ncarriage at the end of the street, and proceeded to make a careful\r\nexamination of Monsieur Detinan\xe2\x80\x99s house and the two adjoining houses.\r\nHe made measurements of certain distances and entered the figures in\r\nhis notebook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDriver\xe2\x80\x94avenue Henri-Martin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the corner of the avenue and the rue de la Pompe, he dismissed the\r\ncarriage, walked down the street to number 134, and performed the same\r\noperations in front of the house of the late Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec and the\r\ntwo adjoining houses, measuring the width of the respective fa\xc3\xa7ades and\r\ncalculating the depth of the little gardens that stood in front of\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nThe avenue was deserted, and was very dark under its four rows of\r\ntrees, between which, at considerable intervals, a few gas-lamps\r\nstruggled in vain to light the deep shadows. One of them threw a dim\r\nlight over a portion of the house, and Sholmes perceived the \xe2\x80\x9cTo-let\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsign posted on the gate, the neglected walks which encircled the small\r\nlawn, and the large bare windows of the vacant house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cthe house has been unoccupied since\r\nthe death of the baron.... Ah! if I could only get in and view the\r\nscene of the murder!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo sooner did the idea occur to him than he sought to put it in\r\nexecution. But how could he manage it? He could not climb over the\r\ngate; it was too high. So he took from his pocket an electric lantern\r\nand a skeleton key which he always carried. Then, to his great\r\nsurprise, he discovered that the gate was not locked; in fact, it was\r\nopen about three or four inches. He entered the garden, and was careful\r\nto leave the gate as he had found it\xe2\x80\x94partly open. But he had not taken\r\nmany steps from the gate when he stopped. He had seen a light pass one\r\nof the windows on the second floor.\r\n\r\nHe saw the light pass a second window and a third, but he saw nothing\r\nelse, except a silhouette outlined on the walls of the rooms. The light\r\ndescended to the first floor, and, for a long time, wandered from room\r\nto room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho the deuce is walking, at one o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning, through the\r\nhouse in which the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec was killed?\xe2\x80\x9d Herlock Sholmes asked\r\nhimself, deeply interested.\r\n\r\nThere was only one way to find out, and that was to enter the house\r\nhimself. He did not hesitate, but started for the door of the house.\r\nHowever, at the moment when he crossed the streak of gaslight that came\r\nfrom the street-lamp, the man must have seen him, for the light in the\r\nhouse was suddenly extinguished and Herlock Sholmes did not see it\r\nagain. Softly, he tried the door. It was open, also. Hearing no sound,\r\nhe advanced through the hallway, encountered the foot of the stairs,\r\nand ascended to the first floor. Here there was the same silence, the\r\nsame darkness.\r\n\r\nHe entered, one of the rooms and approached a window through which came\r\na feeble light from the outside. On looking through the window he saw\r\nthe man, who had no doubt descended by another stairway and escaped by\r\nanother door. The man was threading his way through the shrubbery which\r\nbordered the wall that separated the two gardens.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9che is going to escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe hastened down the stairs and leaped over the steps in his eagerness\r\nto cut off the man\xe2\x80\x99s retreat. But he did not see anyone, and, owing to\r\nthe darkness, it was several seconds before he was able to distinguish\r\na bulky form moving through the shrubbery. This gave the Englishman\r\nfood for reflection. Why had the man not made his escape, which he\r\ncould have done so easily? Had he remained in order to watch the\r\nmovements of the intruder who had disturbed him in his mysterious work?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt all events,\xe2\x80\x9d concluded Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not Lupin; he would be more\r\nadroit. It may be one of his men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor several minutes Herlock Sholmes remained motionless, with his gaze\r\nfixed on the adversary who, in his turn was watching the detective. But\r\nas that adversary had become passive, and as the Englishman was not one\r\nto consume his time in idle waiting, he examined his revolver to see if\r\nit was in good working order, remove his knife from its sheath, and\r\nwalked toward the enemy with that cool effrontery and scorn of danger\r\nfor which he had become famous.\r\n\r\nHe heard a clicking sound; it was his adversary preparing his revolver.\r\nHerlock Sholmes dashed boldly into the thicket, and grappled with his\r\nfoe. There was a sharp, desperate struggle, in the course of which\r\nSholmes suspected that the man was trying to draw a knife. But the\r\nEnglishman, believing his antagonist to be an accomplice of Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin and anxious to win the first trick in the game with that\r\nredoubtable foe, fought with unusual strength and determination. He\r\nhurled his adversary to the ground, held him there with the weight of\r\nhis body, and, gripping him by the throat with one hand, he used his\r\nfree hand to take out his electric lantern, press the button, and throw\r\nthe light over the face of his prisoner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, in amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHerlock Sholmes!\xe2\x80\x9d stammered a weak, stifled voice.\r\n\r\n\r\nFor a long time they remained silent, astounded, foolish. The shriek of\r\nan automobile rent the air. A slight breeze stirred the leaves.\r\nSuddenly, Herlock Sholmes seized his friend by the shoulders and shook\r\nhim violently, as he cried:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing here? Tell me.... What?... Did I tell you to hide\r\nin the bushes and spy on me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpy on you!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Wilson, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know it was you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what are you doing here? You ought to be in bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was in bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to be asleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was asleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what brought you here?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy letter? I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, a messenger brought it to me at the hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom me? Are you crazy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true\xe2\x80\x94I swear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson handed him a sheet of paper, which he read by the light of his\r\nlantern. It was as follows:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, come at once to avenue Henri-Martin. The house is empty.\r\nInspect the whole place and make an exact plan. Then return to\r\nhotel.\xe2\x80\x94Herlock Sholmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was measuring the rooms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I saw a shadow in the\r\ngarden. I had only one idea\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was to seize the shadow.... The idea was excellent.... But\r\nremember this, Wilson, whenever you receive a letter from me, be sure\r\nit is my handwriting and not a forgery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Wilson, as the truth dawned on him, \xe2\x80\x9cthen the letter\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t from you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho sent it, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? For what purpose?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, and that\xe2\x80\x99s what worries me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand why he\r\ntook the trouble to disturb you. Of course, if he had sent me on such a\r\nfoolish errand I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be surprised; but what was his object in\r\ndisturbing you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must hurry back to the hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo must I, Wilson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey arrived at the gate. Wilson, who was ahead, took hold of it and\r\npulled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you closed it?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I left it partly open.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes tried the gate; then, alarmed, he examined the lock. An oath\r\nescaped him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood God! it is locked! locked with a key!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shook the gate with all his strength; then, realizing the futility\r\nof his efforts, he dropped his arms, discouraged, and muttered, in a\r\njerky manner:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can see it all now\xe2\x80\x94it is Lupin. He foresaw that I would leave the\r\ntrain at Creil, and he prepared this neat little trap for me in case I\r\nshould commence my investigation this evening. Moreover, he was kind\r\nenough to send me a companion to share my captivity. All done to make\r\nme lose a day, and, perhaps, also, to teach me to mind my own\r\nbusiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say we are prisoners?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. Herlock Sholmes and Wilson are the prisoners of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s a bad beginning; but he laughs best who laughs last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson seized Sholmes\xe2\x80\x99 arm, and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook!... Look up there!... A light....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA light shone through one of the windows of the first floor. Both of\r\nthem ran to the house, and each ascended by the stairs he had used on\r\ncoming out a short time before, and they met again at the entrance to\r\nthe lighted chamber. A small piece of a candle was burning in the\r\ncenter of the room. Beside it there was a basket containing a bottle, a\r\nroasted chicken, and a loaf of bread.\r\n\r\nSholmes was greatly amused, and laughed heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWonderful! we are invited to supper. It is really an enchanted place,\r\na genuine fairy-land. Come, Wilson, cheer up! this is not a funeral.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s all very funny.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you quite sure it is so very funny?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Wilson, in a lugubrious\r\ntone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I sure?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, with a gaiety that was too boisterous\r\nto be natural, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy, to tell the truth, it\xe2\x80\x99s the funniest thing I ever\r\nsaw. It\xe2\x80\x99s a jolly good comedy! What a master of sarcasm this Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin is! He makes a fool of you with the utmost grace and delicacy. I\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t miss this feast for all the money in the Bank of England.\r\nCome, Wilson, you grieve me. You should display that nobility of\r\ncharacter which rises superior to misfortune. I don\xe2\x80\x99t see that you have\r\nany cause for complaint, really, I don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a time, by dint of good humor and sarcasm, he managed to restore\r\nWilson to his normal mood, and make him swallow a morsel of chicken and\r\na glass of wine. But when the candle went out and they prepared to\r\nspend the night there, with the bare floor for a mattress and the hard\r\nwall for a pillow, the harsh and ridiculous side of the situation was\r\nimpressed upon them. That particular incident will not form a pleasant\r\npage in the memoirs of the famous detective.\r\n\r\nNext morning Wilson awoke, stiff and cold. A slight noise attracted his\r\nattention: Herlock Sholmes was kneeling on the floor, critically\r\nexamining some grains of sand and studying some chalk-marks, now almost\r\neffaced, which formed certain figures and numbers, which figures he\r\nentered in his notebook.\r\n\r\nAccompanied by Wilson, who was deeply interested in the work, he\r\nexamined each room, and found similar chalk-marks in two other\r\napartments. He noticed, also, two circles on the oaken panels, an arrow\r\non a wainscot, and four figures on four steps of the stairs. At the end\r\nof an hour Wilson said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe figures are correct, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know; but, at all events, they mean something,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\r\nSholmes, who had forgotten the discomforts of the night in the joy\r\ncreated by his new discoveries.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is quite obvious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, \xe2\x80\x9cthey represent the number of\r\npieces in the floor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. And the two circles indicate that the panels are false, as you\r\ncan readily ascertain, and the arrow points in the direction in which\r\nthe panels move.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes looked at Wilson, in astonishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! my dear friend, how do you know all that? Your clairvoyance makes\r\nmy poor ability in that direction look quite insignificant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! it is very simple,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, inflated with pride; \xe2\x80\x9cI examined\r\nthose marks last night, according to your instructions, or, rather,\r\naccording to the instructions of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, since he wrote the\r\nletter you sent to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment Wilson faced a greater danger than he had during his\r\nstruggle in the garden with Herlock Sholmes. The latter now felt a\r\nfurious desire to strangle him. But, dominating his feelings, Sholmes\r\nmade a grimace which was intended for a smile, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so, Wilson, you have done well, and your work shows commendable\r\nprogress. But, tell me, have you exercised your powers of observation\r\nand analysis on any other points? I might profit by your deductions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! no, I went no farther.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a pity. Your d\xc3\xa9but was such a promising one. But, since that is\r\nall, we may as well go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo! but how can we get out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe way all honest people go out: through the gate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is locked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will be opened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy whom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease call the two policemen who are strolling down the avenue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very humiliating. What will be said when it becomes known that\r\nHerlock Sholmes and Wilson were the prisoners of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I understand they will roar with laughter,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Herlock\r\nSholmes, in a dry voice and with frowning features, \xe2\x80\x9cbut we can\xe2\x80\x99t set\r\nup housekeeping in this place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will not try to find another way out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the man who brought us the basket of provisions did not cross the\r\ngarden, coming or going. There is some other way out. Let us look for\r\nit, and not bother with the police.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour argument is sound, but you forget that all the detectives in\r\nParis have been trying to find it for the last six months, and that I\r\nsearched the house from top to bottom while you were asleep. Ah! my\r\ndear Wilson, we have not been accustomed to pursue such game as Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin. He leaves no trail behind him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nAt eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Herlock Sholmes and Wilson were liberated, and\r\nconducted to the nearest police station, where the commissary, after\r\nsubjecting them to a severe examination, released them with an\r\naffectation of good-will that was quite exasperating.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry, messieurs, that this unfortunate incident has\r\noccurred. You will have a very poor opinion of French hospitality. Mon\r\nDieu! what a night you must have passed! Ah! that rascally Lupin is no\r\nrespecter of persons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey took a carriage to their hotel. At the office Wilson asked for the\r\nkey of his room.\r\n\r\nAfter some search the clerk replied, much astonished:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, monsieur, you have given up the room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI gave it up? When?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis morning, by the letter your friend brought here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe gentleman who brought your letter.... Ah! your card is still\r\nattached to the letter. Here they are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson looked at them. Certainly, it was one of his cards, and the\r\nletter was in his handwriting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is another of his tricks,\xe2\x80\x9d and he\r\nadded, aloud: \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is my luggage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour friend took it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!... and you gave it to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly; on the strength of your letter and card.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course ... of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey left the hotel and walked, slowly and thoughtfully, through the\r\nChamps-Elys\xc3\xa9es. The avenue was bright and cheerful beneath a clear\r\nautumn sun; the air was mild and pleasant.\r\n\r\nAt Rond-Point, Herlock Sholmes lighted his pipe. Then Wilson spoke:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t understand you, Sholmes. You are so calm and unruffled. They\r\nplay with you as a cat plays with a mouse, and yet you do not say a\r\nword.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes stopped, as he replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, I was thinking of your card.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe point is this: here is a man who, in view of a possible struggle\r\nwith us, procures specimens of our handwriting, and who holds, in his\r\npossession, one or more of your cards. Now, have you considered how\r\nmuch precaution and skill those facts represent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Wilson, to overcome an enemy so well prepared and so thoroughly\r\nequipped requires the infinite shrewdness of ... of a Herlock Sholmes.\r\nAnd yet, as you have seen, Wilson, I have lost the first round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nAt six o\xe2\x80\x99clock the _Echo de France_ published the following article in\r\nits evening edition:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis morning Mon. Thenard, commissary of police in the sixteenth\r\ndistrict, released Herlock Sholmes and his friend Wilson, both of whom\r\nhad been locked in the house of the late Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, where they\r\nspent a very pleasant night\xe2\x80\x94thanks to the thoughtful care and attention\r\nof Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn addition to their other troubles, these gentlemen have been robbed\r\nof their valises, and, in consequence thereof, they have entered a\r\nformal complaint against Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, satisfied that he has given them a mild reproof, hopes\r\nthese gentlemen will not force him to resort to more stringent\r\nmeasures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBah!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Herlock Sholmes, crushing the paper in his hands,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat is only child\xe2\x80\x99s play! And that is the only criticism I have to\r\nmake of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin: he plays to the gallery. There is that much of\r\nthe fakir in him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!
