Sholmes, you are a wonderful man! You have such a command over\r\nyour temper. Nothing ever disturbs you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, nothing disturbs me,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sholmes, in a voice that trembled\r\nfrom rage; \xe2\x80\x9cbesides, what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of losing my temper?... I am quite\r\nconfident of the final result; I shall have the last word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV.\r\nLIGHT IN THE DARKNESS.\r\n\r\n\r\nHowever well-tempered a man\xe2\x80\x99s character may be\xe2\x80\x94and Herlock Sholmes is\r\none of those men over whom ill-fortune has little or no hold\xe2\x80\x94there are\r\ncircumstances wherein the most courageous combatant feels the necessity\r\nof marshaling his forces before risking the chances of a battle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall take a vacation to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what shall I do?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, Wilson\xe2\x80\x94let me see! You can buy some underwear and linen to\r\nreplenish our wardrobe, while I take a rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, Sholmes, I will watch while you sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson uttered these words with all the importance of a sentinel on\r\nguard at the outpost, and therefore exposed to the greatest danger. His\r\nchest was expanded; his muscles were tense. Assuming a shrewd look, he\r\nscrutinized, officially, the little room in which they had fixed their\r\nabode.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, Wilson, you can watch. I shall occupy myself in the\r\npreparation of a line of attack more appropriate to the methods of the\r\nenemy we are called upon to meet. Do you see, Wilson, we have been\r\ndeceived in this fellow Lupin. My opinion is that we must commence at\r\nthe very beginning of this affair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd even before that, if possible. But have we sufficient time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNine days, dear boy. That is five too many.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman spent the entire afternoon in smoking and sleeping. He\r\ndid not enter upon his new plan of attack until the following day. Then\r\nhe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, I am ready. Let us attack the enemy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLead on, Macduff!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Wilson, full of martial ardor. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish to\r\nfight in the front rank. Oh! have no fear. I shall do credit to my King\r\nand country, for I am an Englishman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the first place, Sholmes had three long and important interviews:\r\nWith Monsieur Detinan, whose rooms he examined with the greatest care\r\nand precision; with Suzanne Gerbois, whom he questioned in regard to\r\nthe blonde Lady; and with Sister Auguste, who had retired to the\r\nconvent of the Visitandines since the murder of Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec.\r\n\r\nAt each of these interviews Wilson had remained outside; and each time\r\nhe asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSatisfactory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was sure we were on the right track.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey paid a visit to the two houses adjoining that of the late Baron\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec in the avenue Henri-Martin; then they visited the rue\r\nClapeyron, and, while he was examining the front of number 25, Sholmes\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll these houses must be connected by secret passages, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfind them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor the first time in his life, Wilson doubted the omnipotence of his\r\nfamous associate. Why did he now talk so much and accomplish so little?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, in answer to Wilson\xe2\x80\x99s secret thought,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbecause, with this fellow Lupin, a person has to work in the dark,\r\nand, instead of deducting the truth from established facts, a man must\r\nextract it from his own brain, and afterward learn if it is supported\r\nby the facts in the case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what about the secret passages?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey must exist. But even though I should discover them, and thus\r\nlearn how Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin made his entrance to the lawyer\xe2\x80\x99s house and how\r\nthe blonde Lady escaped from the house of Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec after the\r\nmurder, what good would it do? How would it help me? Would it furnish\r\nme with a weapon of attack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us attack him just the same,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Wilson, who had scarcely\r\nuttered these words when he jumped back with a cry of alarm. Something\r\nhad fallen at their feet; it was a bag filled with sand which might\r\nhave caused them serious injury if it had struck them.\r\n\r\nSholmes looked up. Some men were working on a scaffolding attached to\r\nthe balcony at the fifth floor of the house. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were lucky; one step more, and that heavy bag would have fallen on\r\nour heads. I wonder if\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMoved by a sudden impulse, he rushed into the house, up the five\r\nflights of stairs, rang the bell, pushed his way into the apartment to\r\nthe great surprise and alarm of the servant who came to the door, and\r\nmade his way to the balcony in front of the house. But there was no one\r\nthere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are the workmen who were here a moment ago?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked the\r\nservant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have just gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich way did they go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the servants\xe2\x80\x99 stairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes leaned out of the window. He saw two men leaving the house,\r\ncarrying bicycles. They mounted them and quickly disappeared around the\r\ncorner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long have they been working on this scaffolding?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose men?... only since this morning. It\xe2\x80\x99s their first day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes returned to the street, and joined Wilson. Together they\r\nreturned to the hotel, and thus the second day ended in a mournful\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\nOn the following day their programme was almost similar. They sat\r\ntogether on a bench in the avenue Henri-Martin, much to Wilson\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndisgust, who did not find it amusing to spend long hours watching the\r\nhouse in which the tragedy had occurred.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you expect, Sholmes? That Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin will walk out of the\r\nhouse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat the blonde Lady will make her appearance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am looking for something to occur; some slight incident that will\r\nfurnish me with a clue to work on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if it does not occur?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I must, myself, create the spark that will set fire to the\r\npowder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA solitary incident\xe2\x80\x94and that of a disagreeable nature\xe2\x80\x94broke the\r\nmonotony of the forenoon.\r\n\r\nA gentleman was riding along the avenue when his horse suddenly turned\r\naside in such a manner that it ran against the bench on which they were\r\nsitting, and struck Sholmes a slight blow on the shoulder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9ca little more and I would have had a broken\r\nshoulder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe gentleman struggled with his horse. The Englishman drew his\r\nrevolver and pointed it; but Wilson seized his arm, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be foolish! What are you going to do? Kill the man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave me alone, Wilson! Let go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDuring the brief struggle between Sholmes and Wilson the stranger rode\r\naway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, you can shoot,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson, triumphantly, when the horseman was\r\nat some distance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, you\xe2\x80\x99re an idiot! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you understand that the man is an\r\naccomplice of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes was trembling from rage. Wilson stammered pitifully:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!... that man ... an accomplice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, the same as the workmen who tried to drop the bag of sand on us\r\nyesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt can\xe2\x80\x99t be possible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPossible or not, there was only one way to prove it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy killing the man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94by killing the horse. If you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t grabbed my arm, I should have\r\ncaptured one of Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s accomplices. Now, do you understand the folly\r\nof your act?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThroughout the afternoon both men were morose. They did not speak a\r\nword to each other. At five o\xe2\x80\x99clock they visited the rue Clapeyron, but\r\nwere careful to keep at a safe distance from the houses. However, three\r\nyoung men who were passing through the street, arm in arm, singing, ran\r\nagainst Sholmes and Wilson and refused to let them pass. Sholmes, who\r\nwas in an ill humor, contested the right of way with them. After a\r\nbrief struggle, Sholmes resorted to his fists. He struck one of the men\r\na hard blow on the chest, another a blow in the face, and thus subdued\r\ntwo of his adversaries. Thereupon the three of them took to their heels\r\nand disappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cthat does me good. I needed a little\r\nexercise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Wilson was leaning against the wall. Sholmes said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, old chap? You\xe2\x80\x99re quite pale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson pointed to his left arm, which hung inert, and stammered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what it is. My arm pains me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much?... Is it serious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am afraid so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe tried to raise his arm, but it was helpless. Sholmes felt it, gently\r\nat first, then in a rougher way, \xe2\x80\x9cto see how badly it was hurt,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. He concluded that Wilson was really hurt, so he led him to a\r\nneighboring pharmacy, where a closer examination revealed the fact that\r\nthe arm was broken and that Wilson was a candidate for the hospital. In\r\nthe meantime they bared his arm and applied some remedies to ease his\r\nsuffering.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come, old chap, cheer up!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, who was holding\r\nWilson\xe2\x80\x99s arm, \xe2\x80\x9cin five or six weeks you will be all right again. But I\r\nwill pay them back ... the rascals! Especially Lupin, for this is his\r\nwork ... no doubt of that. I swear to you if ever\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped suddenly, dropped the arm\xe2\x80\x94which caused Wilson such an access\r\nof pain that he almost fainted\xe2\x80\x94and, striking his forehead, Sholmes\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, I have an idea. You know, I have one occasionally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stood for a moment, silent, with staring eyes, and then muttered, in\r\nshort, sharp phrases:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s it ... that will explain all ... right at my feet ... and\r\nI didn\xe2\x80\x99t see it ... ah, parbleu! I should have thought of it before....\r\nWilson, I shall have good news for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAbruptly leaving his old friend, Sholmes ran into the street and went\r\ndirectly to the house known as number 25. On one of the stones, to the\r\nright of the door, he read this inscription: \xe2\x80\x9cDestange, architect,\r\n1875.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a similar inscription on the house numbered 23.\r\n\r\nOf course, there was nothing unusual in that. But what might be read on\r\nthe houses in the avenue Henri-Martin?\r\n\r\nA carriage was passing. He engaged it and directed the driver to take\r\nhim to No. 134 avenue Henri-Martin. He was roused to a high pitch of\r\nexcitement. He stood up in the carriage and urged the horse to greater\r\nspeed. He offered extra pourboires to the driver. Quicker! Quicker!\r\n\r\nHow great was his anxiety as they turned from the rue de la Pompe! Had\r\nhe caught a glimpse of the truth at last?\r\n\r\nOn one of the stones of the late Baron\xe2\x80\x99s house he read the words:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDestange, architect, 1874.\xe2\x80\x9d And a similar inscription appeared on the\r\ntwo adjoining houses.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe reaction was such that he settled down in the seat of the carriage,\r\ntrembling from joy. At last, a tiny ray of light had penetrated the\r\ndark shadows which encompassed these mysterious crimes! In the vast\r\nsombre forest wherein a thousand pathways crossed and re-crossed, he\r\nhad discovered the first clue to the track followed by the enemy!\r\n\r\nHe entered a branch postoffice and obtained telephonic connection with\r\nthe ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon. The Countess answered the telephone call.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello!... Is that you, madame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Sholmes, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? Everything going all right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite well, but I wish to ask you one question.... Hello!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I hear you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me, when was the ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon built?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was destroyed by fire and rebuilt about thirty years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho built it, and in what year?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is an inscription on the front of the house which reads: \xe2\x80\x98Lucien\r\nDestange, architect, 1877.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, madame, that is all. Good-bye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went away, murmuring: \xe2\x80\x9cDestange ... Lucien Destange ... that name\r\nhas a familiar sound.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe noticed a public reading-room, entered, consulted a dictionary of\r\nmodern biography, and copied the following information: \xe2\x80\x9cLucien\r\nDestange, born 1840, Grand-Prix de Rome, officer of the Legion of\r\nHonor, author of several valuable books on architecture, etc....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he returned to the pharmacy and found that Wilson had been taken\r\nto the hospital. There Sholmes found him with his arm in splints, and\r\nshivering with fever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVictory! Victory!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cI hold one end of the thread.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf what thread?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one that leads to victory. I shall now be walking on solid ground,\r\nwhere there will be footprints, clues....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCigarette ashes?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Wilson, whose curiosity had overcome his pain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd many other things! Just think, Wilson, I have found the mysterious\r\nlink which unites the different adventures in which the blonde Lady\r\nplayed a part. Why did Lupin select those three houses for the scenes\r\nof his exploits?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause those three houses were built by the same architect. That was\r\nan easy problem, eh? Of course ... but who would have thought of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one but you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who, except I, knows that the same architect, by the use of\r\nanalogous plans, has rendered it possible for a person to execute three\r\ndistinct acts which, though miraculous in appearance, are, in reality,\r\nquite simple and easy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was a stroke of good luck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it was time, dear boy, as I was becoming very impatient. You know,\r\nthis is our fourth day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut of ten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! after this\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes was excited, delighted, and gayer than usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when I think that these rascals might have attacked me in the\r\nstreet and broken my arm just as they did yours! Isn\xe2\x80\x99t that so,\r\nWilson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson simply shivered at the horrible thought. Sholmes continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must profit by the lesson. I can see, Wilson, that we were wrong to\r\ntry and fight Lupin in the open, and leave ourselves exposed to his\r\nattacks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can see it, and feel it, too, in my broken arm,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have one consolation, Wilson; that is, that I escaped. Now, I must\r\nbe doubly cautious. In an open fight he will defeat me; but if I can\r\nwork in the dark, unseen by him, I have the advantage, no matter how\r\nstrong his forces may be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGanimard might be of some assistance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever! On the day that I can truly say: Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin is there; I show\r\nyou the quarry, and how to catch it; I shall go and see Ganimard at one\r\nof the two addresses that he gave me\xe2\x80\x94his residence in the rue\r\nPergolese, or at the Suisse tavern in the Place du Ch\xc3\xa2telet. But, until\r\nthat time, I shall work alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe approached the bed, placed his hand on Wilson\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder\xe2\x80\x94on the sore\r\none, of course\xe2\x80\x94and said to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake care of yourself, old fellow. Henceforth your r\xc3\xb4le will be to\r\nkeep two or three of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s men busy watching here in vain for\r\nmy return to enquire about your health. It is a secret mission for you,\r\neh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and I shall do my best to fulfil it conscientiously. Then you do\r\nnot expect to come here any more?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat for?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know ... of course.... I am getting on as well as possible.\r\nBut, Herlock, do me a last service: give me a drink.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA drink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am dying of thirst; and with my fever\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be sure\xe2\x80\x94directly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe made a pretense of getting some water, perceived a package of\r\ntobacco, lighted his pipe, and then, as if he had not heard his\r\nfriend\xe2\x80\x99s request, he went away, whilst Wilson uttered a mute prayer for\r\nthe inaccessible water.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Destange!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe servant eyed from head to foot the person to whom he had opened the\r\ndoor of the house\xe2\x80\x94the magnificent house that stood at the corner of the\r\nPlace Malesherbes and the rue Montchanin\xe2\x80\x94and at the sight of the man\r\nwith gray hairs, badly shaved, dressed in a shabby black coat, with a\r\nbody as ill-formed and ungracious as his face, he replied with the\r\ndisdain which he thought the occasion warranted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Destange may or may not be at home. That depends. Has\r\nmonsieur a card?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonsieur did not have a card, but he had a letter of introduction and,\r\nafter the servant had taken the letter to Mon. Destange, he was\r\nconducted into the presence of that gentleman who was sitting in a\r\nlarge circular room or rotunda which occupied one of the wings of the\r\nhouse. It was a library, and contained a profusion of books and\r\narchitectural drawings. When the stranger entered, the architect said\r\nto him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are Monsieur Stickmann?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy secretary tells me that he is ill, and has sent you to continue the\r\ngeneral catalogue of the books which he commenced under my direction,\r\nand, more particularly, the catalogue of German books. Are you familiar\r\nwith that kind of work?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, monsieur, quite so,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, with a strong German accent.\r\n\r\nUnder those circumstances the bargain was soon concluded, and Mon.\r\nDestange commenced work with his new secretary.\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes had gained access to the house.\r\n\r\nIn order to escape the vigilance of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin and gain admittance to\r\nthe house occupied by Lucien Destange and his daughter Clotilde, the\r\nfamous detective had been compelled to resort to a number of\r\nstratagems, and, under a variety of names, to ingratiate himself into\r\nthe good graces and confidence of a number of persons\xe2\x80\x94in short, to\r\nlive, during forty-eight hours, a most complicated life. During that\r\ntime he had acquired the following information: Mon. Destange, having\r\nretired from active business on account of his failing health, now\r\nlived amongst the many books he had accumulated on the subject of\r\narchitecture. He derived infinite pleasure in viewing and handling\r\nthose dusty old volumes.\r\n\r\nHis daughter Clotilde was considered eccentric. She passed her time in\r\nanother part of the house, and never went out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d Sholmes said to himself, as he wrote in a register the\r\ntitles of the books which Mon. Destange dictated to him, \xe2\x80\x9call that is\r\nvague and incomplete, but it is quite a long step in advance. I shall\r\nsurely solve one of these absorbing problems: Is Mon. Destange\r\nassociated with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin? Does he continue to see him? Are the\r\npapers relating to the construction of the three houses still in\r\nexistence? Will those papers not furnish me with the location of other\r\nhouses of similar construction which Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin and his associates\r\nwill plunder in the future?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Destange, an accomplice of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin! That venerable man,\r\nan officer of the Legion of Honor, working in league with a\r\nburglar\xe2\x80\x94such an idea was absurd! Besides, if we concede that such a\r\ncomplicity exists, how could Mon. Destange, thirty years ago, have\r\npossibly foreseen the thefts of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, who was then an infant?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo matter! The Englishman was implacable. With his marvellous scent,\r\nand that instinct which never fails him, he felt that he was in the\r\nheart of some strange mystery. Ever since he first entered the house,\r\nhe had been under the influence of that impression, and yet he could\r\nnot define the grounds on which he based his suspicions.\r\n\r\nUp to the morning of the second day he had not made any significant\r\ndiscovery. At two o\xe2\x80\x99clock of that day he saw Clotilde Destange for the\r\nfirst time; she came to the library in search of a book. She was about\r\nthirty years of age, a brunette, slow and silent in her movements, with\r\nfeatures imbued with that expression of indifference which is\r\ncharacteristic of people who live a secluded life. She exchanged a few\r\nwords with her father, and then retired, without even looking at\r\nSholmes.\r\n\r\nThe afternoon dragged along monotonously. At five o\xe2\x80\x99clock Mon. Destange\r\nannounced his intention to go out. Sholmes was alone on the circular\r\ngallery that was constructed about ten feet above the floor of the\r\nrotunda. It was almost dark. He was on the point of going out, when he\r\nheard a slight sound and, at the same time, experienced the feeling\r\nthat there was someone in the room. Several minutes passed before he\r\nsaw or heard anything more. Then he shuddered; a shadowy form emerged\r\nfrom the gloom, quite close to him, upon the balcony. It seemed\r\nincredible. How long had this mysterious visitor been there? Whence did\r\nhe come?\r\n\r\nThe strange man descended the steps and went directly to a large oaken\r\ncupboard. Sholmes was a keen observer of the man\xe2\x80\x99s movements. He\r\nwatched him searching amongst the papers with which the cupboard was\r\nfilled. What was he looking for?\r\n\r\nThen the door opened and Mlle. Destange entered, speaking to someone\r\nwho was following her:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you have decided not to go out, father?... Then I will make a light\r\n... one second ... do not move....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe strange man closed the cupboard and hid in the embrasure of a large\r\nwindow, drawing the curtains together. Did Mlle. Destange not see him?\r\nDid she not hear him? Calmly she turned on the electric lights; she and\r\nher father sat down close to each other. She opened a book she had\r\nbrought with her, and commenced to read. After the lapse of a few\r\nminutes she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour secretary has gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you like him as well as you did at first?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, as if she\r\nwere not aware of the illness of the real secretary and his replacement\r\nby Stickmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMonsieur Destange\xe2\x80\x99s head bobbed from one side to the other. He was\r\nasleep. The girl resumed her reading. A moment later one of the window\r\ncurtains was pushed back, and the strange man emerged and glided along\r\nthe wall toward the door, which obliged him to pass behind Mon.\r\nDestange but in front of Clotilde, and brought him into the light so\r\nthat Herlock Sholmes obtained a good view of the man\xe2\x80\x99s face. It was\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\r\n\r\nThe Englishman was delighted. His forecast was verified; he had\r\npenetrated to the very heart of the mystery, and found Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin to\r\nbe the moving spirit in it.\r\n\r\nClotilde had not yet displayed any knowledge of his presence, although\r\nit was quite improbable that any movement of the intruder had escaped\r\nher notice. Lupin had almost reached the door and, in fact, his hand\r\nwas already seeking the door-knob, when his coat brushed against a\r\nsmall table and knocked something to the floor. Monsieur Destange awoke\r\nwith a start. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin was already standing in front of him, hat in\r\nhand, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaxime Bermond,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Mon. Destange, joyfully. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Maxime,\r\nwhat lucky chance brings you here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe wish to see you and Mademoiselle Destange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you return from your journey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must stay to dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, thank you, I am sorry, but I have an appointment to dine with some\r\nfriends at a restaurant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, to-morrow, then, Clotilde, you must urge him to come to-morrow.\r\nAh! my dear Maxime.... I thought of you many times during your\r\nabsence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I went through all my old papers in that cupboard, and found our\r\nlast statement of account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat account?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRelating to the avenue Henri-Martin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! do you keep such papers? What for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the three of them left the room, and continued their conversation\r\nin a small parlor which adjoined the library.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d Sholmes asked himself, in a sudden access of doubt.\r\nCertainly, from all appearances, it was he; and yet it was also someone\r\nelse who resembled Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin in certain respects, and who still\r\nmaintained his own individuality, features, and color of hair. Sholmes\r\ncould hear Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s voice in the adjoining room. He was relating some\r\nstories at which Mon. Destange laughed heartily, and which even brought\r\na smile to the lips of the melancholy Clotilde. And each of those\r\nsmiles appeared to be the reward which Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin was seeking, and\r\nwhich he was delighted to have secured. His success caused him to\r\nredouble his efforts and, insensibly, at the sound of that clear and\r\nhappy voice, Clotilde\xe2\x80\x99s face brightened and lost that cold and listless\r\nexpression which usually pervaded it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey love each other,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cbut what the deuce can there\r\nbe in common between Clotilde Destange and Maxime Bermond? Does she\r\nknow that Maxime is none other than Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUntil seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock Sholmes was an anxious listener, seeking to profit\r\nby the conversation. Then, with infinite precaution, he descended from\r\nthe gallery, crept along the side of the room to the door in such a\r\nmanner that the people in the adjoining room did not see him.\r\n\r\nWhen he reached the street Sholmes satisfied himself that there was\r\nneither an automobile nor a cab waiting there; then he slowly limped\r\nalong the boulevard Malesherbes. He turned into an adjacent street,\r\ndonned the overcoat which he had carried on his arm, altered the shape\r\nof his hat, assumed an upright carriage, and, thus transformed,\r\nreturned to a place whence he could watch the door of Mon. Destange\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhouse.\r\n\r\nIn a few minutes Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin came out, and proceeded to walk toward\r\nthe center of Paris by way of the rues de Constantinople and London.\r\nHerlock Sholmes followed at a distance of a hundred paces.\r\n\r\nExciting moments for the Englishman! He sniffed the air, eagerly, like\r\na hound following a fresh scent. It seemed to him a delightful thing\r\nthus to follow his adversary. It was no longer Herlock Sholmes who was\r\nbeing watched, but Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, the invisible Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. He held\r\nhim, so to speak, within the grasp of his eye, by an imperceptible bond\r\nthat nothing could break. And he was pleased to think that the quarry\r\nbelonged to him.\r\n\r\nBut he soon observed a suspicious circumstance. In the intervening\r\nspace between him and Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin he noticed several people traveling\r\nin the same direction, particularly two husky fellows in slouch hats on\r\nthe left side of the street, and two others on the right wearing caps\r\nand smoking cigarettes. Of course, their presence in that vicinity may\r\nhave been the result of chance, but Sholmes was more astonished when he\r\nobserved that the four men stopped when Lupin entered a tobacco shop;\r\nand still more surprised when the four men started again after Lupin\r\nemerged from the shop, each keeping to his own side of the street.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCurse it!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Sholmes; \xe2\x80\x9che is being followed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was annoyed at the idea that others were on the trail of Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin; that someone might deprive him, not of the glory\xe2\x80\x94he cared little\r\nfor that\xe2\x80\x94but of the immense pleasure of capturing, single-handed, the\r\nmost formidable enemy he had ever met. And he felt that he was not\r\nmistaken; the men presented to Sholmes\xe2\x80\x99 experienced eye the appearance\r\nand manner of those who, while regulating their gait to that of\r\nanother, wish to present a careless and natural air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this some of Ganimard\xe2\x80\x99s work?\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cIs he playing me\r\nfalse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe felt inclined to speak to one of the men with a view of acting in\r\nconcert with him; but as they were now approaching the boulevard the\r\ncrowd was becoming denser, and he was afraid he might lose sight of\r\nLupin. So he quickened his pace and turned into the boulevard just in\r\ntime to see Lupin ascending the steps of the Hungarian restaurant at\r\nthe corner of the rue du Helder. The door of the restaurant was open,\r\nso that Sholmes, while sitting on a bench on the other side of the\r\nboulevard, could see Lupin take a seat at a table, luxuriously\r\nappointed and decorated with flowers, at which three gentlemen and two\r\nladies of elegant appearance were already seated and who extended to\r\nLupin a hearty greeting.\r\n\r\nSholmes now looked about for the four men and perceived them amongst a\r\ncrowd of people who were listening to a gipsy orchestra that was\r\nplaying in a neighboring caf\xc3\xa9. It was a curious thing that they were\r\npaying no attention to Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, but seemed to be friendly with the\r\npeople around them. One of them took a cigarette from his pocket and\r\napproached a gentleman who wore a frock coat and silk hat. The\r\ngentleman offered the other his cigar for a light, and Sholmes had the\r\nimpression that they talked to each other much longer than the occasion\r\ndemanded. Finally the gentleman approached the Hungarian restaurant,\r\nentered and looked around. When he caught sight of Lupin he advanced\r\nand spoke to him for a moment, then took a seat at an adjoining table.\r\nSholmes now recognized this gentleman as the horseman who had tried to\r\nrun him down in the avenue Henri-Martin.\r\n\r\nThen Sholmes understood that these men were not tracking Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin;\r\nthey were a part of his band. They were watching over his safety. They\r\nwere his bodyguard, his satellites, his vigilant escort. Wherever\r\ndanger threatened Lupin, these confederates were at hand to avert it,\r\nready to defend him. The four men were accomplices. The gentleman in\r\nthe frock coat was an accomplice. These facts furnished the Englishman\r\nwith food for reflection. Would he ever succeed in capturing that\r\ninaccessible individual? What unlimited power was possessed by such an\r\norganization, directed by such a chief!\r\n\r\nHe tore a leaf from his notebook, wrote a few lines in pencil, which he\r\nplaced in an envelope, and said to a boy about fifteen years of age who\r\nwas sitting on the bench beside him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, my boy; take a carriage and deliver this letter to the cashier\r\nof the Suisse tavern, Place du Ch\xc3\xa2telet. Be quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe gave him a five-franc piece. The boy disappeared.\r\n\r\nA half hour passed away. The crowd had grown larger, and Sholmes\r\nperceived only at intervals the accomplices of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. Then\r\nsomeone brushed against him and whispered in his ear:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? what is it, Monsieur Sholmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! it is you, Ganimard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I received your note at the tavern. What\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere ... in the restaurant. Lean to the right.... Do you see him\r\nnow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is pouring a glass of champagne for the lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I tell you.... Ah! yet, it may be. It looks a great deal like\r\nhim,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard, naively. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the others\xe2\x80\x94accomplices?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; the lady sitting beside him is Lady Cliveden; the other is the\r\nDuchess de Cleath. The gentleman sitting opposite Lupin is the Spanish\r\nAmbassador to London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard took a step forward. Sholmes retained him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe prudent. You are alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo is he.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he has a number of men on the boulevard mounting guard. And inside\r\nthe restaurant that gentleman\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I, when I take Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin by the collar and announce his name, I\r\nshall have the entire room on my side and all the waiters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should prefer to have a few policemen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Monsieur Sholmes, we have no choice. We must catch him when we\r\ncan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was right; Sholmes knew it. It was better to take advantage of the\r\nopportunity and make the attempt. Sholmes simply gave this advice to\r\nGanimard:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConceal your identity as long as possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes glided behind a newspaper kiosk, whence he could still watch\r\nLupin, who was leaning toward Lady Cliveden, talking and smiling.\r\n\r\nGanimard crossed the street, hands in his pockets, as if he were going\r\ndown the boulevard, but when he reached the opposite sidewalk he turned\r\nquickly and bounded up the steps of the restaurant. There was a shrill\r\nwhistle. Ganimard ran against the head waiter, who had suddenly planted\r\nhimself in the doorway and now pushed Ganimard back with a show of\r\nindignation, as if he were an intruder whose presence would bring\r\ndisgrace upon the restaurant. Ganimard was surprised. At the same\r\nmoment the gentleman in the frock coat came out. He took the part of\r\nthe detective and entered into an exciting argument with the waiter;\r\nboth of them hung on to Ganimard, one pushing him in, the other pushing\r\nhim out in such a manner that, despite all his efforts and despite his\r\nfurious protestations, the unfortunate detective soon found himself on\r\nthe sidewalk.\r\n\r\nThe struggling men were surrounded by a crowd. Two policemen, attracted\r\nby the noise, tried to force their way through the crowd, but\r\nencountered a mysterious resistance and could make no headway through\r\nthe opposing backs and pressing shoulders of the mob.\r\n\r\nBut suddenly, as if by magic, the crowd parted and the passage to the\r\nrestaurant was clear. The head waiter, recognizing his mistake, was\r\nprofuse in his apologies; the gentleman in the frock coat ceased his\r\nefforts on behalf of the detective, the crowd dispersed, the policemen\r\npassed on, and Ganimard hastened to the table at which the six guests\r\nwere sitting. But now there were only five! He looked around.... The\r\nonly exit was the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe person who was sitting here!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried to the five astonished\r\nguests. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Destro?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA waiter approached and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe gentleman went upstairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard rushed up in the hope of finding him. The upper floor of the\r\nrestaurant contained private dining-rooms and had a private stairway\r\nleading to the boulevard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo use looking for him now,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is far away by\r\nthis time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nHe was not far away\xe2\x80\x94two hundred yards at most\xe2\x80\x94in the Madeleine-Bastille\r\nomnibus, which was rolling along very peacefully with its three horses\r\nacross the Place de l\xe2\x80\x99Op\xc3\xa9ra toward the Boulevard des Capucines. Two\r\nsturdy fellows were talking together on the platform. On the roof of\r\nthe omnibus near the stairs an old fellow was sleeping; it was Herlock\r\nSholmes.\r\n\r\nWith bobbing head, rocked by the movement of the vehicle, the\r\nEnglishman said to himself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Wilson could see me now, how proud he would be of his\r\ncollaborator!... Bah! It was easy to foresee that the game was lost, as\r\nsoon as the man whistled; nothing could be done but watch the exits and\r\nsee that our man did not escape. Really, Lupin makes life exciting and\r\ninteresting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the terminal point Herlock Sholmes, by leaning over, saw Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin leaving the omnibus, and as he passed in front of the men who\r\nformed his bodyguard Sholmes heard him say: \xe2\x80\x9cA l\xe2\x80\x99Etoile.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA l\xe2\x80\x99Etoile, exactly, a rendezvous. I shall be there,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Sholmes.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will follow the two men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin took an automobile; but the men walked the entire distance,\r\nfollowed by Sholmes. They stopped at a narrow house, No. 40 rue\r\nChalgrin, and rang the bell. Sholmes took his position in the shadow of\r\na doorway, whence he could watch the house in question. A man opened\r\none of the windows of the ground floor and closed the shutters. But the\r\nshutters did not reach to the top of the window. The impost was clear.\r\n\r\nAt the end of ten minutes a gentleman rang at the same door and a few\r\nminutes later another man came. A short time afterward an automobile\r\nstopped in front of the house, bringing two passengers: Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\nand a lady concealed beneath a large cloak and a thick veil.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blonde Lady, no doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes to himself, as the automobile\r\ndrove away.\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes now approached the house, climbed to the window-ledge\r\nand, by standing on tiptoe, he was able to see through the window above\r\nthe shutters. What did he see?\r\n\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, leaning against the mantel, was speaking with\r\nconsiderable animation. The others were grouped around him, listening\r\nto him attentively. Amongst them Sholmes easily recognized the\r\ngentleman in the frock coat and he thought one of the other men\r\nresembled the head-waiter of the restaurant. As to the blonde Lady, she\r\nwas seated in an armchair with her back to the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are holding a consultation,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are worried\r\nover the incident at the restaurant and are holding a council of war.\r\nAh! what a master stroke it would be to capture all of them at one fell\r\nstroke!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne of them, having moved toward the door, Sholmes leaped to the ground\r\nand concealed himself in the shadow. The gentleman in the frock coat\r\nand the head-waiter left the house. A moment later a light appeared at\r\nthe windows of the first floor, but the shutters were closed\r\nimmediately and the upper part of the house was dark as well as the\r\nlower.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLupin and the woman are on the ground floor; the two confederates live\r\non the upper floor,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes.\r\n\r\nSholmes remained there the greater part of the night, fearing that if\r\nhe went away Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin might leave during his absence. At four\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, seeing two policemen at the end of the street, he approached\r\nthem, explained the situation and left them to watch the house. He went\r\nto Ganimard\xe2\x80\x99s residence in the rue Pergolese and wakened him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have him yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you haven\xe2\x80\x99t got any better hold on him than you had a while ago, I\r\nmight as well go back to bed. But we may as well go to the\r\nstation-house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went to the police station in the rue Mesnil and from there to the\r\nresidence of the commissary, Mon. Decointre. Then, accompanied by half\r\na dozen policemen, they went to the rue Chalgrin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything new?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sholmes, addressing the two policemen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was just breaking day when, after taking necessary measures to\r\nprevent escape, the commissary rang the bell and commenced to question\r\nthe concierge. The woman was greatly frightened at this early morning\r\ninvasion, and she trembled as she replied that there were no tenants on\r\nthe ground floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! not a tenant?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ganimard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but on the first floor there are two men named Leroux. They have\r\nfurnished the apartment on the ground floor for some country\r\nrelations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA gentleman and lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho came here last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps ... but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know ... I was asleep. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t think so,\r\nfor the key is here. They did not ask for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith that key the commissary opened the door of the ground-floor\r\napartment. It comprised only two rooms and they were empty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw both of them in this room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t doubt your word,\xe2\x80\x9d said the commissary; \xe2\x80\x9cbut they are not here\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go to the first floor. They must be there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe first floor is occupied by two men named Leroux.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will examine the Messieurs Leroux.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey all ascended the stairs and the commissary rang. At the second\r\nring a man opened the door; he was in his shirt-sleeves. Sholmes\r\nrecognized him as one of Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s bodyguard. The man assumed a furious\r\nair:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean by making such a row at this hour of the morning ...\r\nwaking people up....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut he stopped suddenly, astounded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod forgive me!... really, gentlemen, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t notice who it was. Why,\r\nit is Monsieur Decointre!... and you, Monsieur Ganimard. What can I do\r\nfor you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard burst into an uncontrollable fit of laughter, which caused him\r\nto bend double and turn black in the face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! it is you, Leroux,\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered. \xe2\x80\x9cOh! this is too funny! Leroux,\r\nan accomplice of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin! Oh, I shall die! and your brother,\r\nLeroux, where is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEdmond!\xe2\x80\x9d called the man. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is Ganimard, who has come to visit us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother man appeared and at sight of him Ganimard\xe2\x80\x99s mirth redoubled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! oh! we had no idea of this! Ah! my friends, you are in a bad fix\r\nnow. Who would have ever suspected it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTurning to Sholmes, Ganimard introduced the man:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVictor Leroux, a detective from our office, one of the best men in the\r\niron brigade ... Edmond Leroux, chief clerk in the anthropometric\r\nservice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V.\r\nAN ABDUCTION.\r\n\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes said nothing. To protest? To accuse the two men? That\r\nwould be useless. In the absence of evidence which he did not possess\r\nand had no time to seek, no one would believe him. Moreover, he was\r\nstifled with rage, but would not display his feelings before the\r\ntriumphant Ganimard. So he bowed respectfully to the brothers Leroux,\r\nguardians of society, and retired.\r\n\r\nIn the vestibule he turned toward a low door which looked like the\r\nentrance to a cellar, and picked up a small red stone; it was a garnet.\r\nWhen he reached the street he turned and read on the front of the house\r\nthis inscription: \xe2\x80\x9cLucien Destange, architect, 1877.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe adjoining house, No. 42, bore the same inscription.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways the double passage\xe2\x80\x94numbers 40 and 42 have a secret means of\r\ncommunication. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t I think of that? I should have remained with\r\nthe two policemen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe met the policemen near the corner and said to them:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo people came out of house No. 42 during my absence, didn\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; a gentleman and lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard approached. Sholmes took his arm, and as they walked down the\r\nstreet he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Ganimard, you have had a good laugh and will no doubt forgive\r\nme for the trouble I have caused you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! there\xe2\x80\x99s no harm done; but it was a good joke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI admit that; but the best jokes have only a short life, and this one\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t last much longer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is now the seventh day, and I can remain only three days more.\r\nThen I must return to London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to ask you to be in readiness, as I may call on you at any hour\r\non Tuesday or Wednesday night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor an expedition of the same kind as we had to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, monsieur, the very same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith what result?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe capture of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI swear it, on my honor, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes bade Ganimard good-bye and went to the nearest hotel for a few\r\nhours\xe2\x80\x99 sleep; after which, refreshed and with renewed confidence in\r\nhimself, he returned to the rue Chalgrin, slipped two louis into the\r\nhand of the concierge, assured himself that the brothers Leroux had\r\ngone out, learned that the house belonged to a Monsieur Harmingeat,\r\nand, provided with a candle, descended to the cellar through the low\r\ndoor near which he had found the garnet. At the bottom of the stairs he\r\nfound another exactly like it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not mistaken,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought; \xe2\x80\x9cthis is the means of communication.\r\nLet me see if my skeleton-key will open the cellar reserved for the\r\ntenant of the ground floor. Yes; it will. Now, I will examine those\r\ncases of wine... oh! oh! here are some places where the dust has been\r\ncleared away ... and some footprints on the ground....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA slight noise caused him to listen attentively. Quickly he pushed the\r\ndoor shut, blew out his candle and hid behind a pile of empty wine\r\ncases. After a few seconds he noticed that a portion of the wall swung\r\non a pivot, the light of a lantern was thrown into the cellar, an arm\r\nappeared, then a man entered.\r\n\r\nHe was bent over, as if he were searching for something. He felt in the\r\ndust with his fingers and several times he threw something into a\r\ncardboard box that he carried in his left hand. Afterward he\r\nobliterated the traces of his footsteps, as well as the footprints left\r\nby Lupin and the blonde lady, and he was about to leave the cellar by\r\nthe same way as he had entered, when he uttered a harsh cry and fell to\r\nthe ground. Sholmes had leaped upon him. It was the work of a moment,\r\nand in the simplest manner in the world the man found himself stretched\r\non the ground, bound and handcuffed. The Englishman leaned over him and\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you anything to say?... To tell what you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man replied by such an ironical smile that Sholmes realized the\r\nfutility of questioning him. So he contented himself by exploring the\r\npockets of his captive, but he found only a bunch of keys, a\r\nhandkerchief and the small cardboard box which contained a dozen\r\ngarnets similar to those which Sholmes had found.\r\n\r\nThen what was he to do with the man? Wait until his friends came to his\r\nhelp and deliver all of them to the police? What good would that do?\r\nWhat advantage would that give him over Lupin?\r\n\r\nHe hesitated; but an examination of the box decided the question. The\r\nbox bore this name and address: \xe2\x80\x9cLeonard, jeweler, rue de la Paix.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe resolved to abandon the man to his fate. He locked the cellar and\r\nleft the house. At a branch postoffice he sent a telegram to Monsieur\r\nDestange, saying that he could not come that day. Then he went to see\r\nthe jeweler and, handing him the garnets, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame sent me with these stones. She wishes to have them reset.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes had struck the right key. The jeweler replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly; the lady telephoned to me. She said she would be here\r\nto-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes established himself on the sidewalk to wait for the lady, but\r\nit was five o\xe2\x80\x99clock when he saw a heavily-veiled lady approach and\r\nenter the store. Through the window he saw her place on the counter a\r\npiece of antique jewelry set with garnets.\r\n\r\nShe went away almost immediately, walking quickly and passed through\r\nstreets that were unknown to the Englishman. As it was now almost dark,\r\nhe walked close behind her and followed her into a five-story house of\r\ndouble flats and, therefore, occupied by numerous tenants. At the\r\nsecond floor she stopped and entered. Two minutes later the Englishman\r\ncommenced to try the keys on the bunch he had taken from the man in the\r\nrue Chalgrin. The fourth key fitted the lock.\r\n\r\nNotwithstanding the darkness of the rooms, he perceived that they were\r\nabsolutely empty, as if unoccupied, and the various doors were standing\r\nopen so that he could see all the apartments. At the end of a corridor\r\nhe perceived a ray of light and, by approaching on tiptoe and looking\r\nthrough the glass door, he saw the veiled lady who had removed her hat\r\nand dress and was now wearing a velvet dressing-gown. The discarded\r\ngarments were lying on the only chair in the room and a lighted lamp\r\nstood on the mantel.\r\n\r\nThen he saw her approach the fireplace and press what appeared to be\r\nthe button of an electric bell. Immediately the panel to the right of\r\nthe fireplace moved and slowly glided behind the adjoining panel, thus\r\ndisclosing an opening large enough for a person to pass through. The\r\nlady disappeared through this opening, taking the lamp with her.\r\n\r\nThe operation was a very simple one. Sholmes adopted it and followed\r\nthe lady. He found himself in total darkness and immediately he felt\r\nhis face brushed by some soft articles. He lighted a match and found\r\nthat he was in a very small room completely filled with cloaks and\r\ndresses suspended on hangers. He picked his way through until he\r\nreached a door that was draped with a portiere. He peeped through and,\r\nbehold, the blonde lady was there, under his eyes, and almost within\r\nreach of his hand.\r\n\r\nShe extinguished the lamp and turned on the electric lights. Then for\r\nthe first time Herlock Sholmes obtained a good look at her face. He was\r\namazed. The woman, whom he had overtaken after so much trouble and\r\nafter so many tricks and man\xc5\x93uvres, was none other than Clotilde\r\nDestange.\r\n\r\n\r\nClotilde Destange, the assassin of the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec and the thief\r\nwho stole the blue diamond! Clotilde Destange, the mysterious friend of\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin! And the blonde lady!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am only a stupid ass,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Herlock Sholmes at that moment.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s friend was a blonde and Clotilde is a brunette, I\r\nnever dreamed that they were the same person. But how could the blonde\r\nlady remain a blonde after the murder of the baron and the theft of the\r\ndiamond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes could see a portion of the room; it was a boudoir, furnished\r\nwith the most delightful luxury and exquisite taste, and adorned with\r\nbeautiful tapestries and costly ornaments. A mahogany couch,\r\nupholstered in silk, was located on the side of the room opposite the\r\ndoor at which Sholmes was standing. Clotilde was sitting on this couch,\r\nmotionless, her face covered by her hands. Then he perceived that she\r\nwas weeping. Great tears rolled down her pale cheeks and fell, drop by\r\ndrop, on the velvet corsage. The tears came thick and fast, as if their\r\nsource were inexhaustible.\r\n\r\nA door silently opened behind her and Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin entered. He looked\r\nat her for a long time without making his presence known; then he\r\napproached her, knelt at her feet, pressed her head to his breast,\r\nfolded her in his arms, and his actions indicated an infinite measure\r\nof love and sympathy. For a time not a word was uttered, but her tears\r\nbecame less abundant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was so anxious to make you happy,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; you are crying.... Your tears break my heart, Clotilde.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe caressing and sympathetic tone of his voice soothed her, and she\r\nlistened to him with an eager desire for hope and happiness. Her\r\nfeatures were softened by a smile, and yet how sad a smile! He\r\ncontinued to speak in a tone of tender entreaty:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should not be unhappy, Clotilde; you have no cause to be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe displayed her delicate white hands and said, solemnly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Maxime; so long as I see those hands I shall be sad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are stained with blood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! Do not think of that!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cThe dead is past and\r\ngone. Do not resurrect it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he kissed the long, delicate hand, while she regarded him with a\r\nbrighter smile as if each kiss effaced a portion of that dreadful\r\nmemory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must love me, Maxime; you must\xe2\x80\x94because no woman will ever love you\r\nas I do. For your sake, I have done many things, not at your order or\r\nrequest, but in obedience to your secret desires. I have done things at\r\nwhich my will and conscience revolted, but there was some unknown power\r\nthat I could not resist. What I did I did involuntarily, mechanically,\r\nbecause it helped you, because you wished it ... and I am ready to do\r\nit again to-morrow ... and always.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Clotilde,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, bitterly, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy did I draw you into my\r\nadventurous life? I should have remained the Maxime Bermond that you\r\nloved five years ago, and not have let you know the ... other man that\r\nI am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe replied in a low voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love the other man, also, and I have nothing to regret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you regret your past life\xe2\x80\x94the free and happy life you once\r\nenjoyed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no regrets when you are here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, passionately. \xe2\x80\x9cAll\r\nfaults and crimes disappear when I see you. When you are away I may\r\nsuffer, and weep, and be horrified at what I have done; but when you\r\ncome it is all forgotten. Your love wipes it all away. And I am happy\r\nagain.... But you must love me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not love you on compulsion, Clotilde. I love you simply because\r\n... I love you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am just as sure of my own love as I am of yours. Only my life is a\r\nvery active and exciting one, and I cannot spend as much time with you\r\nas I would like\xe2\x80\x94just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? Some new danger? Tell me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! nothing serious. Only....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly what?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he is on our track.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho? Herlock Sholmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; it was he who dragged Ganimard into that affair at the Hungarian\r\nrestaurant. It was he who instructed the two policemen to watch the\r\nhouse in the rue Chalgrin. I have proof of it. Ganimard searched the\r\nhouse this morning and Sholmes was with him. Besides\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides? What?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there is another thing. One of our men is missing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJeanniot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe concierge?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I sent him to the rue Chalgrin this morning to pick up the\r\ngarnets that fell out of my brooch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no doubt, then, that Sholmes caught him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; the garnets were delivered to the jeweler in the rue de la Paix.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, what has become of him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Maxime, I am afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing to be afraid of, but I confess the situation is very\r\nserious. What does he know? Where does he hide himself? His isolation\r\nis his strong card. I cannot reach him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAct with extreme prudence, Clotilde. Some time ago I decided to change\r\nmy residence to a safer place, and Sholmes\xe2\x80\x99 appearance on the scene has\r\nprompted me to do so at once. When a man like that is on your track,\r\nyou must be prepared for the worst. Well, I am making my preparations.\r\nDay after to-morrow, Wednesday, I shall move. At noon it will be\r\nfinished. At two o\xe2\x80\x99clock I shall leave the place, after removing the\r\nlast trace of our residence there, which will be no small matter. Until\r\nthen\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUntil then we must not see each other and no one must see you,\r\nClotilde. Do not go out. I have no fear for myself, but I have for\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat Englishman cannot possibly reach me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not so sure of that. He is a dangerous man. Yesterday I came here\r\nto search the cupboard that contains all of Monsieur Destange\xe2\x80\x99s old\r\npapers and records. There is danger there. There is danger everywhere.\r\nI feel that he is watching us\xe2\x80\x94that he is drawing his net around us\r\ncloser and closer. It is one of those intuitions which never deceive\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case, Maxime, go, and think no more of my tears. I shall be\r\nbrave, and wait patiently until the danger is past. Adieu, Maxime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey held one another for some time in a last fond embrace. And it was\r\nshe that gently pushed him outside. Sholmes could hear the sound of\r\ntheir voices in the distance.\r\n\r\nEmboldened by the necessities of the situation and the urgent need of\r\nbringing his investigation to a speedy termination, Sholmes proceeded\r\nto make an examination of the house in which he now found himself. He\r\npassed through Clotilde\xe2\x80\x99s boudoir into a corridor, at the end of which\r\nthere was a stairway leading to the lower floor; he was about to\r\ndescend this stairway when he heard voices below, which caused him to\r\nchange his route. He followed the corridor, which was a circular one,\r\nand discovered another stairway, which he descended and found himself\r\namidst surroundings that bore a familiar appearance. He passed through\r\na door that stood partly open and entered a large circular room. It was\r\nMonsieur Destange\xe2\x80\x99s library.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! splendid!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I understand everything. The boudoir\r\nof Mademoiselle Clotilde\xe2\x80\x94the blonde Lady\xe2\x80\x94communicates with a room in\r\nthe adjoining house, and that house does not front on the Place\r\nMalesherbes, but upon an adjacent street, the rue Montchanin, if I\r\nremember the name correctly.... And I now understand how Clotilde\r\nDestange can meet her lover and at the same time create the impression\r\nthat she never leaves the house; and I understand also how Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\nwas enabled to make his mysterious entrance to the gallery last night.\r\nAh! there must be another connection between the library and the\r\nadjoining room. One more house full of ways that are dark! And no doubt\r\nLucien Destange was the architect, as usual!... I should take advantage\r\nof this opportunity to examine the contents of the cupboard and perhaps\r\nlearn the location of other houses with secret passages constructed by\r\nMonsieur Destange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes ascended to the gallery and concealed himself behind some\r\ndraperies, where he remained until late in the evening. At last a\r\nservant came and turned off the electric lights. An hour later the\r\nEnglishman, by the light of his lantern, made his way to the cupboard.\r\nAs he had surmised, it contained the architect\xe2\x80\x99s old papers, plans,\r\nspecifications and books of account. It also contained a series of\r\nregisters, arranged according to date, and Sholmes, having selected\r\nthose of the most recent dates, searched in the indexes for the name\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHarmingeat.\xe2\x80\x9d He found it in one of the registers with a reference to\r\npage 63. Turning to that page, he read:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHarmingeat, 40 rue Chalgrin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was followed by a detailed account of the work done in and about\r\nthe installation of a furnace in the house. And in the margin of the\r\nbook someone had written these words: \xe2\x80\x9cSee account M.B.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! I thought so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes; \xe2\x80\x9cthe account M.B. is the one I want.\r\nI shall learn from it the actual residence of Monsieur Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was morning before he found that important account. It comprised\r\nsixteen pages, one of which was a copy of the page on which was\r\ndescribed the work done for Mon. Harmingeat of the rue Chalgrin.\r\nAnother page described the work performed for Mon. Vatinel as owner of\r\nthe house at No. 25 rue Clapeyron. Another page was reserved for the\r\nBaron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, 134 avenue Henri-Martin; another was devoted to the\r\nCh\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon, and the eleven other pages to various owners of\r\nhouses in Paris.\r\n\r\nSholmes made a list of those eleven names and addresses; after which he\r\nreturned the books to their proper places, opened a window, jumped out\r\nonto the deserted street and closed the shutters behind him.\r\n\r\nWhen he reached his room at the hotel he lighted his pipe with all the\r\nsolemnity with which he was wont to characterize that act, and amidst\r\nclouds of smoke he studied the deductions that might be drawn from the\r\naccount of M.B., or rather, from the account of Maxime Bermond alias\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\r\n\r\nAt eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock he sent the following message to Ganimard:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI expect to pass through the rue Pergolese this forenoon and will\r\ninform you of a person whose arrest is of the highest importance. In\r\nany event, be at home to-night and to-morrow until noon and have at\r\nleast thirty men at your service.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThen he engaged an automobile at the stand on the boulevard, choosing\r\none whose chauffeur looked good-natured but dull-witted, and instructed\r\nhim to drive to the Place Malesherbes, where he stopped him about one\r\nhundred feet from Monsieur Destange\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy boy, close your carriage,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to the chauffeur; \xe2\x80\x9cturn up the\r\ncollar of your coat, for the wind is cold, and wait patiently. At the\r\nend of an hour and a half, crank up your machine. When I return we will\r\ngo to the rue Pergolese.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he was ascending the steps leading to the door a doubt entered his\r\nmind. Was it not a mistake on his part to be spending his time on the\r\naffairs of the blonde Lady, while Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin was preparing to move?\r\nWould he not be better engaged in trying to find the abode of his\r\nadversary amongst the eleven houses on his list?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen the blonde Lady becomes my prisoner, I shall\r\nbe master of the situation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he rang the bell.\r\n\r\n\r\nMonsieur Destange was already in the library. They had been working\r\nonly a few minutes, when Clotilde entered, bade her father good\r\nmorning, entered the adjoining parlor and sat down to write. From his\r\nplace Sholmes could see her leaning over the table and from time to\r\ntime absorbed in deep meditation. After a short time he picked up a\r\nbook and said to Monsieur Destange:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is a book that Mademoiselle Destange asked me to bring to her\r\nwhen I found it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went into the little parlor, stood before Clotilde in such a manner\r\nthat her father could not see her, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Monsieur Stickmann, your father\xe2\x80\x99s new secretary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clotilde, without moving, \xe2\x80\x9cmy father has changed his\r\nsecretary? I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, mademoiselle, and I desire to speak with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKindly take a seat, monsieur; I have finished.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe added a few words to her letter, signed it, enclosed it in the\r\nenvelope, sealed it, pushed her writing material away, rang the\r\ntelephone, got in communication with her dressmaker, asked the latter\r\nto hasten the completion of a traveling dress, as she required it at\r\nonce, and then, turning to Sholmes, she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am at your service, monsieur. But do you wish to speak before my\r\nfather? Would not that be better?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, mademoiselle; and I beg of you, do not raise your voice. It is\r\nbetter that Monsieur Destange should not hear us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor whose sake is it better?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYours, mademoiselle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot agree to hold any conversation with you that my father may\r\nnot hear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you must agree to this. It is imperative.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoth of them arose, eye to eye. She said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill standing, he commenced:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will be so good as to pardon me if I am mistaken on certain points\r\nof secondary importance. I will guarantee, however, the general\r\naccuracy of my statements.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan we not dispense with these preliminaries, monsieur? Or are they\r\nnecessary?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes felt the young woman was on her guard, so he replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; I will come to the point. Five years ago your father made\r\nthe acquaintance of a certain young man called Maxime Bermond, who was\r\nintroduced as a contractor or an architect, I am not sure which it was;\r\nbut it was one or the other. Monsieur Destange took a liking to the\r\nyoung man, and as the state of his health compelled him to retire from\r\nactive business, he entrusted to Monsieur Bermond the execution of\r\ncertain orders he had received from some of his old customers and which\r\nseemed to come within the scope of Monsieur Bermond\xe2\x80\x99s ability.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes stopped. It seemed to him that the girl\xe2\x80\x99s pallor had\r\nincreased. Yet there was not the slightest tremor in her voice when she\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know nothing about the circumstances to which you refer, monsieur,\r\nand I do not see in what way they can interest me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn this way, mademoiselle: You know, as well as I, that Maxime Bermond\r\nis also known by the name of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe laughed, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin? Maxime Bermond is Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin? Oh! no! It\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t possible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have the honor to inform you of that fact, and since you refuse to\r\nunderstand my meaning, I will add that Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin has found in this\r\nhouse a friend\xe2\x80\x94more than a friend\xe2\x80\x94and accomplice, blindly and\r\npassionately devoted to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWithout emotion, or at least with so little emotion that Sholmes was\r\nastonished at her self-control, she declared:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand your object, monsieur, and I do not care to; but I\r\ncommand you to say no more and leave this house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no intention of forcing my presence on you,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sholmes,\r\nwith equal sang-froid, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I shall not leave this house alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who will accompany you, monsieur?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, mademoiselle, we will leave this house together, and you will\r\nfollow me without one word of protest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe strange feature of the foregoing interview was the absolute\r\ncoolness of the two adversaries. It bore no resemblance to an\r\nimplacable duel between two powerful wills; but, judging solely from\r\ntheir attitude and the tone of their voices, an onlooker would have\r\nsupposed their conversation to be nothing more serious than a courteous\r\nargument over some impersonal subject.\r\n\r\nClotilde resumed her seat without deigning to reply to the last remark\r\nof Herlock Sholmes, except by a shrug of her shoulders. Sholmes looked\r\nat his watch and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is half-past ten. We will leave here in five minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf not, I shall go to Monsieur Destange, and tell him\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe truth. I will tell him of the vicious life of Maxime Bermond, and\r\nI will tell him of the double life of his accomplice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf his accomplice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, of the woman known as the blonde Lady, of the woman who was\r\nblonde.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat proofs will you give him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will take him to the rue Chalgrin, and show him the secret passage\r\nmade by Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s workmen,\xe2\x80\x94while doing the work of which he had\r\nthe control\xe2\x80\x94between the houses numbered 40 and 42; the passage which\r\nyou and he used two nights ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will then take Monsieur Destange to the house of Monsieur Detinan;\r\nwe will descend the servant\xe2\x80\x99s stairway which was used by you and Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin when you escaped from Ganimard, and we will search together the\r\nmeans of communication with the adjoining house, which fronts on the\r\nBoulevard des Batignolles, and not upon the rue Clapeyron.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will take Monsieur Destange to the ch\xc3\xa2teau de Crozon, and it will be\r\neasy for him, who knows the nature of the work performed by Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin in the restoration of the Ch\xc3\xa2teau, to discover the secret\r\npassages constructed there by his workmen. It will thus be established\r\nthat those passages allowed the blonde Lady to make a nocturnal visit\r\nto the Countess\xe2\x80\x99 room and take the blue diamond from the mantel; and,\r\ntwo weeks later, by similar means, to enter the room of Herr Bleichen\r\nand conceal the blue diamond in his tooth-powder\xe2\x80\x94a strange action, I\r\nconfess; a woman\xe2\x80\x99s revenge, perhaps; but I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncare.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Herlock Sholmes, in a more serious tone, \xe2\x80\x9cI will\r\ntake Monsieur Destange to 134 avenue Henri-Martin, and we will learn\r\nhow the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, keep quiet,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered the girl, struck with a sudden terror,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI forbid you!... you dare to say that it was I ... you accuse me?...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI accuse you of having killed the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, it is a lie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou killed the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, mademoiselle. You entered his service\r\nunder the name of Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat, for the purpose of stealing the\r\nblue diamond and you killed him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep quiet, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d she implored him. \xe2\x80\x9cSince you know so much, you\r\nmust know that I did not murder the baron.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not say that you murdered him, mademoiselle. Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec was\r\nsubject to fits of insanity that only Sister Auguste could control. She\r\ntold me so herself. In her absence, he must have attacked you, and in\r\nthe course of the struggle you struck him in order to save your own\r\nlife. Frightened at your awful situation, you rang the bell, and fled\r\nwithout even taking the blue diamond from the finger of your victim. A\r\nfew minutes later you returned with one of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s accomplices,\r\nwho was a servant in the adjoining house, you placed the baron on the\r\nbed, you put the room in order, but you were afraid to take the blue\r\ndiamond. Now, I have told you what happened on that night. I repeat,\r\nyou did not murder the baron, and yet it was your hand that struck the\r\nblow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had crossed them over her forehead\xe2\x80\x94those long delicate white\r\nhands\xe2\x80\x94and kept them thus for a long time. At last, loosening her\r\nfingers, she said, in a voice rent by anguish:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you intend to tell all that to my father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; and I will tell him that I have secured as witnesses:\r\nMademoiselle Gerbois, who will recognize the blonde Lady; Sister\r\nAuguste, who will recognize Antoinette Br\xc3\xa9hat; and the Countess de\r\nCrozon, who will recognize Madame de R\xc3\xa9al. That is what I shall tell\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not dare,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, recovering her self-possession in the\r\nface of an immediate peril.\r\n\r\nHe arose, and made a step toward the library. Clotilde stopped him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne moment, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe paused, reflected a moment, and then, perfect mistress of herself,\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are Herlock Sholmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do I want? I am fighting a duel with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, and I must\r\nwin. The contest is now drawing to a climax, and I have an idea that a\r\nhostage as precious as you will give me an important advantage over my\r\nadversary. Therefore, you will follow me, mademoiselle; I will entrust\r\nyou to one of my friends. As soon as the duel is ended, you will be set\r\nat liberty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all. I do not belong to the police service of this country,\r\nand, consequently, I do not consider that I am under any obligation ...\r\nto cause your arrest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe appeared to have come to a decision ... yet she required a\r\nmomentary respite. She closed her eyes, the better to concentrate her\r\nthoughts. Sholmes looked at her in surprise; she was now so tranquil\r\nand, apparently, indifferent to the dangers which threatened her.\r\nSholmes thought: Does she believe that she is in danger? Probably\r\nnot\xe2\x80\x94since Lupin protects her. She has confidence in him. She believes\r\nthat Lupin is omnipotent, and infallible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI told you that we would leave here in five\r\nminutes. That time has almost expired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you permit me to go to my room, monsieur, to get some necessary\r\narticles?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, mademoiselle; and I will wait for you in the rue\r\nMontchanin.
