Jeanniot, the concierge, is a friend of mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you know....\xe2\x80\x9d she said, visibly alarmed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know many things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. I will ring for the maid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe maid brought her hat and jacket. Then Sholmes said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must give Monsieur Destange some reason for our departure, and, if\r\npossible, let your excuse serve for an absence of several days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat shall not be necessary. I shall be back very soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey exchanged defiant glances and an ironic smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat faith you have in him!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbsolute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does everything well, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t he? He succeeds in everything he\r\nundertakes. And whatever he does receives your approval and\r\ncooperation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love him,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with a touch of passion in her voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you think that he will save you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shrugged her shoulders, and, approaching her father, she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to deprive you of Monsieur Stickmann. We are going to the\r\nNational Library.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will return for luncheon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps ... no, I think not ... but don\xe2\x80\x99t be uneasy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen she said to Sholmes, in a firm voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am at your service, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI warn you that if you attempt to escape, I shall call the police and\r\nhave you arrested. Do not forget that the blonde Lady is on parole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give you my word of honor that I shall not attempt to escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe you. Now, let us go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey left the house together, as he had predicted.\r\n\r\nThe automobile was standing where Sholmes had left it. As they\r\napproached it, Sholmes could hear the rumbling of the motor. He opened\r\nthe door, asked Clotilde to enter, and took a seat beside her. The\r\nmachine started at once, gained the exterior boulevards, the avenue\r\nHoche and the avenue de la Grande-Arm\xc3\xa9e. Sholmes was considering his\r\nplans. He thought:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGanimard is at home. I will leave the girl in his care. Shall I tell\r\nhim who she is? No, he would take her to prison at once, and that would\r\nspoil everything. When I am alone, I can consult my list of addresses\r\ntaken from the \xe2\x80\x98account M.B.,\xe2\x80\x99 and run them down. To-night, or\r\nto-morrow morning at the latest, I shall go to Ganimard, as I agreed,\r\nand deliver into his hands Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin and all his band.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rubbed his hand, gleefully, at the thought that his duel with Lupin\r\nwas drawing to a close, and he could not see any serious obstacle in\r\nthe way of his success. And, yielding to an irrepressible desire to\r\ngive vent to his feelings\xe2\x80\x94an unusual desire on his part\xe2\x80\x94he exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, mademoiselle, if I am unable to conceal my satisfaction and\r\ndelight. The battle has been a difficult one, and my success is,\r\ntherefore, more enjoyable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA legitimate success, monsieur, of which you have a just right to be\r\nproud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. But where are we going? The chauffeur must have\r\nmisunderstood my directions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment they were leaving Paris by the gate de Neuilly. That was\r\nstrange, as the rue Pergolese is not outside the fortifications.\r\nSholmes lowered the glass, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChauffeur, you have made a mistake.... Rue Pergolese!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man made no reply. Sholmes repeated, in a louder voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you to go to the rue Pergolese.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill the man did not reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! but you are deaf, my friend. Or is he doing it on purpose? We are\r\nvery much out of our way.... Rue Pergolese!... Turn back at once!...\r\nRue Pergolese!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe chauffeur made no sign of having heard the order. The Englishman\r\nfretted with impatience. He looked at Clotilde; a mysterious smile\r\nplayed upon her lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you laugh?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is an awkward mistake, but it won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhelp you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied.\r\n\r\nThen an idea occurred to him. He rose and made a careful scrutiny of\r\nthe chauffeur. His shoulders were not so broad; his bearing was not so\r\nstiff and mechanical. A cold perspiration covered his forehead and his\r\nhands clenched with sudden fear, as his mind was seized with the\r\nconviction that the chauffeur was Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Monsieur Sholmes, what do you think of our little ride?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDelightful, monsieur, really delightful,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sholmes.\r\n\r\nNever in his life had he experienced so much difficulty in uttering a\r\nfew simple words without a tremor, or without betraying his feelings in\r\nhis voice. But quickly, by a sort of reaction, a flood of hatred and\r\nrage burst its bounds, overcame his self-control, and, brusquely\r\ndrawing his revolver, he pointed it at Mademoiselle Destange.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLupin, stop, this minute, this second, or I fire at mademoiselle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI advise you to aim at the cheek if you wish to hit the temple,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nreplied Lupin, without turning his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaxime, don\xe2\x80\x99t go so fast,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clotilde, \xe2\x80\x9cthe pavement is slippery\r\nand I am very timid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was smiling; her eyes were fixed on the pavement, over which the\r\ncarriage was traveling at enormous speed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him stop! Let him stop!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes to her, wild with rage, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwarn you that I am desperate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe barrel of the revolver brushed the waving locks of her hair. She\r\nreplied, calmly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaxime is so imprudent. He is going so fast, I am really afraid of\r\nsome accident.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes returned the weapon to his pocket and seized the handle of the\r\ndoor, as if to alight, despite the absurdity of such an act. Clotilde\r\nsaid to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe careful, monsieur, there is an automobile behind us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe leaned over. There was an automobile close behind; a large machine\r\nof formidable aspect with its sharp prow and blood-red body, and\r\nholding four men clad in fur coats.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! I am well guarded,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cI may as well be patient.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe folded his arms across his chest with that proud air of submission\r\nso frequently assumed by heroes when fate has turned against them. And\r\nwhile they crossed the river Seine and rushed through Suresnes, Rueil\r\nand Chatou, motionless and resigned, controlling his actions and his\r\npassions, he tried to explain to his own satisfaction by what miracle\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had substituted himself for the chauffeur. It was quite\r\nimprobable that the honest-looking fellow he had selected on the\r\nboulevard that morning was an accomplice placed there in advance. And\r\nyet Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had received a warning in some way, and it must have\r\nbeen after he, Sholmes, had approached Clotilde in the house, because\r\nno one could have suspected his project prior to that time. Since then,\r\nSholmes had not allowed Clotilde out of his sight.\r\n\r\nThen an idea struck him: the telephone communication desired by\r\nClotilde and her conversation with the dressmaker. Now, it was all\r\nquite clear to him. Even before he had spoken to her, simply upon his\r\nrequest to speak to her as the new secretary of Monsieur Destange, she\r\nhad scented the danger, surmised the name and purpose of the visitor,\r\nand, calmly, naturally, as if she were performing a commonplace action\r\nof her every-day life, she had called Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin to her assistance by\r\nsome preconcerted signal.\r\n\r\nHow Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had come and caused himself to be substituted for the\r\nchauffeur were matters of trifling importance. That which affected\r\nSholmes, even to the point of appeasing his fury, was the recollection\r\nof that incident whereby an ordinary woman, a sweetheart it is true,\r\nmastering her nerves, controlling her features, and subjugating the\r\nexpression of her eyes, had completely deceived the astute detective\r\nHerlock Sholmes. How difficult to overcome an adversary who is aided by\r\nsuch confederates, and who, by the mere force of his authority,\r\ninspires in a woman so much courage and strength!\r\n\r\nThey crossed the Seine and climbed the hill at Saint-Germain; but, some\r\nfive hundred metres beyond that town, the automobile slackened its\r\nspeed. The other automobile advanced, and the two stopped, side by\r\nside. There was no one else in the neighborhood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Sholmes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, \xe2\x80\x9ckindly exchange to the other machine.\r\nOurs is really a very slow one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, calmly, convinced that he had no choice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlso, permit me to loan you a fur coat, as we will travel quite fast\r\nand the air is cool. And accept a couple of sandwiches, as we cannot\r\ntell when we will dine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe four men alighted from the other automobile. One of them\r\napproached, and, as he raised his goggles, Sholmes recognized in him\r\nthe gentleman in the frock coat that he had seen at the Hungarian\r\nrestaurant. Lupin said to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will return this machine to the chauffeur from whom I hired it. He\r\nis waiting in the first wine-shop to the right as you go up the rue\r\nLegendre. You will give him the balance of the thousand francs I\r\npromised him.... Ah! yes, kindly give your goggles to Monsieur\r\nSholmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe talked to Mlle. Destange for a moment, then took his place at the\r\nwheel and started, with Sholmes at his side and one of his men behind\r\nhim. Lupin had not exaggerated when he said \xe2\x80\x9cwe will travel quite\r\nfast.\xe2\x80\x9d From the beginning he set a breakneck pace. The horizon rushed\r\nto meet them, as if attracted by some mysterious force, and disappeared\r\ninstantly as though swallowed up in an abyss, into which many other\r\nthings, such as trees, houses, fields and forests, were hurled with the\r\ntumultuous fury and haste of a torrent as it approached the cataract.\r\n\r\nSholmes and Lupin did not exchange a word. Above their heads the leaves\r\nof the poplars made a great noise like the waves of the sea,\r\nrhythmically arranged by the regular spacing of the trees. And the\r\ntowns swept by like spectres: Manteo, Vernon, Gaillon. From one hill to\r\nthe other, from Bon-Secours to Canteleu, Rouen, its suburbs, its\r\nharbor, its miles of wharves, Rouen seemed like the straggling street\r\nof a country village. And this was Duclair, Caudebec, the country of\r\nCaux which they skimmed over in their terrific flight, and Lillebonne,\r\nand Quillebeuf. Then, suddenly, they found themselves on the banks of\r\nthe Seine, at the extremity of a little wharf, beside which lay a\r\nstaunch sea-going yacht that emitted great volumes of black smoke from\r\nits funnel.\r\n\r\nThe automobile stopped. In two hours they had traveled over forty\r\nleagues.\r\n\r\nA man, wearing a blue uniform and a goldlaced cap, came forward and\r\nsaluted. Lupin said to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll ready, captain? Did you receive my telegram?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I got it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs _The Swallow_ ready?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Monsieur Sholmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman looked around, saw a group of people on the terrace in\r\nfront of a caf\xc3\xa9, hesitated a moment, then, realizing that before he\r\ncould secure any assistance he would be seized, carried aboard and\r\nplaced in the bottom of the hold, he crossed the gang-plank and\r\nfollowed Lupin into the captain\xe2\x80\x99s cabin. It was quite a large room,\r\nscrupulously clean, and presented a cheerful appearance with its\r\nvarnished woodwork and polished brass. Lupin closed the door and\r\naddressed Sholmes abruptly, and almost rudely, as he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything? Come, be precise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis voice contained no longer that polite, if ironical, tone, which he\r\nhad affected when speaking to the Englishman. Now, his voice had the\r\nimperious tone of a master accustomed to command and accustomed to be\r\nobeyed\xe2\x80\x94even by a Herlock Sholmes. They measured each other by their\r\nlooks, enemies now\xe2\x80\x94open and implacable foes. Lupin spoke again, but in\r\na milder tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have grown weary of your pursuit, and do not intend to waste any\r\nmore time in avoiding the traps you lay for me. I warn you that my\r\ntreatment of you will depend on your reply. Now, what do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin controlled his temper and said, in a jerky manner:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell you what you know. You know that, under the name of Maxime\r\nBermond, I have ... _improved_ fifteen houses that were originally\r\nconstructed by Monsieur Destange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf those fifteen houses, you have seen four.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have a list of the other eleven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou made that list at Monsieur Destange\xe2\x80\x99s house on that night, no\r\ndoubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have an idea that, amongst those eleven houses, there is one\r\nthat I have kept for the use of myself and my friends, and you have\r\nintrusted to Ganimard the task of finding my retreat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does that signify?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt signifies that I choose to act alone, and do not want his help.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I have nothing to fear, since you are in my hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have nothing to fear as long as I remain in your hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that you will not remain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin approached the Englishman and, placing his hand on the\r\nlatter\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen, monsieur; I am not in a humor to argue with you, and,\r\nunfortunately for you, you are not in a position to choose. So let us\r\nfinish our business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are going to give me your word of honor that you will not try to\r\nescape from this boat until you arrive in English waters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI give you my word of honor that I shall escape if I have an\r\nopportunity,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the indomitable Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, sapristi! you know quite well that at a word from me you would\r\nsoon be rendered helpless. All these men will obey me blindly. At a\r\nsign from me they would place you in irons\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIrons can be broken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd throw you overboard ten miles from shore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can swim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hadn\xe2\x80\x99t thought of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, with a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me,\r\nmaster ... and let us finish. You will agree that I must take the\r\nmeasures necessary to protect myself and my friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly; but they will be useless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet you do not wish me to take them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is your duty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin opened the door and called the captain and two sailors. The\r\nlatter seized the Englishman, bound him hand and foot, and tied him to\r\nthe captain\xe2\x80\x99s bunk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat will do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was only on account of your obstinacy\r\nand the unusual gravity of the situation, that I ventured to offer you\r\nthis indignity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sailors retired. Lupin said to the captain:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet one of the crew remain here to look after Monsieur Sholmes, and\r\nyou can give him as much of your own company as possible. Treat him\r\nwith all due respect and consideration. He is not a prisoner, but a\r\nguest. What time have you, captain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive minutes after two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin consulted his watch, then looked at the clock that was attached\r\nto the wall of the cabin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive minutes past two is right. How long will it take you to reach\r\nSouthampton?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNine hours, easy going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMake it eleven. You must not land there until after the departure of\r\nthe midnight boat, which reaches Havre at eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning.\r\nDo you understand, captain? Let me repeat: As it would be very\r\ndangerous for all of us to permit Monsieur to return to France by that\r\nboat, you must not reach Southampton before one o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the\r\nmorning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAu revoir, master; next year, in this world or in the next.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUntil to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sholmes.\r\n\r\nA few minutes later Sholmes heard the automobile going away, and at the\r\nsame time the steam puffed violently in the depths of _The Swallow_.\r\nThe boat had started for England. About three o\xe2\x80\x99clock the vessel left\r\nthe mouth of the river and plunged into the open sea. At that moment\r\nSholmes was lying on the captain\xe2\x80\x99s bunk, sound asleep.\r\n\r\n\r\nNext morning\xe2\x80\x94it being the tenth and last day of the duel between\r\nSholmes and Lupin\xe2\x80\x94the _Echo de France_ published this interesting bit\r\nof news:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYesterday a judgment of ejectment was entered in the case of Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin against Herlock Sholmes, the English detective. Although signed\r\nat noon, the judgment was executed the same day. At one o\xe2\x80\x99clock this\r\nmorning Sholmes was landed at Southampton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\r\nSECOND ARREST OF ARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN.\r\n\r\n\r\nSince eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock a dozen moving-vans had encumbered the rue Crevaux\r\nbetween the avenue du Bois-de-Boulogne and the avenue Bugeaud. Mon.\r\nFelix Davey was leaving the apartment in which he lived on the fourth\r\nfloor of No. 8; and Mon. Dubreuil, who had united into a single\r\napartment the fifth floor of the same house and the fifth floor of the\r\ntwo adjoining houses, was moving on the same day\xe2\x80\x94a mere coincidence,\r\nsince the gentlemen were unknown to each other\xe2\x80\x94the vast collection of\r\nfurniture regarding which so many foreign agents visited him every day.\r\n\r\nA circumstance which had been noticed by some of the neighbors, but was\r\nnot spoken of until later, was this: None of the twelve vans bore the\r\nname and address of the owner, and none of the men accompanying them\r\nvisited the neighboring wine shops. They worked so diligently that the\r\nfurniture was all out by eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Nothing remained but those\r\nscraps of papers and rags that are always left behind in the corners of\r\nthe empty rooms.\r\n\r\nMon. Felix Davey, an elegant young man, dressed in the latest fashion,\r\ncarried in his hand a walking-stick, the weight of which indicated that\r\nits owner possessed extraordinary biceps\xe2\x80\x94Mon. Felix Davey walked calmly\r\naway and took a seat on a bench in the avenue du Bois-de-Boulogne\r\nfacing the rue Pergolese. Close to him a woman, dressed in a neat but\r\ninexpensive costume, was reading a newspaper, whilst a child was\r\nplaying with a shovel in a heap of sand.\r\n\r\nAfter a few minutes Felix Davey spoke to the woman, without turning his\r\nhead:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGanimard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWent out at nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo police headquarters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo telegram during the night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo they suspect you in the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I do some little things for Madame Ganimard, and she tells me\r\neverything her husband does. I have been with her all morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. Until further orders come here every day at eleven\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rose and walked away in the direction of the Dauphine gate, stopping\r\nat the Chinese pavilion, where he partook of a frugal repast consisting\r\nof two eggs, with some fruit and vegetables. Then he returned to the\r\nrue Crevaux and said to the concierge:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will just glance through the rooms and then give you the keys.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe finished his inspection of the room that he had used as a library;\r\nthen he seized the end of a gas-pipe, which hung down the side of the\r\nchimney. The pipe was bent and a hole made in the elbow. To this hole\r\nhe fitted a small instrument in the form of an ear-trumpet and blew\r\ninto it. A slight whistling sound came by way of reply. Placing the\r\ntrumpet to his mouth, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyone around, Dubreuil?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I come up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe returned the pipe to its place, saying to himself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow progressive we are! Our century abounds with little inventions\r\nwhich render life really charming and picturesque. And so amusing!...\r\nespecially when a person knows how to enjoy life as I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned one of the marble mouldings of the mantel, and the entire\r\nhalf of the mantel moved, and the mirror above it glided in invisible\r\ngrooves, disclosing an opening and the lower steps of a stairs built in\r\nthe very body of the chimney; all very clean and complete\xe2\x80\x94the stairs\r\nwere constructed of polished metal and the walls of white tiles. He\r\nascended the steps, and at the fifth floor there was the same opening\r\nin the chimney. Mon. Dubreuil was waiting for him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you finished in your rooms?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything cleared out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the three men on guard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; come on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey ascended to the upper floor by the same means, one after the\r\nother, and there found three men, one of whom was looking through the\r\nwindow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything new?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing, governor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll quiet in the street?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn ten minutes I will be ready to leave. You will go also. But in the\r\nmeantime if you see the least suspicious movement in the street, warn\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have my finger on the alarm-bell all the time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDubreuil, did you tell the moving men not to touch the wire of that\r\nbell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly; it is working all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all I want to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two gentlemen then descended to the apartment of Felix Davey and\r\nthe latter, after adjusting the marble mantel, exclaimed, joyfully:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDubreuil, I should like to see the man who is able to discover all the\r\ningenious devices, warning bells, net-works of electric wires and\r\nacoustic tubes, invisible passages, moving floors and hidden stairways.\r\nA real fairy-land!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat fame for Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFame I could well dispense with. It\xe2\x80\x99s a pity to be compelled to leave\r\na place so well equipped, and commence all over again, Dubreuil ... and\r\non a new model, of course, for it would never do to duplicate this.\r\nCurse Herlock Sholmes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he returned to Paris?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow could he? There has been only one boat come from Southampton and\r\nit left there at midnight; only one train from Havre, leaving there at\r\neight o\xe2\x80\x99clock this morning and due in Paris at eleven fifteen. As he\r\ncould not catch the midnight boat at Southampton\xe2\x80\x94and the instructions\r\nto the captain on that point were explicit\xe2\x80\x94he cannot reach France until\r\nthis evening via Newhaven and Dieppe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he will come back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; he never gives up. He will return to Paris; but it will be too\r\nlate. We will be far away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Mademoiselle Destange?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am to see her in an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt her house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! no; she will not return there for several days. But you, Dubreuil,\r\nyou must hurry. The loading of our goods will take a long time and you\r\nshould be there to look after them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure that we are not being watched?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy whom? I am not afraid of anyone but Sholmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDubreuil retired. Felix Davey made a last tour of the apartment, picked\r\nup two or three torn letters, then, noticing a piece of chalk, he took\r\nit and, on the dark paper of the drawing-room, drew a large frame and\r\nwrote within it the following:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, gentleman-burglar, lived here for five years at the\r\nbeginning of the twentieth century_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis little pleasantry seemed to please him very much. He looked at it\r\nfor a moment, whistling a lively air, then said to himself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow that I have placed myself in touch with the historians of future\r\ngenerations, I can go. You must hurry, Herlock Sholmes, as I shall\r\nleave my present abode in three minutes, and your defeat will be an\r\naccomplished fact.... Two minutes more! you are keeping me waiting,\r\nMonsieur Sholmes.... One minute more! Are you not coming? Well, then, I\r\nproclaim your downfall and my apotheosis. And now I make my escape.\r\nFarewell, kingdom of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin! I shall never see you again.\r\nFarewell to the fifty-five rooms of the six apartments over which I\r\nreigned! Farewell, my own royal bed chamber!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis outburst of joy was interrupted by the sharp ringing of a bell,\r\nwhich stopped twice, started again and then ceased. It was the alarm\r\nbell.\r\n\r\nWhat was wrong? What unforeseen danger? Ganimard? No; that wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\npossible!\r\n\r\nHe was on the point of returning to his library and making his escape.\r\nBut, first, he went to the window. There was no one in the street. Was\r\nthe enemy already in the house? He listened and thought he could\r\ndiscern certain confused sounds. He hesitated no longer. He ran to his\r\nlibrary, and as he crossed the threshold he heard the noise of a key\r\nbeing inserted in the lock of the vestibule door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured; \xe2\x80\x9cI have no time to lose. The house may be\r\nsurrounded. The servants\xe2\x80\x99 stairway\xe2\x80\x94impossible! Fortunately, there is\r\nthe chimney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pushed the moulding; it did not move. He made a greater effort\xe2\x80\x94still\r\nit refused to move. At the same time he had the impression that the\r\ndoor below opened and that he could hear footsteps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood God!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried; \xe2\x80\x9cI am lost if this cursed mechanism\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pushed with all his strength. Nothing moved\xe2\x80\x94nothing! By some\r\nincredible accident, by some evil stroke of fortune, the mechanism,\r\nwhich had worked only a few moments ago, would not work now.\r\n\r\nHe was furious. The block of marble remained immovable. He uttered\r\nfrightful imprecations on the senseless stone. Was his escape to be\r\nprevented by that stupid obstacle? He struck the marble wildly, madly;\r\nhe hammered it, he cursed it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Monsieur Lupin? You seem to be displeased about\r\nsomething.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin turned around. Herlock Sholmes stood before him!\r\n\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes!... Lupin gazed at him with squinting eyes as if his\r\nsight were defective and misleading. Herlock Sholmes in Paris! Herlock\r\nSholmes, whom he had shipped to England only the day before as a\r\ndangerous person, now stood before him free and victorious!... Ah! such\r\na thing was nothing less than a miracle; it was contrary to all natural\r\nlaws; it was the culmination of all that is illogical and abnormal....\r\nHerlock Sholmes here\xe2\x80\x94before his face!\r\n\r\nAnd when the Englishman spoke his words were tinged with that keen\r\nsarcasm and mocking politeness with which his adversary had so often\r\nlashed him. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Lupin, in the first place I have the honor to inform you that\r\nat this time and place I blot from my memory forever all thoughts of\r\nthe miserable night that you forced me to endure in the house of Baron\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99Hautrec, of the injury done to my friend Wilson, of my abduction in\r\nthe automobile, and of the voyage I took yesterday under your orders,\r\nbound to a very uncomfortable couch. But the joy of this moment effaces\r\nall those bitter memories. I forgive everything. I forget everything\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nwipe out the debt. I am paid\xe2\x80\x94and royally paid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin made no reply. So the Englishman continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think so yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe appeared to insist as if demanding an acquiescence, as a sort of\r\nreceipt in regard to the part.\r\n\r\nAfter a moment\xe2\x80\x99s reflection, during which the Englishman felt that he\r\nwas scrutinized to the very depth of his soul, Lupin declared:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI presume, monsieur, that your conduct is based upon serious motives?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery serious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fact that you have escaped from my captain and his crew is only a\r\nsecondary incident of our struggle. But the fact that you are here\r\nbefore me alone\xe2\x80\x94understand, alone\xe2\x80\x94face to face with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, leads\r\nme to think that your revenge is as complete as possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs complete as possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurrounded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe two adjoining houses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurrounded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe apartment above this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _three_ apartments on the fifth floor that were formerly occupied\r\nby Monsieur Dubreuil are surrounded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that you are captured, Monsieur Lupin\xe2\x80\x94absolutely captured.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe feelings that Sholmes had experienced during his trip in the\r\nautomobile were now suffered by Lupin, the same concentrated fury, the\r\nsame revolt, and also, let us admit, the same loyalty of submission to\r\nforce of circumstances. Equally brave in victory or defeat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur accounts are squared, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, frankly.\r\n\r\nThe Englishman was pleased with that confession. After a short silence\r\nLupin, now quite self-possessed, said smiling:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am not sorry! It becomes monotonous to win all the time.\r\nYesterday I had only to stretch out my hand to finish you forever.\r\nToday I belong to you. The game is yours.\xe2\x80\x9d Lupin laughed heartily and\r\nthen continued: \xe2\x80\x9cAt last the gallery will be entertained! Lupin in\r\nprison! How will he get out? In prison!... What an adventure!... Ah!\r\nSholmes, life is just one damn thing after another!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pressed his closed hands to his temples as if to suppress the\r\ntumultuous joy that surged within him, and his actions indicated that\r\nhe was moved by an uncontrollable mirth. At last, when he had recovered\r\nhis self-possession, he approached the detective and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now what are you waiting for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I waiting for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; Ganimard is here with his men\xe2\x80\x94why don\xe2\x80\x99t they come in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI asked him not to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he consented?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI accepted his services on condition that he would be guided by me.\r\nBesides, he thinks that Felix Davey is only an accomplice of Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will repeat my question in another form. Why did you come in\r\nalone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I wished to speak to you alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! ah! you have something to say to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat idea seemed to please Lupin immensely. There are certain\r\ncircumstances in which words are preferable to deeds.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Sholmes, I am sorry I cannot offer you an easy chair. How\r\nwould you like that broken box? Or perhaps you would prefer the window\r\nledge? I am sure a glass of beer would be welcome ... light or dark?...\r\nBut sit down, please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you; we can talk as well standing up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well\xe2\x80\x94proceed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will be brief. The object of my sojourn in France was not to\r\naccomplish your arrest. If I have been led to pursue you, it was\r\nbecause I saw no other way to achieve my real object.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich was?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo recover the blue diamond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blue diamond!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly; since the one found in Herr Bleichen\xe2\x80\x99s tooth-powder was\r\nonly an imitation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right; the genuine diamond was taken by the blonde Lady. I made\r\nan exact duplicate of it and then, as I had designs on other jewels\r\nbelonging to the Countess and as the Consul Herr Bleichen was already\r\nunder suspicion, the aforesaid blonde Lady, in order to avert\r\nsuspicion, slipped the false stone into the aforesaid Consul\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nluggage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhile you kept the genuine diamond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat diamond\xe2\x80\x94I want it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry, but it is impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have promised it to the Countess de Crozon. I must have it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow will you get it, since it is in my possession?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is precisely the reason\xe2\x80\x94because it is in your possession.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I am to give it to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVoluntarily?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will buy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lupin, in an access of mirth, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are certainly an\r\nEnglishman. You treat this as a matter of business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a matter of business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? what is your offer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe liberty of Mademoiselle Destange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer liberty?... I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know she was under arrest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will give Monsieur Ganimard the necessary information. When deprived\r\nof your protection, she can readily be taken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin laughed again, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear monsieur, you are offering me something you do not possess.\r\nMademoiselle Destange is in a place of safety, and has nothing to fear.\r\nYou must make me another offer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman hesitated, visibly embarrassed and vexed. Then, placing\r\nhis hand on the shoulder of his adversary, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if I should propose to you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy liberty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo ... but I can leave the room to consult with Ganimard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd leave me alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! mon dieu, what good would that be? The cursed mechanism will not\r\nwork,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, at the same time savagely pushing the moulding of\r\nthe mantel. He stifled a cry of surprise; this time fortune favored\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94the block of marble moved. It was his salvation; his hope of\r\nescape. In that event, why submit to the conditions imposed by Sholmes?\r\nHe paced up and down the room, as if he were considering his reply.\r\nThen, in his turn, he placed his hand on the shoulder of his adversary,\r\nand said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll things considered, Monsieur Sholmes, I prefer to do my own\r\nbusiness in my own way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t require anyone\xe2\x80\x99s assistance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen Ganimard gets his hand on you, it will be all over. You can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nescape from them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho knows?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, that is foolish. Every door and window is guarded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_The one I will choose_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMere words! Your arrest is as good as made.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! no\xe2\x80\x94not at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall keep the blue diamond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes looked at his watch, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is now ten minutes to three. At three o\xe2\x80\x99clock I shall call\r\nGanimard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, we have ten minutes to chat. And to satisfy my curiosity,\r\nMonsieur Sholmes, I should like to know how you procured my address and\r\nmy name of Felix Davey?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlthough his adversary\xe2\x80\x99s easy manner caused Sholmes some anxiety, he\r\nwas willing to give Lupin the desired information since it reflected\r\ncredit on his professional astuteness; so he replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour address? I got it from the blonde Lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClotilde!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHerself. Do you remember, yesterday morning, when I wished to take her\r\naway in the automobile, she telephoned to her dressmaker.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I understood, later, that you were the dressmaker. And last\r\nnight, on the boat, by exercising my memory\xe2\x80\x94and my memory is something\r\nI have good reason to be proud of\xe2\x80\x94I was able to recollect the last two\r\nfigures of your telephone number\xe2\x80\x9473. Then, as I possessed a list of the\r\nhouses you had \xe2\x80\x98improved,\xe2\x80\x99 it was an easy matter, on my arrival in\r\nParis at eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock this morning, to search in the telephone\r\ndirectory and find there the name and address of Felix Davey. Having\r\nobtained that information, I asked the aid of Monsieur Ganimard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdmirable! I congratulate you. But how did you manage to catch the\r\neight o\xe2\x80\x99clock train at Havre? How did you escape from _The Swallow_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ordered the captain not to reach Southampton before one o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\nHe landed me there at midnight. I was able to catch the twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock\r\nboat for Havre.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid the captain betray me? I can\xe2\x80\x99t believe it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, he did not betray you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was his watch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis watch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I put it ahead one hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the usual way, by turning the hands. We were sitting side by side,\r\ntalking, and I was telling him some funny stories.... Why! he never saw\r\nme do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravo! a very clever trick. I shall not forget it. But the clock that\r\nwas hanging on the wall of the cabin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! the clock was a more difficult matter, as my feet were tied, but\r\nthe sailor, who guarded me during the captain\xe2\x80\x99s absence, was kind\r\nenough to turn the hands for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe? Nonsense! He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t know the importance of his act. I told him I must\r\ncatch the first train for London, at any price, and ... he allowed\r\nhimself to be persuaded\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy means of\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy means of a slight gift, which the excellent fellow, loyal and true\r\nto his master, intends to send to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA mere trifle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blue diamond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blue diamond!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, the false stone that you substituted for the Countess\xe2\x80\x99 diamond.\r\nShe gave it to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a sudden explosion of violent laughter. Lupin laughed until\r\nthe tears started in his eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMon dieu, but it is funny! My false diamond palmed off on my innocent\r\nsailor! And the captain\xe2\x80\x99s watch! And the hands of the clock!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes felt that the duel between him and Lupin was keener than ever.\r\nHis marvellous instinct warned him that, behind his adversary\xe2\x80\x99s display\r\nof mirth, there was a shrewd intellect debating the ways and means to\r\nescape. Gradually Lupin approached the Englishman, who recoiled, and,\r\nunconsciously, slipped his hand into his watch-pocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is three o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Monsieur Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree o\xe2\x80\x99clock, already? What a pity! We were enjoying our chat so\r\nmuch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am waiting for your answer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy answer? Mon dieu! but you are particular!... And so this is the\r\nlast move in our little game\xe2\x80\x94and the stake is my liberty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr the blue diamond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. It\xe2\x80\x99s your play. What are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI play the king,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, as he fired his revolver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I the ace,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Lupin, as he struck at Sholmes with his fist.\r\n\r\nSholmes had fired into the air, as a signal to Ganimard, whose\r\nassistance he required. But Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s fist had caught Sholmes in the\r\nstomach, and caused him to double up with pain. Lupin rushed to the\r\nfireplace and set the marble slab in motion.... Too late! The door\r\nopened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurrender, Lupin, or I fire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard, doubtless stationed closer than Lupin had thought, Ganimard\r\nwas there, with his revolver turned on Lupin. And behind Ganimard there\r\nwere twenty men, strong and ruthless fellows, who would beat him like a\r\ndog at the least sign of resistance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHands down! I surrender!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, calmly; and he folded his arms\r\nacross his breast.\r\n\r\nEveryone was amazed. In the room, divested of its furniture and\r\nhangings, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s words sounded like an echo.... \xe2\x80\x9cI surrender!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n... It seemed incredible. No one would have been astonished if he had\r\nsuddenly vanished through a trap, or if a section of the wall had\r\nrolled away and allowed him to escape. But he surrendered!\r\n\r\nGanimard advanced, nervously, and with all the gravity that the\r\nimportance of the occasion demanded, he placed his hand on the shoulder\r\nof his adversary, and had the infinite pleasure of saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI arrest you, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrrr!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, \xe2\x80\x9cyou make me shiver, my dear Ganimard. What a\r\nlugubrious face! One would imagine you were speaking over the grave of\r\na friend. For Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake, don\xe2\x80\x99t assume such a funereal air.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI arrest you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let that worry you! In the name of the law, of which he is a\r\nwell-deserving pillar, Ganimard, the celebrated Parisian detective,\r\narrests the wicked Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. An historic event, of which you will\r\nappreciate the true importance.... And it is the second time that it\r\nhas happened. Bravo, Ganimard, you are sure of advancement in your\r\nchosen profession!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he held out his wrists for the hand-cuffs. Ganimard adjusted them\r\nin a most solemn manner. The numerous policemen, despite their\r\ncustomary presumption and the bitterness of their feelings toward\r\nLupin, conducted themselves with becoming modesty, astonished at being\r\npermitted to gaze upon that mysterious and intangible creature.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor Lupin,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed our hero, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat would your aristocratic friends\r\nsay if they should see you in this humiliating position?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pulled his wrists apart with all his strength. The veins in his\r\nforehead expanded. The links of the chain cut into his flesh. The chain\r\nfell off\xe2\x80\x94broken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother, comrades, that one was useless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey placed two on him this time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot be too careful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, counting the detectives and policemen, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many are you, my friends? Twenty-five? Thirty? That\xe2\x80\x99s too many. I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t do anything. Ah! if there had been only fifteen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was something fascinating about Lupin; it was the fascination of\r\nthe great actor who plays his r\xc3\xb4le with spirit and understanding,\r\ncombined with assurance and ease. Sholmes regarded him as one might\r\nregard a beautiful painting with a due appreciation of all its\r\nperfection in coloring and technique. And he really thought that it was\r\nan equal struggle between those thirty men on one side, armed as they\r\nwere with all the strength and majesty of the law, and, on the other\r\nside, that solitary individual, unarmed and handcuffed. Yes, the two\r\nsides were well-matched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, master,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin to the Englishman, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is your work.\r\nThanks to you, Lupin is going to rot on the damp straw of a dungeon.\r\nConfess that your conscience pricks you a little, and that your soul is\r\nfilled with remorse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn spite of himself, Sholmes shrugged his shoulders, as if to say:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s your own fault.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever! never!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cGive you the blue diamond? Oh! no, it\r\nhas cost me too much trouble. I intend to keep it. On my occasion of my\r\nfirst visit to you in London\xe2\x80\x94which will probably be next month\xe2\x80\x94I will\r\ntell you my reasons. But will you be in London next month? Or do you\r\nprefer Vienna? Or Saint Petersburg?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Lupin received a surprise. A bell commenced to ring. It was not\r\nthe alarm-bell, but the bell of the telephone which was located between\r\nthe two windows of the room and had not yet been removed.\r\n\r\nThe telephone! Ah! Who could it be? Who was about to fall into this\r\nunfortunate trap? Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin exhibited an access of rage against the\r\nunlucky instrument as if he would like to break it into a thousand\r\npieces and thus stifle the mysterious voice that was calling for him.\r\nBut it was Ganimard who took down the receiver, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello!... Hello!... number 648.73 ... yes, this is it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Sholmes stepped up, and, with an air of authority, pushed Ganimard\r\naside, took the receiver, and covered the transmitter with his\r\nhandkerchief in order to obscure the tone of his voice. At that moment\r\nhe glanced toward Lupin, and the look which they exchanged indicated\r\nthat the same idea had occurred to each of them, and that they foresaw\r\nthe ultimate result of that theory: it was the blonde Lady who was\r\ntelephoning. She wished to telephone to Felix Davey, or rather to\r\nMaxime Bermond, and it was to Sholmes she was about to speak. The\r\nEnglishman said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello ... Hello!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, after a silence, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is I, Maxime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe drama had commenced and was progressing with tragic precision.\r\nLupin, the irrepressible and nonchalant Lupin, did not attempt to\r\nconceal his anxiety, and he strained every nerve in a desire to hear\r\nor, at least, to divine the purport of the conversation. And Sholmes\r\ncontinued, in reply to the mysterious voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello!... Hello!... Yes, everything has been moved, and I am just\r\nready to leave here and meet you as we agreed.... Where?... Where you\r\nare now.... Don\xe2\x80\x99t believe that he is here yet!...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes stopped, seeking for words. It was clear that he was trying to\r\nquestion the girl without betraying himself, and that he was ignorant\r\nof her whereabouts. Moreover, Ganimard\xe2\x80\x99s presence seemed to embarrass\r\nhim.... Ah! if some miracle would only interrupt that cursed\r\nconversation! Lupin prayed for it with all his strength, with all the\r\nintensity of his incited nerves! After a momentary pause, Sholmes\r\ncontinued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHello!... Hello!... Do you hear me?... I can\xe2\x80\x99t hear you very well....\r\nCan scarcely make out what you say.... Are you listening? Well, I think\r\nyou had better return home.... No danger now.... But he is in England!\r\nI have received a telegram from Southampton announcing his arrival.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe sarcasm of those words! Sholmes uttered them with an inexpressible\r\ncomfort. And he added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, don\xe2\x80\x99t lose any time. I will meet you there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe hung up the receiver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Ganimard, can you furnish me with three men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the blonde Lady, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know who she is, and where she is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood! That settles Monsieur Lupin.... Folenfant, take two men, and go\r\nwith Monsieur Sholmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman departed, accompanied by the three men.\r\n\r\nThe game was ended. The blonde Lady was, also, about to fall into the\r\nhands of the Englishman. Thanks to his commendable persistence and to a\r\ncombination of fortuitous circumstances, the battle had resulted in a\r\nvictory for the detective, and in irreparable disaster for Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Sholmes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman stopped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin was clearly shattered by this final blow. His forehead was marked\r\nby deep wrinkles. He was sullen and dejected. However, he pulled\r\nhimself together, and, notwithstanding his defeat, he exclaimed, in a\r\ncheerful tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will concede that fate has been against me. A few minutes ago, it\r\nprevented my escape through that chimney, and delivered me into your\r\nhands. Now, by means of the telephone, it presents you with the blonde\r\nLady. I submit to its decrees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean that I am ready to re-open our negotiation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes took Ganimard aside and asked, in a manner that did not permit\r\na reply, the authority to exchange a few words with the prisoner. Then\r\nhe approached Lupin, and said, in a sharp, nervous tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle Destange\xe2\x80\x99s liberty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know the price.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you accept?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I accept your terms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Englishman, in surprise, \xe2\x80\x9cbut ... you refused ... for\r\nyourself\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I can look out for myself, Monsieur Sholmes, but now the question\r\nconcerns a young woman ... and a woman I love. In France, understand,\r\nwe have very decided ideas about such things. And Lupin has the same\r\nfeelings as other people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke with simplicity and candor. Sholmes replied by an almost\r\nimperceptible inclination of his head, and murmured:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, the blue diamond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake my cane, there, at the end of the mantel. Press on the head of\r\nthe cane with one hand, and, with the other, turn the iron ferrule at\r\nthe bottom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHolmes took the cane and followed the directions. As he did so, the\r\nhead of the cane divided and disclosed a cavity which contained a small\r\nball of wax which, in turn, enclosed a diamond. He examined it. It was\r\nthe blue diamond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Lupin, Mademoiselle Destange is free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs her future safety assured? Has she nothing to fear from you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither from me, nor anyone else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can you manage it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite easily. I have forgotten her name and address.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. And au revoir\xe2\x80\x94for I will see you again, sometime, Monsieur\r\nSholmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no doubt of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen followed an animated conversation between Sholmes and Ganimard,\r\nwhich was abruptly terminated by the Englishman, who said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry, Monsieur Ganimard, that we cannot agree on that\r\npoint, but I have no time to waste trying to convince you. I leave for\r\nEngland within an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut ... the blonde Lady?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know such a person.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet, a moment ago\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must take the affair as it stands. I have delivered Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\ninto your hands. Here is the blue diamond, which you will have the\r\npleasure of returning to the Countess de Crozon. What more do you\r\nwant?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blonde Lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFind her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes pulled his cap down over his forehead and walked rapidly away,\r\nlike a man who is accustomed to go as soon as his business is finished.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBon voyage, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lupin, \xe2\x80\x9cand, believe me, I shall never\r\nforget the friendly way in which our little business affairs have been\r\narranged. My regards to Monsieur Wilson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNot receiving any reply, Lupin added, sneeringly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what is called \xe2\x80\x98taking British leave.\xe2\x80\x99 Ah! their insular\r\ndignity lacks the flower of courtesy by which we are distinguished.\r\nConsider for a moment, Ganimard, what a charming exit a Frenchman would\r\nhave made under similar circumstances! With what exquisite courtesy he\r\nwould have masked his triumph!... But, God bless me, Ganimard, what are\r\nyou doing? Making a search? Come, what\xe2\x80\x99s the use? There is nothing\r\nleft\xe2\x80\x94not even a scrap of paper. I assure you my archives are in a safe\r\nplace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not so sure of that,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cI must search\r\neverything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin submitted to the operation. Held by two detectives and surrounded\r\nby the others, he patiently endured the proceedings for twenty minutes,\r\nthen he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHurry up, Ganimard, and finish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are in a hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I am. An important appointment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the police station?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; in the city.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! at what time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is three o\xe2\x80\x99clock now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust so; I will be late. And punctuality is one of my virtues.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, give me five minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a second more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am doing my best to expedite\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! don\xe2\x80\x99t talk so much.... Still searching that cupboard? It is\r\nempty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere are some letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld invoices, I presume!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; a packet tied with a ribbon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA red ribbon? Oh! Ganimard, for God\xe2\x80\x99s sake, don\xe2\x80\x99t untie it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom a woman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA woman of the world?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe best in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame Ganimard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery funny! very funny!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the detective.\r\n\r\nAt that moment the men, who had been sent to search the other rooms,\r\nreturned and announced their failure to find anything. Lupin laughed\r\nand said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cParbleu! Did you expect to find my visiting list, or evidence of my\r\nbusiness relations with the Emperor of Germany? But I can tell you what\r\nyou should investigate, Ganimard: All the little mysteries of this\r\napartment. For instance, that gas-pipe is a speaking tube. That chimney\r\ncontains a stairway. That wall is hollow. And the marvellous system of\r\nbells! Ah! Ganimard, just press that button!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard obeyed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you hear anything?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither did I. And yet you notified my aeronaut to prepare the\r\ndirigible balloon which will soon carry us into the clouds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome!\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard, who had completed his search; \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ve had enough\r\nnonsense\xe2\x80\x94let\xe2\x80\x99s be off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe started away, followed by his men. Lupin did not move. His guardians\r\npushed him in vain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you refuse to go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. But it depends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere you want to take me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the station-house, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I refuse to go. I have no business there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you crazy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I not tell you that I had an important appointment?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLupin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Ganimard, I have an appointment with the blonde Lady, and do you\r\nsuppose I would be so discourteous as to cause her a moment\xe2\x80\x99s anxiety?\r\nThat would be very ungentlemanly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen, Lupin,\xe2\x80\x9d said the detective, who was becoming annoyed by this\r\npersiflage; \xe2\x80\x9cI have been very patient with you, but I will endure no\r\nmore. Follow me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible; I have an appointment and I shall keep it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the last time\xe2\x80\x94follow me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIm-pos-sible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt a sign from Ganimard two men seized Lupin by the arms; but they\r\nreleased him at once, uttering cries of pain. Lupin had thrust two long\r\nneedles into them. The other men now rushed at Lupin with cries of rage\r\nand hatred, eager to avenge their comrades and to avenge themselves for\r\nthe many affronts he had heaped upon them; and now they struck and beat\r\nhim to their heart\xe2\x80\x99s desire. A violent blow on the temple felled Lupin\r\nto the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you hurt him you will answer to me,\xe2\x80\x9d growled Ganimard, in a rage.\r\n\r\nHe leaned over Lupin to ascertain his condition. Then, learning that he\r\nwas breathing freely, Ganimard ordered his men to carry the prisoner by\r\nthe head and feet, while he himself supported the body.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo gently, now!... Don\xe2\x80\x99t jolt him. Ah! the brutes would have killed\r\nhim.... Well, Lupin, how goes it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone too well, Ganimard ... you let them knock me out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was your own fault; you were so obstinate,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nI hope they didn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had left the apartment and were now on the landing. Lupin groaned\r\nand stammered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGanimard ... the elevator ... they are breaking my bones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good idea, an excellent idea,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cBesides, the\r\nstairway is too narrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe summoned the elevator. They placed Lupin on the seat with the\r\ngreatest care. Ganimard took his place beside him and said to his men:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo down the stairs and wait for me below. Understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard closed the door of the elevator. Suddenly the elevator shot\r\nupward like a balloon released from its cable. Lupin burst into a fit\r\nof sardonic laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood God!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Ganimard, as he made a frantic search in the dark for\r\nthe button of descent. Having found it, he cried:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fifth floor! Watch the door of the fifth floor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis assistants clambered up the stairs, two and three steps at a time.\r\nBut this strange circumstance happened: The elevator seemed to break\r\nthrough the ceiling of the last floor, disappeared from the sight of\r\nGanimard\xe2\x80\x99s assistants, suddenly made its appearance on the upper\r\nfloor\xe2\x80\x94the servants\xe2\x80\x99 floor\xe2\x80\x94and stopped. Three men were there waiting for\r\nit. They opened the door. Two of them seized Ganimard, who, astonished\r\nat the sudden attack, scarcely made any defence. The other man carried\r\noff Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI warned you, Ganimard ... about the dirigible balloon. Another time,\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t be so tender-hearted. And, moreover, remember that Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t allow himself to be struck and knocked down without sufficient\r\nreason. Adieu.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door of the elevator was already closed on Ganimard, and the\r\nmachine began to descend; and it all happened so quickly that the old\r\ndetective reached the ground floor as soon as his assistants. Without\r\nexchanging a word they crossed the court and ascended the servants\xe2\x80\x99\r\nstairway, which was the only way to reach the servants\xe2\x80\x99 floor through\r\nwhich the escape had been made.\r\n\r\nA long corridor with several turns and bordered with little numbered\r\nrooms led to a door that was not locked. On the other side of this door\r\nand, therefore, in another house there was another corridor with\r\nsimilar turns and similar rooms, and at the end of it a servants\xe2\x80\x99\r\nstairway. Ganimard descended it, crossed a court and a vestibule and\r\nfound himself in the rue Picot. Then he understood the situation: the\r\ntwo houses, built the entire depth of the lots, touched at the rear,\r\nwhile the fronts of the houses faced upon two streets that ran parallel\r\nto each other at a distance of more than sixty metres apart.\r\n\r\nHe found the concierge and, showing his card, enquired:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid four men pass here just now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; the two servants from the fourth and fifth floors, with two\r\nfriends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho lives on the fourth and fifth floors?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo men named Fauvel and their cousins, whose name is Provost. They\r\nmoved to-day, leaving the two servants, who went away just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Ganimard; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat a grand opportunity we have missed! The\r\nentire band lived in these houses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he sank down on a chair in despair.\r\n\r\n\r\nForty minutes later two gentlemen were driven up to the station of the\r\nNorthern Railway and hurried to the Calais express, followed by a\r\nporter who carried their valises. One of them had his arm in a sling,\r\nand the pallor of his face denoted some illness. The other man was in a\r\njovial mood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must hurry, Wilson, or we will miss the train.... Ah! Wilson, I\r\nshall never forget these ten days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither will I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! it was a great struggle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuperb!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA few repulses, here and there\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf no consequence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, at last, victory all along the line. Lupin arrested! The blue\r\ndiamond recovered!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy arm broken!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does a broken arm count for in such a victory as that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEspecially when it is my arm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! yes, don\xe2\x80\x99t you remember, Wilson, that it was at the very time you\r\nwere in the pharmacy, suffering like a hero, that I discovered the clue\r\nto the whole mystery?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow lucky!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe doors of the carriages were being closed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll aboard. Hurry up, gentlemen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe porter climbed into an empty compartment and placed their valises\r\nin the rack, whilst Sholmes assisted the unfortunate Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Wilson? You\xe2\x80\x99re not done up, are you? Come, pull\r\nyour nerves together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy nerves are all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what is it, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have only one hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat of it?\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not the only one\r\nwho has had a broken arm. Cheer up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes handed the porter a piece of fifty centimes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, Monsieur Sholmes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the porter.\r\n\r\nThe Englishman looked at him; it was Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou!... you!\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered, absolutely astounded.\r\n\r\nAnd Wilson brandished his sound arm in the manner of a man who\r\ndemonstrates a fact as he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou! you! but you were arrested! Sholmes told me so. When he left you\r\nGanimard and thirty men had you in charge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin folded his arms and said, with an air of indignation:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you suppose I would let you go away without bidding you adieu?\r\nAfter the very friendly relations that have always existed between us!\r\nThat would be discourteous and ungrateful on my part.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe train whistled. Lupin continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, but have you everything you need? Tobacco and\r\nmatches ... yes ... and the evening papers? You will find in them an\r\naccount of my arrest\xe2\x80\x94your last exploit, Monsieur Sholmes. And now, au\r\nrevoir. Am delighted to have made your acquaintance. And if ever I can\r\nbe of any service to you, I shall be only too happy....\xe2\x80\x9d He leaped to\r\nthe platform and closed the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdieu,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, waving his handkerchief. \xe2\x80\x9cAdieu.... I shall write\r\nto you.... You will write also, eh? And your arm broken, Wilson.... I\r\nam truly sorry.... I shall expect to hear from both of you. A postal\r\ncard, now and then, simply address: Lupin, Paris. That is\r\nsufficient.... Adieu.... See you soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\nTHE JEWISH LAMP.\r\n\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes and Wilson were sitting in front of the fireplace, in\r\ncomfortable armchairs, with the feet extended toward the grateful\r\nwarmth of a glowing coke fire.\r\n\r\nSholmes\xe2\x80\x99 pipe, a short brier with a silver band, had gone out. He\r\nknocked out the ashes, filled it, lighted it, pulled the skirts of his\r\ndressing-gown over his knees, and drew from his pipe great puffs of\r\nsmoke, which ascended toward the ceiling in scores of shadow rings.\r\n\r\nWilson gazed at him, as a dog lying curled up on a rug before the fire\r\nmight look at his master, with great round eyes which have no hope\r\nother than to obey the least gesture of his owner. Was the master going\r\nto break the silence? Would he reveal to Wilson the subject of his\r\nreverie and admit his satellite into the charmed realm of his thoughts?\r\n\r\nWhen Sholmes had maintained his silent attitude for some time. Wilson\r\nventured to speak:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything seems quiet now. Not the shadow of a case to occupy our\r\nleisure moments.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes did not reply, but the rings of smoke emitted by Sholmes were\r\nbetter formed, and Wilson observed that his companion drew considerable\r\npleasure from that trifling fact\xe2\x80\x94an indication that the great man was\r\nnot absorbed in any serious meditation. Wilson, discouraged, arose and\r\nwent to the window.\r\n\r\nThe lonely street extended between the gloomy fa\xc3\xa7ades of grimy houses,\r\nunusually gloomy this morning by reason of a heavy downfall of rain. A\r\ncab passed; then another. Wilson made an entry of their numbers in his\r\nmemorandum-book. One never knows!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cthe postman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man entered, shown in by the servant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo registered letters, sir ... if you will sign, please?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes signed the receipts, accompanied the man to the door, and was\r\nopening one of the letters as he returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to please you,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Wilson, after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis letter contains a very interesting proposition. You are anxious\r\nfor a case\xe2\x80\x94here\xe2\x80\x99s one. Read\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson read:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur,\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cI desire the benefit of your services and experience. I have been\r\n    the victim of a serious theft, and the investigation has as yet\r\n    been unsuccessful. I am sending to you by this mail a number of\r\n    newspapers which will inform you of the affair, and if you will\r\n    undertake the case, I will place my house at your disposal and ask\r\n    you to fill in the enclosed check, signed by me, for whatever sum\r\n    you require for your expenses.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cKindly reply by telegraph, and much oblige,\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour humble servant,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBaron Victor d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c18 rue Murillo, Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cthat sounds good ... a little trip to Paris\r\n... and why not, Wilson? Since my famous duel with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, I have\r\nnot had an excuse to go there. I should be pleased to visit the capital\r\nof the world under less strenuous conditions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe tore the check into four pieces and, while Wilson, whose arm had not\r\nyet regained its former strength, uttered bitter words against Paris\r\nand the Parisians, Sholmes opened the second envelope. Immediately, he\r\nmade a gesture of annoyance, and a wrinkle appeared on his forehead\r\nduring the reading of the letter; then, crushing the paper into a ball,\r\nhe threw it, angrily, on the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? What\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Wilson, anxiously.\r\n\r\nHe picked up the ball of paper, unfolded it, and read, with increasing\r\namazement:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy Dear Monsieur:\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cYou know full well the admiration I have for you and the interest\r\n    I take in your renown. Well, believe me, when I warn you to have\r\n    nothing whatever to do with the case on which you have just now\r\n    been called to Paris. Your intervention will cause much harm; your\r\n    efforts will produce a most lamentable result; and you will be\r\n    obliged to make a public confession of your defeat.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cHaving a sincere desire to spare you such humiliation, I implore\r\n    you, in the name of the friendship that unites us, to remain\r\n    peacefully reposing at your own fireside.\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cMy best wishes to Monsieur Wilson, and, for yourself, the sincere\r\n    regards of your devoted\r\n\r\n\r\nARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin!\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Wilson, astounded.\r\n\r\nSholmes struck the table with his fist, and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! he is pestering me already, the fool! He laughs at me as if I were\r\na schoolboy! The public confession of my defeat! Didn\xe2\x80\x99t I force him to\r\ndisgorge the blue diamond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense! Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin is not afraid, and this taunting letter proves\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how did he know that the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle had written to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do I know about it? You do ask some stupid questions, my boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought ... I supposed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? That I am a clairvoyant? Or a sorcerer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but I have seen you do some marvellous things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo person can perform _marvellous_ things. I no more than you. I\r\nreflect, I deduct, I conclude\xe2\x80\x94that is all; but I do not divine. Only\r\nfools divine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson assumed the attitude of a whipped cur, and resolved not to make\r\na fool of himself by trying to divine why Sholmes paced the room with\r\nquick, nervous strides. But when Sholmes rang for the servant and\r\nordered his valise, Wilson thought that he was in possession of a\r\nmaterial fact which gave him the right to reflect, deduct and conclude\r\nthat his associate was about to take a journey. The same mental\r\noperation permitted him to assert, with almost mathematical exactness:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSholmes, you are going to Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPossibly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s affront impels you to go, rather than the desire to assist\r\nthe Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPossibly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSholmes, I shall go with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh; ah! my old friend,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, interrupting his walking,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou are not afraid that your right arm will meet the same fate as your\r\nleft?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can happen to me? You will be there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the way to talk, Wilson. We will show that clever Frenchman\r\nthat he made a mistake when he threw his glove in our faces. Be quick,\r\nWilson, we must catch the first train.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout waiting for the papers the baron has sent you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat good are they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will send a telegram.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; if you do that, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin will know of my arrival. I wish to\r\navoid that. This time, Wilson, we must fight under cover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThat afternoon, the two friends embarked at Dover. The passage was a\r\ndelightful one. In the train from Calais to Paris, Sholmes had three\r\nhours sound sleep, while Wilson guarded the door of the compartment.\r\n\r\nSholmes awoke in good spirits. He was delighted at the idea of another\r\nduel with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, and he rubbed his hands with the satisfied air\r\nof a man who looks forward to a pleasant vacation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt last!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Wilson, \xe2\x80\x9cwe are getting to work again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he rubbed his hands with the same satisfied air.\r\n\r\nAt the station, Sholmes took the wraps and, followed by Wilson, who\r\ncarried the valises, he gave up his tickets and started off briskly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine weather, Wilson.... Blue sky and sunshine! Paris is giving us a\r\nroyal reception.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but what a crowd!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the better, Wilson, we will pass unnoticed. No one will\r\nrecognize us in such a crowd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this Monsieur Sholmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped, somewhat puzzled. Who the deuce could thus address him by\r\nhis name? A woman stood beside him; a young girl whose simple dress\r\noutlined her slender form and whose pretty face had a sad and anxious\r\nexpression.
