She repeated her enquiry:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are Monsieur Sholmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he still remained silent, as much from confusion as from a habit of\r\nprudence, the girl asked a third time:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave I the honor of addressing Monsieur Sholmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want?\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, testily, considering the incident a\r\nsuspicious one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must listen to me, Monsieur Sholmes, as it is a serious matter. I\r\nknow that you are going to the rue Murillo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know ... I know ... rue Murillo ... number 18. Well, you must not go\r\n... no, you must not. I assure you that you will regret it. Do not\r\nthink that I have any interest in the matter. I do it because it is\r\nright ... because my conscience tells me to do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes tried to get away, but she persisted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I beg of you, don\xe2\x80\x99t neglect my advice.... Ah! if I only knew how\r\nto convince you! Look at me! Look into my eyes! They are sincere ...\r\nthey speak the truth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe gazed at Sholmes, fearlessly but innocently, with those beautiful\r\neyes, serious and clear, in which her very soul seemed to be reflected.\r\n\r\nWilson nodded his head, as he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle looks honest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she implored, \xe2\x80\x9cand you must have confidence\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have confidence in you, mademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Wilson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, how happy you make me! And so has your friend? I feel it ... I am\r\nsure of it! What happiness! Everything will be all right now!... What a\r\ngood idea of mine!... Ah! yes, there is a train for Calais in twenty\r\nminutes. You will take it.... Quick, follow me ... you must come this\r\nway ... there is just time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe tried to drag them along. Sholmes seized her arm, and in as gentle\r\na voice as he could assume, said to her:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, mademoiselle, if I cannot yield to your wishes, but I never\r\nabandon a task that I have once undertaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beseech you ... I implore you.... Ah if you could only understand!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes passed outside and walked away at a quick pace. Wilson said to\r\nthe girl:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave no fear ... he will be in at the finish. He never failed yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he ran to overtake Sholmes.\r\n\r\nHERLOCK SHOLMES\xe2\x80\x94ARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN.\r\n\r\nThese words, in great black letters, met their gaze as soon as they\r\nleft the railway station. A number of sandwich-men were parading\r\nthrough the street, one behind the other, carrying heavy canes with\r\niron ferrules with which they struck the pavement in harmony, and, on\r\ntheir backs, they carried large posters, on which one could read the\r\nfollowing notice:\r\n\r\nTHE MATCH BETWEEN HERLOCK SHOLMES\r\nAND ARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN. ARRIVAL OF THE ENGLISH\r\nCHAMPION. THE GREAT DETECTIVE ATTACKS\r\nTHE MYSTERY OF THE RUE MURILLO. READ THE\r\nDETAILS IN THE \xe2\x80\x9cECHO DE FRANCE.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nWilson shook his head, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at that, Sholmes, and we thought we were traveling incognito! I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be surprised to find the republican guard waiting for us at\r\nthe rue Murillo to give us an official reception with toasts and\r\nchampagne.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, when you get funny, you get beastly funny,\xe2\x80\x9d growled Sholmes.\r\n\r\nThen he approached one of the sandwich-men with the obvious intention\r\nof seizing him in his powerful grip and crushing him, together with his\r\ninfernal sign-board. There was quite a crowd gathered about the men,\r\nreading the notices, and joking and laughing.\r\n\r\nRepressing a furious access of rage, Sholmes said to the man:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did they hire you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long have you been parading?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the boards were ready before that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, they were ready when we went to the agency this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo then it appears that Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin had foreseen that he, Sholmes,\r\nwould accept the challenge. More than that, the letter written by Lupin\r\nshowed that he was eager for the fray and that he was prepared to\r\nmeasure swords once more with his formidable rival. Why? What motive\r\ncould Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin have in renewing the struggle?\r\n\r\nSholmes hesitated for a moment. Lupin must be very confident of his\r\nsuccess to show so much insolence in advance; and was not he, Sholmes,\r\nfalling into a trap by rushing into the battle at the first call for\r\nhelp?\r\n\r\nHowever, he called a carriage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Wilson!... Driver, 18 rue Murillo!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, with an\r\noutburst of his accustomed energy. With distended veins and clenched\r\nfists, as if he were about to engage in a boxing bout, he jumped into\r\nthe carriage.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe rue Murillo is bordered with magnificent private residences, the\r\nrear of which overlook the Parc Monceau. One of the most pretentious of\r\nthese houses is number 18, owned and occupied by the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle\r\nand furnished in a luxurious manner consistent with the owner\xe2\x80\x99s taste\r\nand wealth. There was a courtyard in front of the house, and, in the\r\nrear, a garden well filled with trees whose branches mingle with those\r\nof the park.\r\n\r\nAfter ringing the bell, the two Englishmen were admitted, crossed the\r\ncourtyard, and were received at the door by a footman who showed them\r\ninto a small parlor facing the garden in the rear of the house. They\r\nsat down and, glancing about, made a rapid inspection of the many\r\nvaluable objects with which the room was filled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything very choice,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Wilson, \xe2\x80\x9cand in the best of taste.\r\nIt is a safe deduction to make that those who had the leisure to\r\ncollect these articles must now be at least fifty years of age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door opened, and the Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle entered, followed by his\r\nwife. Contrary to the deduction made by Wilson, they were both quite\r\nyoung, of elegant appearance, and vivacious in speech and action. They\r\nwere profuse in their expressions of gratitude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo kind of you to come! Sorry to have caused you so much trouble! The\r\ntheft now seems of little consequence, since it has procured us this\r\npleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow charming these French people are!\xe2\x80\x9d thought Wilson, evolving one of\r\nhis commonplace deductions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut time is money,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the baron, \xe2\x80\x9cespecially your time,\r\nMonsieur Sholmes. So I will come to the point. Now, what do you think\r\nof the affair? Do you think you can succeed in it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore I can answer that I must know what it is about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you knew.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; so I must ask you for full particulars, even to the smallest\r\ndetail. First, what is the nature of the case?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA theft.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did it take place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLast Saturday,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the baron, \xe2\x80\x9cor, at least, some time during\r\nSaturday night or Sunday morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was six days ago. Now, you can tell me all about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the first place, monsieur, I must tell you that my wife and I,\r\nconforming to the manner of life that our position demands, go out very\r\nlittle. The education of our children, a few receptions, and the care\r\nand decoration of our house\xe2\x80\x94such constitutes our life; and nearly all\r\nour evenings are spent in this little room, which is my wife\xe2\x80\x99s boudoir,\r\nand in which we have gathered a few artistic objects. Last Saturday\r\nnight, about eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock, I turned off the electric lights, and my\r\nwife and I retired, as usual, to our room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is your room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt adjoins this. That is the door. Next morning, that is to say,\r\nSunday morning, I arose quite early. As Suzanne, my wife, was still\r\nasleep, I passed into the boudoir as quietly as possible so as not to\r\nwake her. What was my astonishment when I found that window open\xe2\x80\x94as we\r\nhad left it closed the evening before!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA servant\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one enters here in the morning until we ring. Besides, I always\r\ntake the precaution to bolt the second door which communicates with the\r\nante-chamber. Therefore, the window must have been opened from the\r\noutside. Besides, I have some evidence of that: the second pane of\r\nglass from the right\xe2\x80\x94close to the fastening\xe2\x80\x94had been cut.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what does that window overlook?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs you can see for yourself, it opens on a little balcony, surrounded\r\nby a stone railing. Here, we are on the first floor, and you can see\r\nthe garden behind the house and the iron fence which separates it from\r\nthe Parc Monceau. It is quite certain that the thief came through the\r\npark, climbed the fence by the aid of a ladder, and thus reached the\r\nterrace below the window.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is quite certain, you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, in the soft earth on either side of the fence, they found the\r\ntwo holes made by the bottom of the ladder, and two similar holes can\r\nbe seen below the window. And the stone railing of the balcony shows\r\ntwo scratches which were doubtless made by the contact of the ladder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the Parc Monceau closed at night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but if it were, there is a house in course of erection at number\r\n14, and a person could enter that way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes reflected for a few minutes, and then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us come down to the theft. It must have been committed in this\r\nroom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; there was here, between that twelfth century Virgin and that\r\ntabernacle of chased silver, a small Jewish lamp. It has disappeared.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is that all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!... And what is a Jewish lamp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of those copper lamps used by the ancient Jews, consisting of a\r\nstandard which supported a bowl containing the oil, and from this bowl\r\nprojected several burners intended for the wicks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon the whole, an object of small value.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo great value, of course. But this one contained a secret\r\nhiding-place in which we were accustomed to place a magnificent jewel,\r\na chimera in gold, set with rubies and emeralds, which was of great\r\nvalue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you hide it there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I can\xe2\x80\x99t give any reason, monsieur, unless it was an odd fancy to\r\nutilize a hiding-place of that kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid anyone know it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one\xe2\x80\x94except the thief,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cOtherwise he would not have\r\ntaken the trouble to steal the lamp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course. But how could he know it, as it was only by accident that\r\nthe secret mechanism of the lamp was revealed to us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA similar accident has revealed it to some one else ... a servant ...\r\nor an acquaintance. But let us proceed: I suppose the police have been\r\nnotified?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. The examining magistrate has completed his investigation. The\r\nreporter-detectives attached to the leading newspapers have also made\r\ntheir investigations. But, as I wrote to you, it seems to me the\r\nmystery will never be solved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes arose, went to the window, examined the casement, the balcony,\r\nthe terrace, studied the scratches on the stone railing with his\r\nmagnifying-glass, and then requested Mon. d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle to show him the\r\ngarden.\r\n\r\nOutside, Sholmes sat down in a rattan chair and gazed at the roof of\r\nthe house in a dreamy way. Then he walked over to the two little wooden\r\nboxes with which they had covered the holes made in the ground by the\r\nbottom of the ladder with a view of preserving them intact. He raised\r\nthe boxes, kneeled on the ground, scrutinized the holes and made some\r\nmeasurements. After making a similar examination of the holes near the\r\nfence, he and the baron returned to the boudoir where Madame\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle was waiting for them. After a short silence Sholmes said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the very outset of your story, baron, I was surprised at the very\r\nsimple methods employed by the thief. To raise a ladder, cut a\r\nwindow-pane, select a valuable article, and walk out again\xe2\x80\x94no, that is\r\nnot the way such things are done. All that is too plain, too simple.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat the Jewish lamp was stolen under the direction of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the baron.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but he did not do it himself, as no one came from the outside.\r\nPerhaps a servant descended from the upper floor by means of a\r\nwaterspout that I noticed when I was in the garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes you think so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin would not leave this room empty-handed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEmpty-handed! But he had the lamp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that would not have prevented his taking that snuff-box, set with\r\ndiamonds, or that opal necklace. When he leaves anything, it is because\r\nhe can\xe2\x80\x99t carry it away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the marks of the ladder outside?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA false scent. Placed there simply to avert suspicion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the scratches on the balustrade?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA farce! They were made with a piece of sandpaper. See, here are\r\nscraps of the paper that I picked up in the garden.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what about the marks made by the bottom of the ladder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCounterfeit! Examine the two rectangular holes below the window, and\r\nthe two holes near the fence. They are of a similar form, but I find\r\nthat the two holes near the house are closer to each other than the two\r\nholes near the fence. What does that fact suggest? To me, it suggested\r\nthat the four holes were made by a piece of wood prepared for the\r\npurpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe better proof would be the piece of wood itself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cI found it in the garden, under the box of\r\na laurel tree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe baron bowed to Sholmes in recognition of his skill. Only forty\r\nminutes had elapsed since the Englishman had entered the house, and he\r\nhad already exploded all the theories theretofore formed, and which had\r\nbeen based on what appeared to be obvious and undeniable facts. But\r\nwhat now appeared to be the real facts of the case rested upon a more\r\nsolid foundation, to-wit, the astute reasoning of a Herlock Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe accusation which you make against one of our household is a very\r\nserious matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said the baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cOur servants have been with us a\r\nlong time and none of them would betray our trust.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf none of them has betrayed you, how can you explain the fact that I\r\nreceived this letter on the same day and by the same mail as the letter\r\nyou wrote to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe handed to the baroness the letter that he had received from Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin. She exclaimed, in amazement:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin! How could he know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you tell anyone that you had written to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the baron. \xe2\x80\x9cThe idea occurred to us the other evening\r\nat the dinner-table.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore the servants?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, only our two children. Oh, no ... Sophie and Henriette had left\r\nthe table, hadn\xe2\x80\x99t they, Suzanne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle, after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s reflection, replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, they had gone to Mademoiselle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe governess, Mademoiselle Alice Demun.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes she take her meals with you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Her meals are served in her room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson had an idea. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe letter written to my friend Herlock Sholmes was posted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho posted it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDominique, who has been my valet for twenty years,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the baron.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny search in that direction would be a waste of time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne never wastes his time when engaged in a search,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson,\r\nsententiously.\r\n\r\nThis preliminary investigation now ended, and Sholmes asked permission\r\nto retire.\r\n\r\nAt dinner, an hour later, he saw Sophie and Henriette, the two children\r\nof the family, one was six and the other eight years of age. There was\r\nvery little conversation at the table. Sholmes responded to the\r\nfriendly advances of his hosts in such a curt manner that they were\r\nsoon reduced to silence. When the coffee was served, Sholmes swallowed\r\nthe contents of his cup, and rose to take his leave.\r\n\r\nAt that moment, a servant entered with a telephone message addressed to\r\nSholmes. He opened it, and read:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have my enthusiastic admiration. The results attained by you in so\r\nshort a time are simply marvellous. I am dismayed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nSholmes made a gesture of indignation and handed the message to the\r\nbaron, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think now, monsieur? Are the walls of your house furnished\r\nwith eyes and ears?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the baron, in amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor do I; but I do understand that Lupin has knowledge of everything\r\nthat occurs in this house. He knows every movement, every word. There\r\nis no doubt of it. But how does he get his information? That is the\r\nfirst mystery I have to solve, and when I know that I will know\r\neverything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThat night, Wilson retired with the clear conscience of a man who has\r\nperformed his whole duty and thus acquired an undoubted right to sleep\r\nand repose. So he fell asleep very quickly, and was soon enjoying the\r\nmost delightful dreams in which he pursued Lupin and captured him\r\nsingle-handed; and the sensation was so vivid and exciting that it woke\r\nhim from his sleep. Someone was standing at his bedside. He seized his\r\nrevolver, and cried:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t move, Lupin, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce! Wilson, what do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! it is you, Sholmes. Do you want me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to show you something. Get up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes led him to the window, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook!... on the other side of the fence....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the park?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. What do you see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you do see something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! of course, a shadow ... two of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, close to the fence. See, they are moving. Come, quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nQuickly they descended the stairs, and reached a room which opened into\r\nthe garden. Through the glass door they could see the two shadowy forms\r\nin the same place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very strange,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it seems to me I can hear a\r\nnoise inside the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInside the house? Impossible! Everybody is asleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, listen\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment a low whistle came from the other side of the fence, and\r\nthey perceived a dim light which appeared to come from the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe baron must have turned on the light in his room. It is just above\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat must have been the noise you heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Wilson. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps they\r\nare watching the fence also.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen there was a second whistle, softer than before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand it; I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, irritably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo more do I,\xe2\x80\x9d confessed Wilson.\r\n\r\nSholmes turned the key, drew the bolt, and quietly opened the door. A\r\nthird whistle, louder than before, and modulated to another form. And\r\nthe noise above their heads became more pronounced. Sholmes said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to be on the balcony outside the boudoir window.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe put his head through the half-opened door, but immediately recoiled,\r\nwith a stifled oath. Then Wilson looked. Quite close to them there was\r\na ladder, the upper end of which was resting on the balcony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is someone in the boudoir. That is\r\nwhat we heard. Quick, let us remove the ladder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut at that instant a man slid down the ladder and ran toward the spot\r\nwhere his accomplices were waiting for him outside the fence. He\r\ncarried the ladder with him. Sholmes and Wilson pursued the man and\r\novertook him just as he was placing the ladder against the fence. From\r\nthe other side of the fence two shots were fired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWounded?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Wilson.\r\n\r\nWilson seized the man by the body and tried to hold him, but the man\r\nturned and plunged a knife into Wilson\xe2\x80\x99s breast. He uttered a groan,\r\nstaggered and fell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamnation!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cif they have killed him I will kill\r\nthem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe laid Wilson on the grass and rushed toward the ladder. Too late\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nman had climbed the fence and, accompanied by his confederates, had\r\nfled through the bushes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilson, Wilson, it is not serious, hein? Merely a scratch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe house door opened, and Monsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle appeared, followed by\r\nthe servants, carrying candles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the baron. \xe2\x80\x9cIs Monsieur Wilson wounded?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! it\xe2\x80\x99s nothing\xe2\x80\x94a mere scratch,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Sholmes, trying to deceive\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\nThe blood was flowing profusely, and Wilson\xe2\x80\x99s face was livid. Twenty\r\nminutes later the doctor ascertained that the point of the knife had\r\npenetrated to within an inch and a half of the heart.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn inch and a half of the heart! Wilson always was lucky!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nSholmes, in an envious tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLucky ... lucky....\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the doctor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course! Why, with his robust constitution he will soon be out\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSix weeks in bed and two months of convalescence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot more?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, unless complications set in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! the devil! what does he want complications for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFully reassured, Sholmes joined the baron in the boudoir. This time the\r\nmysterious visitor had not exercised the same restraint. Ruthlessly, he\r\nhad laid his vicious hand upon the diamond snuff-box, upon the opal\r\nnecklace, and, in a general way, upon everything that could find a\r\nplace in the greedy pockets of an enterprising burglar.\r\n\r\nThe window was still open; one of the window-panes had been neatly cut;\r\nand, in the morning, a summary investigation showed that the ladder\r\nbelonged to the house then in course of construction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, you can see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mon. d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle, with a touch of irony, \xe2\x80\x9cit\r\nis an exact repetition of the affair of the Jewish lamp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if we accept the first theory adopted by the police.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you adopted it yet? Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t this second theft shatter your\r\ntheory in regard to the first?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt only confirms it, monsieur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is incredible! You have positive evidence that last night\xe2\x80\x99s theft\r\nwas committed by an outsider, and yet you adhere to your theory that\r\nthe Jewish lamp was stolen by someone in the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am sure of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you explain it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not explain anything, monsieur; I have established two facts\r\nwhich do not appear to have any relation to each other, and yet I am\r\nseeking the missing link that connects them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis conviction seemed to be so earnest and positive that the baron\r\nsubmitted to it, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, we will notify the police\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the Englishman, quickly, \xe2\x80\x9cnot at all! I intend\r\nto ask for their assistance when I need it\xe2\x80\x94but not before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the attack on your friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s of no consequence. He is only wounded. Secure the license of\r\nthe doctor. I shall be responsible for the legal side of the affair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next two days proved uneventful. Yet Sholmes was investigating the\r\ncase with a minute care, and with a sense of wounded pride resulting\r\nfrom that audacious theft, committed under his nose, in spite of his\r\npresence and beyond his power to prevent it. He made a thorough\r\ninvestigation of the house and garden, interviewed the servants, and\r\npaid lengthy visits to the kitchen and stables. And, although his\r\nefforts were fruitless, he did not despair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will succeed,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, \xe2\x80\x9cand the solution must be sought within\r\nthe walls of this house. This affair is quite different from that of\r\nthe blonde Lady, where I had to work in the dark, on unknown ground.\r\nThis time I am on the battlefield itself. The enemy is not the elusive\r\nand invisible Lupin, but the accomplice, in flesh and blood, who lives\r\nand moves within the confines of this house. Let me secure the\r\nslightest clue and the game is mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat clue was furnished to him by accident.\r\n\r\nOn the afternoon of the third day, when he entered a room located above\r\nthe boudoir, which served as a study for the children, he found\r\nHenriette, the younger of the two sisters. She was looking for her\r\nscissors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cI make papers like that you received\r\nthe other evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe other evening?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, just as dinner was over, you received a paper with marks on it\r\n... you know, a telegram.... Well, I make them, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe left the room. To anyone else these words would seem to be nothing\r\nmore than the insignificant remark of a child, and Sholmes himself\r\nlistened to them with a distracted air and continued his investigation.\r\nBut, suddenly, he ran after the child, and overtook her at the head of\r\nthe stairs. He said to her:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you paste stamps and marks on papers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenriette, very proudly, replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I cut them out and paste them on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho taught you that little game?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle ... my governess ... I have seen her do it often. She\r\ntakes words out of the newspapers and pastes them\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does she make out of them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTelegrams and letters that she sends away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes returned to the study, greatly puzzled by the\r\ninformation and seeking to draw from it a logical deduction. There was\r\na pile of newspapers on the mantel. He opened them and found that many\r\nwords and, in some places, entire lines had been cut out. But, after\r\nreading a few of the word\xe2\x80\x99s which preceded or followed, he decided that\r\nthe missing words had been cut out at random\xe2\x80\x94probably by the child. It\r\nwas possible that one of the newspapers had been cut by mademoiselle;\r\nbut how could he assure himself that such was the case?\r\n\r\nMechanically, Sholmes turned over the school-books on the table; then\r\nothers which were lying on the shelf of a bookcase. Suddenly he uttered\r\na cry of joy. In a corner of the bookcase, under a pile of old exercise\r\nbooks, he found a child\xe2\x80\x99s alphabet-book, in which the letters were\r\nornamented with pictures, and on one of the pages of that book he\r\ndiscovered a place where a word had been removed. He examined it. It\r\nwas a list of the days of the week. Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday, etc.\r\nThe word \xe2\x80\x9cSaturday\xe2\x80\x9d was missing. Now, the theft of the Jewish lamp had\r\noccurred on a Saturday night.\r\n\r\nSholmes experienced that slight fluttering of the heart which always\r\nannounced to him, in the clearest manner, that he had discovered the\r\nroad which leads to victory. That ray of truth, that feeling of\r\ncertainty, never deceived him.\r\n\r\nWith nervous fingers he hastened to examine the balance of the book.\r\nVery soon he made another discovery. It was a page composed of capital\r\nletters, followed by a line of figures. Nine of those letters and three\r\nof those figures had been carefully cut out. Sholmes made a list of the\r\nmissing letters and figures in his memorandum book, in alphabetical and\r\nnumerical order, and obtained the following result:\r\n\r\nCDEHNOPEZ\xe2\x80\x94237.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell? at first sight, it is a rather formidable puzzle,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut, by transposing the letters and using all of them, is it possible\r\nto form one, two or three complete words?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes tried it, in vain.\r\n\r\nOnly one solution seemed possible; it constantly appeared before him,\r\nno matter which way he tried to juggle the letters, until, at length,\r\nhe was satisfied it was the true solution, since it harmonized with the\r\nlogic of the facts and the general circumstances of the case.\r\n\r\nAs that page of the book did not contain any duplicate letters it was\r\nprobable, in fact quite certain, that the words he could form from\r\nthose letters would be incomplete, and that the original words had been\r\ncompleted with letters taken from other pages. Under those conditions\r\nhe obtained the following solution, errors and omissions excepted:\r\n\r\nREPOND Z\xe2\x80\x94CH\xe2\x80\x94237.\r\n\r\nThe first word was quite clear: r\xc3\xa9pondez [reply], a letter E is missing\r\nbecause it occurs twice in the word, and the book furnished only one\r\nletter of each kind.\r\n\r\nAs to the second incomplete word, no doubt it formed, with the aid of\r\nthe number 237, an address to which the reply was to be sent. They\r\nappointed Saturday as the time, and requested a reply to be sent to the\r\naddress CH. 237.\r\n\r\nOr, perhaps, CH. 237 was an address for a letter to be sent to the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cgeneral delivery\xe2\x80\x9d of some postoffice, or, again, they might form a\r\npart of some incomplete word. Sholmes searched the book once more, but\r\ndid not discover that any other letters had been removed. Therefore,\r\nuntil further orders, he decided to adhere to the foregoing\r\ninterpretation.\r\n\r\nHenriette returned and observed what he was doing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmusing, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, very amusing,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, have you any other papers?...\r\nOr, rather, words already cut out that I can paste?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPapers?... No.... And Mademoiselle wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she has scolded me already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I have told you some things ... and she says that a person\r\nshould never tell things about those they love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are quite right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHenriette was delighted to receive his approbation, in fact so highly\r\npleased that she took from a little silk bag that was pinned to her\r\ndress some scraps of cloth, three buttons, two cubes of sugar and,\r\nlastly, a piece of paper which she handed to Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee, I give it to you just the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was the number of a cab\xe2\x80\x948,279.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did this number come from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt fell out of her pocketbook.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSunday, at mass, when she was taking out some sous for the\r\ncollection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly! And now I shall tell you how to keep from being scolded\r\nagain. Do not tell Mademoiselle that you saw me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes then went to Mon. d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle and questioned him in regard to\r\nMademoiselle. The baron replied, indignantly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice Demun! How can you imagine such a thing? It is utterly\r\nimpossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long has she been in your service?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a year, but there is no one in the house in whom I have greater\r\nconfidence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy have I not seen her yet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has been away for a few days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut she is here now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; since her return she has been watching at the bedside of your\r\nfriend. She has all the qualities of a nurse ... gentle ... thoughtful\r\n... Monsieur Wilson seems much pleased....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, who had completely neglected to inquire about his\r\nfriend. After a moment\xe2\x80\x99s reflection he asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid she go out on Sunday morning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe day after the theft?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe baron called his wife and asked her. She replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle went to the eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock mass with the children, as\r\nusual.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut before that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore that? No.... Let me see!... I was so upset by the theft ... but\r\nI remember now that, on the evening before, she asked permission to go\r\nout on Sunday morning ... to see a cousin who was passing through\r\nParis, I think. But, surely, you don\xe2\x80\x99t suspect her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not ... but I would like to see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went to Wilson\xe2\x80\x99s room. A woman dressed in a gray cloth dress, as in\r\nthe hospitals, was bending over the invalid, giving him a drink. When\r\nshe turned her face Sholmes recognized her as the young girl who had\r\naccosted him at the railway station.\r\n\r\nAlice Demun smiled sweetly; her great serious, innocent eyes showed no\r\nsign of embarrassment. The Englishman tried to speak, muttered a few\r\nsyllables, and stopped. Then she resumed her work, acting quite\r\nnaturally under Sholmes\xe2\x80\x99 astonished gaze, moved the bottles, unrolled\r\nand rolled cotton bandages, and again regarded Sholmes with her\r\ncharming smile of pure innocence.\r\n\r\nHe turned on his heels, descended the stairs, noticed Mon.\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle\xe2\x80\x99s automobile in the courtyard, jumped into it, and went to\r\nLevallois, to the office of the cab company whose address was printed\r\non the paper he had received from Henriette. The man who had driven\r\ncarriage number 8,279 on Sunday morning not being there, Sholmes\r\ndismissed the automobile and waited for the man\xe2\x80\x99s return. He told\r\nSholmes that he had picked up a woman in the vicinity of the Parc\r\nMonceau, a young woman dressed in black, wearing a heavy veil, and,\r\napparently, quite nervous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid she have a package?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, quite a long package.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you take her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAvenue des Ternes, corner of the Place Saint-Ferdinand. She remained\r\nthere about ten minutes, and then returned to the Parc Monceau.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould you recognize the house in the avenue des Ternes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cParbleu! Shall I take you there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPresently. First take me to 36 quai des Orf\xc3\xa8vres.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the police office he saw Detective Ganimard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Ganimard, are you at liberty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it has anything to do with Lupin\xe2\x80\x94no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has something to do with Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I do not go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! you surrender\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI bow to the inevitable. I am tired of the unequal struggle, in which\r\nwe are sure to be defeated. Lupin is stronger than I am\xe2\x80\x94stronger than\r\nthe two of us; therefore, we must surrender.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not surrender.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will make you, as he has all others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you would be pleased to see it\xe2\x80\x94eh, Ganimard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt all events, it is true,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard, frankly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd since you are\r\ndetermined to pursue the game, I will go with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTogether they entered the carriage and were driven to the avenue des\r\nTernes. Upon their order the carriage stopped on the other side of the\r\nstreet, at some distance from the house, in front of a little caf\xc3\xa9, on\r\nthe terrace of which the two men took seats amongst the shrubbery. It\r\nwas commencing to grow dark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWaiter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9csome writing material.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe wrote a note, recalled the waiter and gave him the letter with\r\ninstructions to deliver it to the concierge of the house which he\r\npointed out.\r\n\r\nIn a few minutes the concierge stood before them. Sholmes asked him if,\r\non the Sunday morning, he had seen a young woman dressed in black.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn black? Yes, about nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock. She went to the second floor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you seen her often?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but for some time\xe2\x80\x94well, during the last few weeks, I have seen her\r\nalmost every day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd since Sunday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly once ... until to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! Did she come to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is here now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she came about ten minutes ago. Her carriage is standing in the\r\nPlace Saint-Ferdinand, as usual. I met her at the door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is the occupant of the second floor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are two: a modiste, Mademoiselle Langeais, and a gentleman who\r\nrented two furnished rooms a month ago under the name of Bresson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you say \xe2\x80\x98under the name\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I have an idea that it is an assumed name. My wife takes care\r\nof his rooms, and ... well, there are not two shirts there with the\r\nsame initials.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he there much of the time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; he is nearly always out. He has not been here for three days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas he here on Saturday night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaturday night?... Let me think.... Yes, Saturday night, he came in\r\nand stayed all night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat sort of a man is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can scarcely answer that. He is so changeable. He is, by\r\nturns, big, little, fat, thin ... dark and light. I do not always\r\nrecognize him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard and Sholmes exchanged looks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is he, all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the concierge, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is the girl now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMademoiselle had just emerged from the house and was walking toward her\r\ncarriage in the Place Saint-Ferdinand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there is Monsieur Bresson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Bresson? Which is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe man with the parcel under his arm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he is not looking after the girl. She is going to her carriage\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have never seen them together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two detectives had arisen. By the light of the street-lamps they\r\nrecognized the form of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, who had started off in a direction\r\nopposite to that taken by the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich will you follow?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Ganimard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will follow him, of course. He\xe2\x80\x99s the biggest game.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will follow the girl,\xe2\x80\x9d proposed Ganimard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, quickly, who did not wish to disclose the\r\ngirl\xe2\x80\x99s identity to Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9cI know where to find her. Come with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey followed Lupin at a safe distance, taking care to conceal\r\nthemselves as well as possible amongst the moving throng and behind the\r\nnewspaper kiosks. They found the pursuit an easy one, as he walked\r\nsteadily forward without turning to the right or left, but with a\r\nslight limp in the right leg, so slight as to require the keen eye of a\r\nprofessional observer to detect it. Ganimard observed it, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is pretending to be lame. Ah! if we could only collect two or three\r\npolicemen and pounce on our man! We run a chance to lose him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut they did not meet any policemen before they reached the Porte des\r\nTernes, and, having passed the fortifications, there was no prospect of\r\nreceiving any assistance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had better separate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cas there are so few people on\r\nthe street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were now on the Boulevard Victor-Hugo. They walked one on each\r\nside of the street, and kept well in the shadow of the trees. They\r\ncontinued thus for twenty minutes, when Lupin turned to the left and\r\nfollowed the Seine. Very soon they saw him descend to the edge of the\r\nriver. He remained there only a few seconds, but they could not observe\r\nhis movements. Then Lupin retraced his steps. His pursuers concealed\r\nthemselves in the shadow of a gateway. Lupin passed in front of them.\r\nHis parcel had disappeared. And as he walked away another man emerged\r\nfrom the shelter of a house and glided amongst the trees.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe seems to be following him also,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, in a low voice.\r\n\r\nThe pursuit continued, but was now embarrassed by the presence of the\r\nthird man. Lupin returned the same way, passed through the Porte des\r\nTernes, and re-entered the house in the avenue des Ternes.\r\n\r\nThe concierge was closing the house for the night when Ganimard\r\npresented himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the concierge, \xe2\x80\x9cI was putting out the gas on the landing\r\nwhen he closed and bolted his door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there any person with him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; he has no servant. He never eats here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there a servants\xe2\x80\x99 stairway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGanimard said to Sholmes:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had better stand at the door of his room while you go for the\r\ncommissary of police in the rue Demours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if he should escape during that time?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhile I am here! He can\xe2\x80\x99t escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne to one, with Lupin, is not an even chance for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can\xe2\x80\x99t force the door. I have no right to do that, especially\r\nat night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes shrugged his shoulders and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you arrest Lupin no one will question the methods by which you\r\nmade the arrest. However, let us go up and ring, and see what happens\r\nthen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey ascended to the second floor. There was a double door at the left\r\nof the landing. Ganimard rang the bell. No reply. He rang again. Still\r\nno reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us go in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, come on,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Ganimard.\r\n\r\nYet, they stood still, irresolute. Like people who hesitate when they\r\nought to accomplish a decisive action they feared to move, and it\r\nseemed to them impossible that Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin was there, so close to\r\nthem, on the other side of that fragile door that could be broken down\r\nby one blow of the fist. But they knew Lupin too well to suppose that\r\nhe would allow himself to be trapped in that stupid manner. No, no\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nthousand times, no\xe2\x80\x94Lupin was no longer there. Through the adjoining\r\nhouses, over the roofs, by some conveniently prepared exit, he must\r\nhave already made his escape, and, once more, it would only be Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshadow that they would seize.\r\n\r\nThey shuddered as a slight noise, coming from the other side of the\r\ndoor, reached their ears. Then they had the impression, amounting\r\nalmost to a certainty, that he was there, separated from them by that\r\nfrail wooden door, and that he was listening to them, that he could\r\nhear them.\r\n\r\nWhat was to be done? The situation was a serious one. In spite of their\r\nvast experience as detectives, they were so nervous and excited that\r\nthey thought they could hear the beating of their own hearts. Ganimard\r\nquestioned Sholmes by a look. Then he struck the door a violent blow\r\nwith his fist. Immediately they heard the sound of footsteps,\r\nconcerning which there was no attempt at concealment.\r\n\r\nGanimard shook the door. Then he and Sholmes, uniting their efforts,\r\nrushed at the door, and burst it open with their shoulders. Then they\r\nstood still, in surprise. A shot had been fired in the adjoining room.\r\nAnother shot, and the sound of a falling body.\r\n\r\nWhen they entered they saw the man lying on the floor with his face\r\ntoward the marble mantel. His revolver had fallen from his hand.\r\nGanimard stooped and turned the man\xe2\x80\x99s head. The face was covered with\r\nblood, which was flowing from two wounds, one in the cheek, the other\r\nin the temple.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t recognize him for blood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo matter!\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know? You haven\xe2\x80\x99t even looked at him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think that Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin is the kind of a man that would kill\r\nhimself?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sholmes, with a sneer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we thought we recognized him outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe thought so, because the wish was father to the thought. That man\r\nhas us bewitched.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it must be one of his accomplices.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe accomplices of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin do not kill themselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, who is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey searched the corpse. In one pocket Herlock Sholmes found an empty\r\npocketbook; in another Ganimard found several louis. There were no\r\nmarks of identification on any part of his clothing. In a trunk and two\r\nvalises they found nothing but wearing apparel. On the mantel there was\r\na pile of newspapers. Ganimard opened them. All of them contained\r\narticles referring to the theft of the Jewish lamp.\r\n\r\nAn hour later, when Ganimard and Sholmes left the house, they had\r\nacquired no further knowledge of the strange individual who had been\r\ndriven to suicide by their untimely visit.\r\n\r\nWho was he? Why had he killed himself? What was his connection with the\r\naffair of the Jewish lamp? Who had followed him on his return from the\r\nriver? The situation involved many complex questions\xe2\x80\x94many mysteries\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes went to bed in a very bad humor. Early next morning he\r\nreceived the following telephonic message:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin has the honor to inform you of his tragic death in the\r\nperson of Monsieur Bresson, and requests the honor of your presence at\r\nthe funeral service and burial, which will be held at the public\r\nexpense on Thursday, 25 June.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\nTHE SHIPWRECK.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I don\xe2\x80\x99t like, Wilson,\xe2\x80\x9d said Herlock Sholmes, after he had\r\nread Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s message; \xe2\x80\x9cthat is what exasperates me in this\r\naffair\xe2\x80\x94to feel that the cunning, mocking eye of that fellow follows me\r\neverywhere. He sees everything; he knows everything; he reads my inmost\r\nthoughts; he even foresees my slightest movement. Ah! he is possessed\r\nof a marvellous intuition, far surpassing that of the most instinctive\r\nwoman, yes, surpassing even that of Herlock Sholmes himself. Nothing\r\nescapes him. I resemble an actor whose every step and movement are\r\ndirected by a stage-manager; who says this and does that in obedience\r\nto a superior will. That is my position. Do you understand, Wilson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCertainly Wilson would have understood if his faculties had not been\r\ndeadened by the profound slumber of a man whose temperature varies\r\nbetween one hundred and one hundred and three degrees. But whether he\r\nheard or not was a matter of no consequence to Herlock Sholmes, who\r\ncontinued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have to concentrate all my energy and bring all my resources into\r\naction in order to make the slightest progress. And, fortunately for\r\nme, those petty annoyances are like so many pricks from a needle and\r\nserve only to stimulate me. As soon as the heat of the wound is\r\nappeased and the shock to my vanity has subsided I say to myself:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Amuse yourself, my dear fellow, but remember that he who laughs last\r\nlaughs best. Sooner or later you will betray yourself.\xe2\x80\x99 For you know,\r\nWilson, it was Lupin himself, who, by his first dispatch and the\r\nobservation that it suggested to little Henriette, disclosed to me the\r\nsecret of his correspondence with Alice Hemun. Have you forgotten that\r\ncircumstance, dear boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Wilson was asleep; and Sholmes, pacing to and fro, resumed his\r\nspeech:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, now, things are not in a bad shape; a little obscure, perhaps,\r\nbut the light is creeping in. In the first place, I must learn all\r\nabout Monsieur Bresson. Ganimard and I will visit the bank of the\r\nriver, at the spot where Bresson threw away the package, and the\r\nparticular r\xc3\xb4le of that gentleman will be known to me. After that the\r\ngame will be played between me and Alice Demun. Rather a light-weight\r\nopponent, hein, Wilson? And do you not think that I will soon know the\r\nphrase represented by the letters clipped from the alphabet-book, and\r\nwhat the isolated letters\xe2\x80\x94the \xe2\x80\x98C\xe2\x80\x99 and the \xe2\x80\x98H\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94mean? That is all I want\r\nto know, Wilson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMademoiselle entered at that moment, and, observing Sholmes\r\ngesticulating, she said, in her sweetest manner:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Sholmes, I must scold you if you waken my patient. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nnice of you to disturb him. The doctor has ordered absolute rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked at her in silence, astonished, as on their first meeting, at\r\nher wonderful self-possession.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you look at me so, Monsieur Sholmes?... You seem to be trying\r\nto read my thoughts.... No?... Then what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe questioned him with the most innocent expression on her pretty face\r\nand in her frank blue eyes. A smile played upon her lips; and she\r\ndisplayed so much unaffected candor that the Englishman almost lost his\r\ntemper. He approached her and said, in a low voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBresson killed himself last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe affected not to understand him; so he repeated:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBresson killed himself yesterday....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe did not show the slightest emotion; she acted as if the matter did\r\nnot concern or interest her in any way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have been informed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, displaying his annoyance.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOtherwise, the news would have caused you to start, at least. Ah! you\r\nare stronger than I expected. But what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of your trying to\r\nconceal anything from me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe picked up the alphabet-book, which he had placed on a convenient\r\ntable, and, opening it at the mutilated page, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you tell me the order in which the missing letters should be\r\narranged in order to express the exact wording of the message you sent\r\nto Bresson four days before the theft of the Jewish lamp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe order?... Bresson?... the theft of the Jewish lamp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe repeated the words slowly, as if trying to grasp their meaning. He\r\ncontinued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Here are the letters employed ... on this bit of paper.... What\r\ndid you say to Bresson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe letters employed ... what did I say....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly she burst into laughter:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! that is it! I understand! I am an accomplice in the crime! There\r\nis a Monsieur Bresson who stole the Jewish lamp and who has now\r\ncommitted suicide. And I am the friend of that gentleman. Oh! how\r\nabsurd you are!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhom did you go to see last night on the second floor of a house in\r\nthe avenue des Ternes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho? My modiste, Mademoiselle Langeais. Do you suppose that my modiste\r\nand my friend Monsieur Bresson are the same person?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDespite all he knew, Sholmes was now in doubt. A person can feign\r\nterror, joy, anxiety, in fact all emotions; but a person cannot feign\r\nabsolute indifference or light, careless laughter. Yet he continued to\r\nquestion her:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you accost me the other evening at the Northern Railway\r\nstation? And why did you entreat me to leave Paris immediately without\r\ninvestigating this theft?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you are too inquisitive, Monsieur Sholmes,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, still\r\nlaughing in the most natural manner. \xe2\x80\x9cTo punish you I will tell you\r\nnothing, and, besides, you must watch the patient while I go to the\r\npharmacy on an urgent message. Au revoir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe left the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am beaten ... by a girl,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cNot only did I get\r\nnothing out of her but I exposed my hand and put her on her guard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd he recalled the affair of the blue diamond and his first interview\r\nwith Clotilde Destange. Had not the blonde Lady met his question with\r\nthe same unruffled serenity, and was he not once more face to face with\r\none of those creatures who, under the protection and influence of\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, maintain the utmost coolness in the face of a terrible\r\ndanger?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSholmes ... Sholmes....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was Wilson who called him. Sholmes approached the bed, and, leaning\r\nover, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Wilson? Does your wound pain you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWilson\xe2\x80\x99s lips moved, but he could not speak. At last, with a great\r\neffort, he stammered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo ... Sholmes ... it is not she ... that is impossible\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, Wilson, what do you know about it? I tell you that it is she! It\r\nis only when I meet one of Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s creatures, prepared and instructed\r\nby him, that I lose my head and make a fool of myself.... I bet you\r\nthat within an hour Lupin will know all about our interview. Within an\r\nhour? What am I saying?... Why, he may know already. The visit to the\r\npharmacy ... urgent message. All nonsense!... She has gone to telephone\r\nto Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes left the house hurriedly, went down the avenue de Messine, and\r\nwas just in time to see Mademoiselle enter a pharmacy. Ten minutes\r\nlater she emerged from the shop carrying some small packages and a\r\nbottle wrapped in white paper. But she had not proceeded far, when she\r\nwas accosted by a man who, with hat in hand and an obsequious air,\r\nappeared to be asking for charity. She stopped, gave him something, and\r\nproceeded on her way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe spoke to him,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Englishman to himself.\r\n\r\nIf not a certainty, it was at least an intuition, and quite sufficient\r\nto cause him to change his tactics. Leaving the girl to pursue her own\r\ncourse, he followed the suspected mendicant, who walked slowly to the\r\navenue des Ternes and lingered for a long time around the house in\r\nwhich Bresson had lived, sometimes raising his eyes to the windows of\r\nthe second floor and watching the people who entered the house.\r\n\r\nAt the end of an hour he climbed to the top of a tramcar going in the\r\ndirection of Neuilly. Sholmes followed and took a seat behind the man,\r\nand beside a gentleman who was concealed behind the pages of a\r\nnewspaper. At the fortifications the gentleman lowered the paper, and\r\nSholmes recognized Ganimard, who thereupon whispered, as he pointed to\r\nthe man in front:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the man who followed Bresson last night. He has been watching\r\nthe house for an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything new in regard to Bresson?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, a letter came to his address this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis morning? Then it was posted yesterday before the sender could\r\nknow of Bresson\xe2\x80\x99s death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. It is now in the possession of the examining magistrate. But\r\nI read it. It says: _He will not accept any compromise. He wants\r\neverything\xe2\x80\x94the first thing as well as those of the second affair.\r\nOtherwise he will proceed._\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no signature,\xe2\x80\x9d added Ganimard. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me those few\r\nlines won\xe2\x80\x99t help us much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with you, Monsieur Ganimard. To me those few lines are\r\nvery interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy so? I can\xe2\x80\x99t see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor reasons that are personal to me,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Sholmes, with the\r\nindifference that he frequently displayed toward his colleague.\r\n\r\nThe tramcar stopped at the rue de Ch\xc3\xa2teau, which was the terminus. The\r\nman descended and walked away quietly. Sholmes followed at so short a\r\ndistance that Ganimard protested, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he should turn around he will suspect us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will not turn around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is an accomplice of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, and the fact that he walks in\r\nthat manner, with his hands in his pockets, proves, in the first place,\r\nthat he knows he is being followed and, in the second place, that he is\r\nnot afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I think we are keeping too close to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot too close to prevent his slipping through our fingers. He is too\r\nsure of himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! Look there! In front of that caf\xc3\xa9 there are two of the bicycle\r\npolice. If I summon them to our assistance, how can the man slip\r\nthrough our fingers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, our friend doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be worried about it. In fact, he is\r\nasking for their assistance himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMon Dieu!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Ganimard, \xe2\x80\x9che has a nerve.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man approached the two policemen just as they were mounting their\r\nbicycles. After a few words with them he leaped on a third bicycle,\r\nwhich was leaning against the wall of the caf\xc3\xa9, and rode away at a fast\r\npace, accompanied by the two policemen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHein! one, two, three and away!\xe2\x80\x9d growled Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd through whose\r\nagency, Monsieur Ganimard? Two of your colleagues.... Ah! but Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin has a wonderful organization! Bicycle policemen in his\r\nservice!... I told you our man was too calm, too sure of himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ganimard, quite vexed, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat are we to do now? It is\r\neasy enough to laugh! Anyone can do that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come, don\xe2\x80\x99t lose your temper! We will get our revenge. But, in\r\nthe meantime, we need reinforcements.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFolenfant is waiting for me at the end of the avenue de Neuilly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, go and get him and join me later. I will follow our fugitive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes followed the bicycle tracks, which were plainly visible in the\r\ndust of the road as two of the machines were furnished with striated\r\ntires. Very soon he ascertained that the tracks were leading him to the\r\nedge of the Seine, and that the three men had turned in the direction\r\ntaken by Bresson on the preceding evening. Thus he arrived at the\r\ngateway where he and Ganimard had concealed themselves, and, a little\r\nfarther on, he discovered a mingling of the bicycle tracks which showed\r\nthat the men had halted at that spot. Directly opposite there was a\r\nlittle point of land which projected into the river and, at the\r\nextremity thereof, an old boat was moored.\r\n\r\nIt was there that Bresson had thrown away the package, or, rather, had\r\ndropped it. Sholmes descended the bank and saw that the declivity was\r\nnot steep and the water quite shallow, so it would be quite easy to\r\nrecover the package, provided the three men had not forestalled him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that can\xe2\x80\x99t be,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, \xe2\x80\x9cthey have not had time. A quarter of\r\nan hour at the most. And yet, why did they come this way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA fisherman was seated on the old boat. Sholmes asked him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see three men on bicycles a few minutes ago?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fisherman made a negative gesture. But Sholmes insisted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree men who stopped on the road just on top of the bank?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fisherman rested his pole under his arm, took a memorandum book\r\nfrom his pocket, wrote on one of the pages, tore it out, and handed it\r\nto Sholmes. The Englishman gave a start of surprise. In the middle of\r\nthe paper which he held in his hand he saw the series of letters cut\r\nfrom the alphabet-book:\r\n\r\nCDEHNOPRZEO\xe2\x80\x94237.\r\n\r\nThe man resumed his fishing, sheltered from the sun by a large straw\r\nhat, with his coat and vest lying beside him. He was intently watching\r\nthe cork attached to his line as it floated on the surface of the\r\nwater.\r\n\r\nThere was a moment of silence\xe2\x80\x94solemn and terrible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it he?\xe2\x80\x9d conjectured Sholmes, with an anxiety that was almost\r\npitiful. Then the truth burst upon him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is he! It is he! No one else could remain there so calmly, without\r\nthe slightest display of anxiety, without the least fear of what might\r\nhappen. And who else would know the story of those mysterious letters?\r\nAlice had warned him by means of her messenger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly the Englishman felt that his hand\xe2\x80\x94that his own hand had\r\ninvoluntarily seized the handle of his revolver, and that his eyes were\r\nfixed on the man\xe2\x80\x99s back, a little below the neck. One movement, and the\r\ndrama would be finished; the life of the strange adventurer would come\r\nto a miserable end.\r\n\r\nThe fisherman did not stir.\r\n\r\nSholmes nervously toyed with his revolver, and experienced a wild\r\ndesire to fire it and end everything; but the horror of such an act was\r\nrepugnant to his nature. Death would be certain and would end all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, \xe2\x80\x9clet him get up and defend himself. If he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t, so\r\nmuch the worse for him. One second more ... and I fire....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut a sound of footsteps behind him caused him to turn his head. It was\r\nGanimard coming with some assistants.\r\n\r\nThen, quickly changing his plans, Sholmes leaped into the boat, which\r\nwas broken from its moorings by his sudden action; he pounced upon the\r\nman and seized him around the body. They rolled to the bottom of the\r\nboat together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lupin, struggling to free himself, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat does\r\nthis mean? When one of us has conquered the other, what good will it\r\ndo? You will not know what to do with me, nor I with you. We will\r\nremain here like two idiots.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two oars slipped into the water. The boat drifted into the stream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord, what a fuss you make! A man of your age ought to know\r\nbetter! You act like a child.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin succeeded in freeing himself from the grasp of the detective,\r\nwho, thoroughly exasperated and ready to kill, put his hand in his\r\npocket. He uttered an oath: Lupin had taken his revolver. Then he knelt\r\ndown and tried to capture one of the lost oars in order to regain the\r\nshore, while Lupin was trying to capture the other oar in order to\r\ndrive the boat down the river.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s gone! I can\xe2\x80\x99t reach it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s of no consequence.\r\nIf you get your oar I can prevent your using it. And you could do the\r\nsame to me. But, you see, that is the way in this world, we act without\r\nany purpose or reason, as our efforts are in vain since Fate decides\r\neverything. Now, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see, Fate is on the side of his friend\r\nLupin. The game is mine! The current favors me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boat was slowly drifting down the river.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook out!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lupin, quickly.\r\n\r\nSomeone on the bank was pointing a revolver. Lupin stooped, a shot was\r\nfired; it struck the water beyond the boat. Lupin burst into laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless me! It\xe2\x80\x99s my friend Ganimard! But it was very wrong of you to\r\ndo that, Ganimard. You have no right to shoot except in self-defense.\r\nDoes poor Lupin worry you so much that you forget yourself?... Now, be\r\ngood, and don\xe2\x80\x99t shoot again!... If you do you will hit our English\r\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stood behind Sholmes, facing Ganimard, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Ganimard, I am ready! Aim for his heart!... Higher!... A little\r\nto the left.... Ah! you missed that time ... deuced bad shot.... Try\r\nagain.... Your hand shakes, Ganimard.... Now, once more ... one, two,\r\nthree, fire!... Missed!... Parbleu! the authorities furnish you with\r\ntoy-pistols.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin drew a long revolver and fired without taking aim. Ganimard put\r\nhis hand to his hat: the bullet had passed through it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of that, Ganimard! Ah! that\xe2\x80\x99s a real revolver! A\r\ngenuine English bulldog. It belongs to my friend, Herlock Sholmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, with a laugh, he threw the revolver to the shore, where it landed\r\nat Ganimard\xe2\x80\x99s feet.\r\n\r\nSholmes could not withhold a smile of admiration. What a torrent of\r\nyouthful spirits! And how he seemed to enjoy himself! It appeared as if\r\nthe sensation of peril caused him a physical pleasure; and this\r\nextraordinary man had no other purpose in life than to seek for dangers\r\nsimply for the amusement it afforded him in avoiding them.\r\n\r\nMany people had now gathered on the banks of the river, and Ganimard\r\nand his men followed the boat as it slowly floated down the stream.\r\nLupin\xe2\x80\x99s capture was a mathematical certainty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConfess, old fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lupin, turning to the Englishman, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you\r\nwould not exchange your present position for all the gold in the\r\nTransvaal! You are now in the first row of the orchestra chairs! But,\r\nin the first place, we must have the prologue ... after which we can\r\nleap, at one bound, to the fifth act of the drama, which will represent\r\nthe capture or escape of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin. Therefore, I am going to ask you\r\na plain question, to which I request a plain answer\xe2\x80\x94a simple yes or no.\r\nWill you renounce this affair? At present I can repair the damage you\r\nhave done; later it will be beyond my power. Is it a bargain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin\xe2\x80\x99s face showed his disappointment and annoyance. He continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI insist. More for your sake than my own, I insist, because I am\r\ncertain you will be the first to regret your intervention. For the last\r\ntime, yes or no?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLupin stooped down, removed one of the boards in the bottom of the\r\nboat, and, for some minutes, was engaged in a work the nature of which\r\nSholmes could not discern. Then he arose, seated himself beside the\r\nEnglishman, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe, monsieur, that we came to the river to-day for the same\r\npurpose: to recover the object which Bresson threw away. For my part I\r\nhad invited a few friends to join me here, and I was on the point of\r\nmaking an examination of the bed of the river when my friends announced\r\nyour approach. I confess that the news did not surprise me, as I have\r\nbeen notified every hour concerning the progress of your investigation.\r\nThat was an easy matter. Whenever anything occurred in the rue Murillo\r\nthat might interest me, simply a ring on the telephone and I was\r\ninformed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped. The board that he had displaced in the bottom of the boat\r\nwas rising and water was working into the boat all around it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe deuce! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how to fix it. I was afraid this old boat\r\nwould leak. You are not afraid, monsieur?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes shrugged his shoulders. Lupin continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will understand then, in those circumstances, and knowing in\r\nadvance that you would be more eager to seek a battle than I would be\r\nto avoid it, I assure you I was not entirely displeased to enter into a\r\ncontest of which the issue is quite certain, since I hold all the trump\r\ncards in my hand. And I desired that our meeting should be given the\r\nwidest publicity in order that your defeat may be universally known, so\r\nthat another Countess de Crozon or another Baron d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle may not\r\nbe tempted to solicit your aid against me. Besides, my dear monsieur\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stopped again and, using his half-closed hands as a lorgnette, he\r\nscanned the banks of the river.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMon Dieu! they have chartered a superb boat, a real war-vessel, and\r\nsee how they are rowing. In five minutes they will be along-side, and I\r\nam lost. Monsieur Sholmes, a word of advice; you seize me, bind me and\r\ndeliver me to the officers of the law. Does that programme please\r\nyou?... Unless, in the meantime, we are shipwrecked, in which event we\r\ncan do nothing but prepare our wills. What do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey exchanged looks. Sholmes now understood Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s scheme: he had\r\nscuttled the boat. And the water was rising. It had reached the soles\r\nof their boots. Then it covered their feet; but they did not move. It\r\nwas half-way to their knees. The Englishman took out his tobacco,\r\nrolled a cigarette, and lighted it. Lupin continued to talk:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut do not regard that offer as a confession of my weakness. I\r\nsurrender to you in a battle in which I can achieve a victory in order\r\nto avoid a struggle upon a field not of my own choosing. In so doing I\r\nrecognize the fact that Sholmes is the only enemy I fear, and announce\r\nmy anxiety that Sholmes will not be diverted from my track. I take this\r\nopportunity to tell you these things since fate has accorded me the\r\nhonor of a conversation with you. I have only one regret; it is that\r\nour conversation should have occurred while we are taking a foot-bath\r\n... a situation that is lacking in dignity, I must confess.... What did\r\nI say? A foot-bath? It is worse than that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe water had reached the board on which they were sitting, and the\r\nboat was gradually sinking.\r\n\r\nSholmes, smoking his cigarette, appeared to be calmly admiring the\r\nscenery. For nothing in the world, while face to face with that man\r\nwho, while threatened by dangers, surrounded by a crowd, followed by a\r\nposse of police, maintained his equanimity and good humor, for nothing\r\nin the world would he, Sholmes, display the slightest sign of\r\nnervousness.\r\n\r\nEach of them looked as if he might say: Should a person be disturbed by\r\nsuch trifles? Are not people drowned in a river every day? Is it such\r\nan unusual event as to deserve special attention? One chatted, whilst\r\nthe other dreamed; both concealing their wounded pride beneath a mask\r\nof indifference.\r\n\r\nOne minute more and the boat will sink. Lupin continued his chatter:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe important thing to know is whether we will sink before or after\r\nthe arrival of the champions of the law. That is the main question. As\r\nto our shipwreck, that is a foregone conclusion. Now, monsieur, the\r\nhour has come in which we must make our wills. I give, devise and\r\nbequeath all my property to Herlock Sholmes, a citizen of England, for\r\nhis own use and benefit. But, mon Dieu, how quickly the champions of\r\nthe law are approaching! Ah! the brave fellows! It is a pleasure to\r\nwatch them. Observe the precision of the oars! Ah! is it you, Brigadier\r\nFolenfant? Bravo! The idea of a war-vessel is an excellent one. I\r\ncommend you to your superiors, Brigadier Folenfant.... Do you wish a\r\nmedal? You shall have it. And your comrade Dieuzy, where is he?... Ah!\r\nyes, I think I see him on the left bank of the river at the head of a\r\nhundred natives. So that, if I escape shipwreck, I shall be captured on\r\nthe left by Dieuzy and his natives, or, on the right, by Ganimard and\r\nthe populace of Neuilly. An embarrassing dilemma!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boat entered an eddy; it swung around and Sholmes caught hold of\r\nthe oarlocks. Lupin said to him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur, you should remove your coat. You will find it easier to swim\r\nwithout a coat. No? You refuse? Then I shall put on my own.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe donned his coat, buttoned it closely, the same as Sholmes, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a discourteous man you are! And what a pity that you should be so\r\nstubborn in this affair, in which, of course, you display your\r\nstrength, but, oh! so vainly! really, you mar your genius\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Lupin,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Sholmes, emerging from his silence, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\ntalk too much, and you frequently err through excess of confidence and\r\nthrough your frivolity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a severe reproach.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThus, without knowing it, you furnished me, only a moment ago, with\r\nthe information I required.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! you required some information and you didn\xe2\x80\x99t tell me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had no occasion to ask you for it\xe2\x80\x94you volunteered it. Within three\r\nhours I can deliver the key of the mystery to Monsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle.\r\nThat is the only reply\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe did not finish the sentence. The boat suddenly sank, taking both of\r\nthe men down with it. It emerged immediately, with its keel in the air.\r\nShouts were heard on either bank, succeeded by an anxious moment of\r\nsilence. Then the shouts were renewed: one of the shipwrecked party had\r\ncome to the surface.\r\n\r\nIt was Herlock Sholmes. He was an excellent swimmer, and struck out,\r\nwith powerful strokes, for Folenfant\xe2\x80\x99s boat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCourage, Monsieur Sholmes,\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Folenfant; \xe2\x80\x9cwe are here. Keep it\r\nup ... we will get you ... a little more, Monsieur Sholmes ... catch\r\nthe rope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman seized the rope they had thrown to him. But, while they\r\nwere hauling him into the boat, he heard a voice behind him, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe key of the mystery, monsieur, yes, you shall have it.
