I am\r\nastonished that you haven\xe2\x80\x99t got it already. What then? What good will\r\nit do you? By that time you will have lost the battle....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow comfortably installed astride the keel of the boat, Lupin continued\r\nhis speech with solemn gestures, as if he hoped to convince his\r\nadversary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must understand, my dear Sholmes, there is nothing to be done,\r\nabsolutely nothing. You find yourself in the deplorable position of a\r\ngentleman\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurrender, Lupin!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Folenfant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are an ill-bred fellow, Folenfant, to interrupt me in the middle\r\nof a sentence. I was saying\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurrender, Lupin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! parbleu! Brigadier Folenfant, a man surrenders only when he is in\r\ndanger. Surely, you do not pretend to say that I am in any danger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the last time, Lupin, I call on you to surrender.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrigadier Folenfant, you have no intention of killing me; you may wish\r\nto wound me since you are afraid I may escape. But if by chance the\r\nwound prove mortal? Just think of your remorse! It would embitter your\r\nold age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe shot was fired.\r\n\r\nLupin staggered, clutched at the keel of the boat for a moment, then\r\nlet go and disappeared.\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was exactly three o\xe2\x80\x99clock when the foregoing events transpired.\r\nPrecisely at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock, as he had foretold, Herlock Sholmes, dressed\r\nin trousers that were too short and a coat that was too small, which he\r\nhad borrowed from an innkeeper at Neuilly, wearing a cap and a flannel\r\nshirt, entered the boudoir in the Rue Murillo, after having sent word\r\nto Monsieur and Madame d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle that he desired an interview.\r\n\r\nThey found him walking up and down the room. And he looked so ludicrous\r\nin his strange costume that they could scarcely suppress their mirth.\r\nWith pensive air and stooped shoulders, he walked like an automaton\r\nfrom the window to the door and from the door to the window, taking\r\neach time the same number of steps, and turning each time in the same\r\nmanner.\r\n\r\nHe stopped, picked up a small ornament, examined it mechanically, and\r\nresumed his walk. At last, planting himself before them, he asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Mademoiselle here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she is in the garden with the children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish Mademoiselle to be present at this interview.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it necessary\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave a little patience, monsieur. From the facts I am going to present\r\nto you, you will see the necessity for her presence here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. Suzanne, will you call her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle arose, went out, and returned almost immediately,\r\naccompanied by Alice Demun. Mademoiselle, who was a trifle paler than\r\nusual, remained standing, leaning against a table, and without even\r\nasking why she had been called. Sholmes did not look at her, but,\r\nsuddenly turning toward Monsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle, he said, in a tone which\r\ndid not admit of a reply:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter several days\xe2\x80\x99 investigation, monsieur, I must repeat what I told\r\nyou when I first came here: the Jewish lamp was stolen by some one\r\nliving in the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe name of the guilty party?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour proof?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have sufficient to establish that fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we require more than that. We desire the restoration of the stolen\r\ngoods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Jewish lamp? It is in my possession.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe opal necklace? The snuff-box?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe opal necklace, the snuff-box, and all the goods stolen on the\r\nsecond occasion are in my possession.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes delighted in these dramatic dialogues, and it pleased him to\r\nannounce his victories in that curt manner. The baron and his wife were\r\namazed, and looked at Sholmes with a silent curiosity, which was the\r\nhighest praise.\r\n\r\nHe related to them, very minutely, what he had done during those three\r\ndays. He told of his discovery of the alphabet book, wrote upon a sheet\r\nof paper the sentence formed by the missing letters, then related the\r\njourney of Bresson to the bank of the river and the suicide of the\r\nadventurer, and, finally, his struggle with Lupin, the shipwreck, and\r\nthe disappearance of Lupin. When he had finished, the baron said, in a\r\nlow voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, you have told us everything except the name of the guilty party.\r\nWhom do you accuse?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI accuse the person who cut the letters from the alphabet book, and\r\ncommunicated with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin by means of those letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know that such correspondence was carried on with Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy information comes from Lupin himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe produced a piece of paper that was wet and crumpled. It was the page\r\nwhich Lupin had torn from his memorandum-book, and upon which he had\r\nwritten the phrase.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will notice,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, with satisfaction, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he was\r\nnot obliged to give me that sheet of paper, and, in that way, disclose\r\nhis identity. Simple childishness on his part, and yet it gave me\r\nexactly the information I desired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the baron. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes took a pencil and made a fresh copy of the letters and figures.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCDEHNOPRZEO\xe2\x80\x94237.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d said the baron; \xe2\x80\x9cit is the formula you showed me yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. If you had turned and returned that formula in every way, as I\r\nhave done, you would have seen at first glance that this formula is not\r\nlike the first one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn what respect do they differ?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis one has two more letters\xe2\x80\x94an E and an O.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally; I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t noticed that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJoin those two letters to the C and the H which remained after forming\r\nthe word \xe2\x80\x98respondez,\xe2\x80\x99 and you will agree with me that the only possible\r\nword is ECHO.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does that mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt refers to the _Echo de France_, Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s newspaper, his official\r\norgan, the one in which he publishes his communications. Reply in the\r\n_Echo de France_, in the personal advertisements, under number 237.\r\nThat is the key to the mystery, and Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin was kind enough to\r\nfurnish it to me. I went to the newspaper office.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you find there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI found the entire story of the relations between Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin and his\r\naccomplice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes produced seven newspapers which he opened at the fourth page\r\nand pointed to the following lines:\r\n\r\n1. Ars. Lup. Lady implores protection. 540.\r\n\r\n2. 540. Awaiting particulars. A.L.\r\n\r\n3. A.L. Under domin. enemy. Lost.\r\n\r\n4. 540. Write address. Will make investigation.\r\n\r\n5. A.L. Murillo.\r\n\r\n6. 540. Park three o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Violets.\r\n\r\n7. 237. Understand. Sat. Will be Sun. morn. park.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you call that the whole story!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the baron.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and if you will listen to me for a few minutes, I think I can\r\nconvince you. In the first place, a lady who signs herself 540 implores\r\nthe protection of Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, who replies by asking for particulars.\r\nThe lady replies that she is under the domination of an enemy\xe2\x80\x94who is\r\nBresson, no doubt\xe2\x80\x94and that she is lost if some one does not come to her\r\nassistance. Lupin is suspicious and does not yet venture to appoint an\r\ninterview with the unknown woman, demands the address and proposes to\r\nmake an investigation. The lady hesitates for four days\xe2\x80\x94look at the\r\ndates\xe2\x80\x94finally, under stress of circumstances and influenced by\r\nBresson\xe2\x80\x99s threats, she gives the name of the street\xe2\x80\x94Murillo. Next day,\r\nArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin announces that he will be in the Park Monceau at three\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, and asks his unknown correspondent to wear a bouquet of\r\nviolets as a means of identification. Then there is a lapse of eight\r\ndays in the correspondence. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin and the lady do not require to\r\ncorrespond through the newspaper now, as they see each other or write\r\ndirectly. The scheme is arranged in this way: in order to satisfy\r\nBresson\xe2\x80\x99s demands, the lady is to carry off the Jewish lamp. The date\r\nis not yet fixed. The lady who, as a matter of prudence, corresponds by\r\nmeans of letters cut out of a book, decides on Saturday and adds:\r\n_Reply Echo 237_. Lupin replies that it is understood and that he will\r\nbe in the park on Sunday morning. Sunday morning, the theft takes\r\nplace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, that is an excellent chain of circumstantial evidence and\r\nevery link is complete,\xe2\x80\x9d said the baron.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe theft has taken place,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Sholmes. \xe2\x80\x9cThe lady goes out on\r\nSunday morning, tells Lupin what she has done, and carries the Jewish\r\nlamp to Bresson. Everything occurs then exactly as Lupin had foreseen.\r\nThe officers of the law, deceived by an open window, four holes in the\r\nground and two scratches on the balcony railing, immediately advance\r\nthe theory that the theft was committed by a burglar. The lady is\r\nsafe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I confess the theory was a logical one,\xe2\x80\x9d said the baron. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the\r\nsecond theft\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe second theft was provoked by the first. The newspapers having\r\nrelated how the Jewish lamp had disappeared, some one conceived the\r\nidea of repeating the crime and carrying away what had been left. This\r\ntime, it was not a simulated theft, but a real one, a genuine burglary,\r\nwith ladders and other paraphernalia\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLupin, of course\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Lupin does not act so stupidly. He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t fire at people for\r\ntrifling reasons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, who was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBresson, no doubt, and unknown to the lady whom he had menaced. It was\r\nBresson who entered here; it was Bresson that I pursued; it was Bresson\r\nwho wounded poor Wilson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you sure of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely. One of Bresson\xe2\x80\x99s accomplices wrote to him yesterday,\r\nbefore his suicide, a letter which proves that negotiations were\r\npending between this accomplice and Lupin for the restitution of all\r\nthe articles stolen from your house. Lupin demanded everything, \xe2\x80\x98_the\r\nfirst thing_ (that is, the Jewish lamp) _as well as those of the second\r\naffair_.\xe2\x80\x99 Moreover, he was watching Bresson. When the latter returned\r\nfrom the river last night, one of Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s men followed him as well as\r\nwe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was Bresson doing at the river?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaving been warned of the progress of my investigations\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWarned! by whom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the same lady, who justly feared that the discovery of the Jewish\r\nlamp would lead to the discovery of her own adventure. Thereupon,\r\nBresson, having been warned, made into a package all the things that\r\ncould compromise him and threw them into a place where he thought he\r\ncould get them again when the danger was past. It was after his return,\r\ntracked by Ganimard and myself, having, no doubt, other sins on his\r\nconscience, that he lost his head and killed himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what did the package contain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Jewish lamp and your other ornaments.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, they are not in your possession?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImmediately after Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s disappearance, I profited by the bath he had\r\nforced upon me, went to the spot selected by Bresson, where I found the\r\nstolen articles wrapped in some soiled linen. They are there, on the\r\ntable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWithout a word, the baron cut the cord, tore open the wet linen, picked\r\nout the lamp, turned a screw in the foot, then divided the bowl of the\r\nlamp which opened in two equal parts and there he found the golden\r\nchimera, set with rubies and emeralds.\r\n\r\nIt was intact.\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was in that scene, so natural in appearance and which consisted\r\nof a simple exposition of facts, something which rendered it\r\nfrightfully tragic\xe2\x80\x94it was the formal, direct, irrefutable accusation\r\nthat Sholmes launched in each of his words against Mademoiselle. And it\r\nwas also the impressive silence of Alice Demun.\r\n\r\nDuring that long, cruel accumulation of accusing circumstances heaped\r\none upon another, not a muscle of her face had moved, not a trace of\r\nrevolt or fear had marred the serenity of her limpid eyes. What were\r\nher thoughts. And, especially, what was she going to say at the solemn\r\nmoment when it would become necessary for her to speak and defend\r\nherself in order to break the chain of evidence that Herlock Sholmes\r\nhad so cleverly woven around her?\r\n\r\nThat moment had come, but the girl was silent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak! Speak!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mon. d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle.\r\n\r\nShe did not speak. So he insisted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne word will clear you. One word of denial, and I will believe you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat word, she would not utter.\r\n\r\nThe baron paced to and fro in his excitement; then, addressing Sholmes,\r\nhe said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, monsieur, I cannot believe it, I do not believe it. There are\r\nimpossible crimes! and this is opposed to all I know and to all that I\r\nhave seen during the past year. No, I cannot believe it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe placed his hand on the Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you yourself, monsieur, are you absolutely certain that you are\r\nright?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes hesitated, like a man on whom a sudden demand is made and\r\ncannot frame an immediate reply. Then he smiled, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the person whom I accuse, by reason of her situation in your\r\nhouse, could know that the Jewish lamp contained that magnificent\r\njewel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated the baron.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was, really, the very thing he would not have done, blinded by the\r\nconfidence the girl had inspired in him. But he could no longer refrain\r\nfrom doing it. He approached her and, looking into her eyes, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it you, mademoiselle? Was it you who took the jewel? Was it you\r\nwho corresponded with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin and committed the theft?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was I, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied.\r\n\r\nShe did not drop her head. Her face displayed no sign of shame or fear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible?\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mon. d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle. \xe2\x80\x9cI would never have\r\nbelieved it.... You are the last person in the world that I would have\r\nsuspected. How did you do it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did it exactly as Monsieur Sholmes has told it. On Saturday night I\r\ncame to the boudoir, took the lamp, and, in the morning I carried it\r\n... to that man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said the baron; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat you pretend to have done is impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible\xe2\x80\x94why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, in the morning I found the door of the boudoir bolted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe blushed, and looked at Sholmes as if seeking his counsel. Sholmes\r\nwas astonished at her embarrassment. Had she nothing to say? Did the\r\nconfessions, which had corroborated the report that he, Sholmes, had\r\nmade concerning the theft of the Jewish lamp, merely serve to mask a\r\nlie? Was she misleading them by a false confession?\r\n\r\nThe baron continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat door was locked. I found the door exactly as I had left it the\r\nnight before. If you entered by that door, as you pretend, some one\r\nmust have opened it from the interior\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, from the boudoir\r\nor from our chamber. Now, there was no one inside these two rooms ...\r\nthere was no one except my wife and myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSholmes bowed his head and covered his face with his hands in order to\r\nconceal his emotion. A sudden light had entered his mind, that startled\r\nhim and made him exceedingly uncomfortable. Everything was revealed to\r\nhim, like the sudden lifting of a fog from the morning landscape. He\r\nwas annoyed as well as ashamed, because his deductions were fallacious\r\nand his entire theory was wrong.\r\n\r\nAlice Demun was innocent!\r\n\r\nAlice Demun was innocent. That proposition explained the embarrassment\r\nhe had experienced from the beginning in directing the terrible\r\naccusation against that young girl. Now, he saw the truth; he knew it.\r\nAfter a few seconds, he raised his head, and looked at Madame\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle as naturally as he could. She was pale\xe2\x80\x94with that unusual\r\npallor which invades us in the relentless moments of our lives. Her\r\nhands, which she endeavored to conceal, were trembling as if stricken\r\nwith palsy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne minute more,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cand she will betray herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe placed himself between her and her husband in the desire to avert\r\nthe awful danger which, _through his fault_, now threatened that man\r\nand woman. But, at sight of the baron, he was shocked to the very\r\ncentre of his soul. The same dreadful idea had entered the mind of\r\nMonsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle. The same thought was at work in the brain of the\r\nhusband. He understood, also! He saw the truth!\r\n\r\nIn desperation, Alice Demun hurled herself against the implacable\r\ntruth, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are right, monsieur. I made a mistake. I did not enter by this\r\ndoor. I came through the garden and the vestibule ... by aid of a\r\nladder\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a supreme effort of true devotion. But a useless effort! The\r\nwords rang false. The voice did not carry conviction, and the poor girl\r\nno longer displayed those clear, fearless eyes and that natural air of\r\ninnocence which had served her so well. Now, she bowed her\r\nhead\xe2\x80\x94vanquished.\r\n\r\nThe silence became painful. Madame d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle was waiting for her\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x99s next move, overwhelmed with anxiety and fear. The baron\r\nappeared to be struggling against the dreadful suspicion, as if he\r\nwould not submit to the overthrow of his happiness. Finally, he said to\r\nhis wife:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak! Explain!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing to tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied, in a very low voice, and\r\nwith features drawn by anguish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, then ... Mademoiselle....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle saved me ... through devotion ... through affection ...\r\nand accused herself....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaved you from what? From whom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom that man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBresson?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; it was I whom he held in fear by threats.... I met him at one of\r\nmy friends\xe2\x80\x99.... and I was foolish enough to listen to him. Oh! there\r\nwas nothing that you cannot pardon. But I wrote him two letters ...\r\nletters which you will see.... I had to buy them back ... you know\r\nhow.... Oh! have pity on me?... I have suffered so much!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou! You! Suzanne!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe raised his clenched fists, ready to strike her, ready to kill her.\r\nBut he dropped his arms, and murmured:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, Suzanne.... You!... Is it possible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy short detached sentences, she related the heartrending story, her\r\ndreadful awakening to the infamy of the man, her remorse, her fear, and\r\nshe also told of Alice\xe2\x80\x99s devotion; how the young girl divined the\r\nsorrow of her mistress, wormed a confession out of her, wrote to Lupin,\r\nand devised the scheme of the theft in order to save her from Bresson.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, Suzanne, you,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Monsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle, bowed with grief\r\nand shame.... \xe2\x80\x9cHow could you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nOn the same evening, the steamer \xe2\x80\x9cCity of London,\xe2\x80\x9d which plies between\r\nCalais and Dover, was gliding slowly over the smooth sea. The night was\r\ndark; the wind was fainter than a zephyr. The majority of the\r\npassengers had retired to their cabins; but a few, more intrepid, were\r\npromenading on the deck or sleeping in large rocking-chairs, wrapped in\r\ntheir travelling-rugs. One could see, here and there, the light of a\r\ncigar, and one could hear, mingled with the soft murmur of the breeze,\r\nthe faint sound of voices which were carefully subdued to harmonize\r\nwith the deep silence of the night.\r\n\r\nOne of the passengers, who had been pacing to and fro upon the deck,\r\nstopped before a woman who was lying on a bench, scrutinized her, and,\r\nwhen she moved a little, he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you were asleep, Mademoiselle Alice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Monsieur Sholmes, I am not sleepy. I was thinking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf what? If I may be so bold as to inquire?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was thinking of Madame d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle. She must be very unhappy. Her\r\nlife is ruined.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! no, no,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied quickly. \xe2\x80\x9cHer mistake was not a serious one.\r\nMonsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle will forgive and forget it. Why, even before we\r\nleft, his manner toward her had softened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps ... but he will remember it for a long time ... and she will\r\nsuffer a great deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou love her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much. It was my love for her that gave me strength to smile when\r\nI was trembling from fear, that gave me courage to look in your face\r\nwhen I desired to hide from your sight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are sorry to leave her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, very sorry. I have no relatives, no friends\xe2\x80\x94but her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will have friends,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Englishman, who was affected by her\r\nsorrow. \xe2\x80\x9cI have promised that. I have relatives ... and some influence.\r\nI assure you that you will have no cause to regret coming to England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may be, monsieur, but Madame d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle will not be there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHerlock Sholmes resumed his promenade upon the deck. After a few\r\nminutes, he took a seat near his travelling companion, filled his pipe,\r\nand struck four matches in a vain effort to light it. Then, as he had\r\nno more matches, he arose and said to a gentleman who was sitting near\r\nhim:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I trouble you for a match?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe gentleman opened a box of matches and struck one. The flame lighted\r\nup his face. Sholmes recognized him\xe2\x80\x94it was Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\r\n\r\nIf the Englishman had not given an almost imperceptible movement of\r\nsurprise, Lupin would have supposed that his presence on board had been\r\nknown to Sholmes, so well did he control his feelings and so natural\r\nwas the easy manner in which he extended his hand to his adversary.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s the good health, Monsieur Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravo!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lupin, who could not repress a cry of admiration at\r\nthe Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s sang-froid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBravo? and why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? Because I appear before you like a ghost, only a few hours after\r\nyou saw me drowned in the Seine; and through pride\xe2\x80\x94a quality that is\r\nessentially English\xe2\x80\x94you evince not the slightest surprise. You greet me\r\nas a matter of course. Ah! I repeat: Bravo! Admirable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing remarkable about it. From the manner in which you\r\nfell from the boat, I knew very well that you fell voluntarily, and\r\nthat the bullet had not touched you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you went away without knowing what had become of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat had become of you? Why, I knew that. There were at least five\r\nhundred people on the two banks of the river within a space of\r\nhalf-a-mile. If you escaped death, your capture was certain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet I am here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonsieur Lupin, there are two men in the world at whom I am never\r\nastonished: in the first place, myself\xe2\x80\x94and then, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe treaty of peace was concluded.\r\n\r\nIf Sholmes had not been successful in his contests with Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin;\r\nif Lupin remained the only enemy whose capture he must never hope to\r\naccomplish; if, in the course of their struggles, he had not always\r\ndisplayed a superiority, the Englishman had, none the less, by means of\r\nhis extraordinary intuition and tenacity, succeeded in recovering the\r\nJewish lamp as well as the blue diamond.\r\n\r\nThis time, perhaps, the finish had not been so brilliant, especially\r\nfrom the standpoint of the public spectators, since Sholmes was\r\nobliged to maintain a discreet silence in regard to the circumstances\r\nin which the Jewish lamp had been recovered, and to announce that he\r\ndid not know the name of the thief. But as man to man, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\nagainst Herlock Sholmes, detective against burglar, there was neither\r\nvictor nor vanquished. Each of them had won corresponding victories.\r\n\r\nTherefore they could now converse as courteous adversaries who had lain\r\ndown their arms and held each other in high regard.\r\n\r\nAt Sholmes\xe2\x80\x99 request, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin related the strange story of his\r\nescape.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I may dignify it by calling it an escape,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was so\r\nsimple! My friends were watching for me, as I had asked them to meet me\r\nthere to recover the Jewish lamp. So, after remaining a good half-hour\r\nunder the overturned boat, I took advantage of an occasion when\r\nFolenfant and his men were searching for my dead body along the bank of\r\nthe river, to climb on top of the boat. Then my friends simply picked\r\nme up as they passed by in their motor-boat, and we sailed away under\r\nthe staring eyes of an astonished multitude, including Ganimard and\r\nFolenfant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Sholmes, \xe2\x80\x9cvery neatly played. And now you have\r\nsome business in England?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, some accounts to square up.... But I forgot ... what about\r\nMonsieur d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe knows everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll! my dear Sholmes, what did I tell you? The wrong is now\r\nirreparable. Would it not have been better to have allowed me to carry\r\nout the affair in my own way? In a day or two more, I should have\r\nrecovered the stolen goods from Bresson, restored them to Monsieur\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle, and those two honest citizens would have lived together\r\nin peace and happiness ever after. Instead of that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInstead of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes, sneeringly, \xe2\x80\x9cI have mixed the cards\r\nand sown the seeds of discord in the bosom of a family that was under\r\nyour protection.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMon Dieu! of course, I was protecting them. Must a person steal, cheat\r\nand wrong all the time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you do good, also?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I have the time. Besides, I find it amusing. Now, for instance,\r\nin our last adventure, I found it extremely diverting that I should be\r\nthe good genius seeking to help and save unfortunate mortals, while you\r\nwere the evil genius who dispensed only despair and tears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTears! Tears!\xe2\x80\x9d protested Sholmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly! The d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle household is demolished, and Alice Demun\r\nweeps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe could not remain any longer. Ganimard would have discovered her\r\nsome day, and, through her, reached Madame d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right, monsieur; but whose fault is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwo men passed by. Sholmes said to Lupin, in a friendly tone:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know those gentlemen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought I recognized one of them as the captain of the steamer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the other?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is Austin Gilett, who occupies in London a position similar to that\r\nof Monsieur Dudouis in Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! how fortunate! Will you be so kind as to introduce me? Monsieur\r\nDudouis is one of my best friends, and I shall be delighted to say as\r\nmuch of Monsieur Austin Gilett.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two gentlemen passed again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if I should take you at your word, Monsieur Lupin?\xe2\x80\x9d said Sholmes,\r\nrising, and seizing Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s wrist with a hand of iron.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you grasp me so tightly, monsieur? I am quite willing to follow\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn fact, he allowed himself to be dragged along without the least\r\nresistance. The two gentlemen were disappearing from sight. Sholmes\r\nquickened his pace. His finger-nails even sank into Lupin\xe2\x80\x99s flesh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome! Come!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, with a sort of feverish haste, in harmony\r\nwith his action. \xe2\x80\x9cCome! quicker than that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut he stopped suddenly. Alice Demun was following them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing, Mademoiselle? You need not come. You must not\r\ncome!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was Lupin who replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will notice, monsieur, that she is not coming of her own free\r\nwill. I am holding her wrist in the same tight grasp that you have on\r\nmine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I wish to present her also. Her part in the affair of the\r\nJewish lamp is much more important than mine. Accomplice of Ars\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin, accomplice of Bresson, she has a right to tell her adventure\r\nwith the Baroness d\xe2\x80\x99Imblevalle\xe2\x80\x94which will deeply interest Monsieur\r\nGilett as an officer of the law. And by introducing her also, you will\r\nhave carried your gracious intervention to the very limit, my dear\r\nSholmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Englishman released his hold on his prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s wrist. Lupin\r\nliberated Mademoiselle.\r\n\r\nThey stood looking at each other for a few seconds, silently and\r\nmotionless. Then Sholmes returned to the bench and sat down, followed\r\nby Lupin and the girl. After a long silence, Lupin said: \xe2\x80\x9cYou see,\r\nmonsieur, whatever we may do, we will never be on the same side. You\r\nare on one side of the fence; I am on the other. We can exchange\r\ngreetings, shake hands, converse a moment, but the fence is always\r\nthere. You will remain Herlock Sholmes, detective, and I, Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin,\r\ngentleman-burglar. And Herlock Sholmes will ever obey, more or less\r\nspontaneously, with more or less propriety, his instinct as a\r\ndetective, which is to pursue the burglar and run him down, if\r\npossible. And Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin, in obedience to his burglarious instinct,\r\nwill always be occupied in avoiding the reach of the detective, and\r\nmaking sport of the detective, if he can do it. And, this time, he can\r\ndo it. Ha-ha-ha!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe burst into a loud laugh, cunning, cruel and odious.\r\n\r\nThen, suddenly becoming serious, he addressed Alice Demun:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may be sure, mademoiselle, even when reduced to the last\r\nextremity, I shall not betray you. Ars\xc3\xa8ne Lupin never betrays\r\nanyone\xe2\x80\x94especially those whom he loves and admires. And, may I be\r\npermitted to say, I love and admire the brave, dear woman you have\r\nproved yourself to be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took from his pocket a visiting card, tore it in two, gave one-half\r\nof it to the girl, as he said, in a voice shaken with emotion:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf Monsieur Sholmes\xe2\x80\x99 plans for you do not succeed, mademoiselle, go to\r\nLady Strongborough\xe2\x80\x94you can easily find her address\xe2\x80\x94and give her that\r\nhalf of the card, and, at the same time, say to her: _Faithful friend_.\r\nLady Strongborough will show you the true devotion of a sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl; \xe2\x80\x9cI shall see her to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, Monsieur Sholmes,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lupin, with the satisfied air of\r\na gentleman who has fulfilled his duty, \xe2\x80\x9cI will say good-night. We will\r\nnot land for an hour yet, so I will get that much rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe lay down on the bench, with his hands beneath his head.\r\n\r\nIn a short time the high cliffs of the English coast loomed up in the\r\nincreasing light of a new-born day. The passengers emerged from the\r\ncabins and crowded the deck, eagerly gazing on the approaching shore.\r\nAustin Gilette passed by, accompanied by two men whom Sholmes\r\nrecognized as sleuths from Scotland Yard.\r\n\r\nLupin was asleep, on his bench.\r\n\r\nTHE END.\r\n\r\n\r\n_The further startling, wonderful and thrilling adventures of \xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne\r\nLupin\xe2\x80\x9d will be found in the book entitled \xe2\x80\x9cArs\xc3\xa8ne Lupin\r\nGentleman-Burglar\xe2\x80\x9d._\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK ARS\xc3\x88NE LUPIN VS HERLOCK SHOLMES ***\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
